33111 "<CHAPTER I."
33111 ""
33111 "PREHISTORIC STAGES.>"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "Morgan was the first to make an attempt at introducing a logical order"
33111 "into the history of primeval society. Until considerably more material"
33111 "is obtained, no further changes will be necessary and his arrangement"
33111 "will surely remain in force."
33111 ""
33111 "Of the three main epochs--savagery, barbarism and"
33111 "civilization--naturally only the first two and the transition to the"
33111 "third required his attention. He subdivided each of these into a lower,"
33111 "middle and higher stage, according to the progress in the production of"
33111 "the means of sustenance. His reason for doing so is that the degree of"
33111 "human supremacy over nature is conditioned on the ability to produce the"
33111 "necessities of life. For of all living beings, man alone has acquired an"
33111 "almost unlimited control over food production. All great epochs of human"
33111 "progress, according to Morgan, coincide more or less directly with times"
33111 "of greater abundance in the means that sustain life. The evolution of"
33111 "the family proceeds in the same measure without, however, offering"
33111 "equally convenient marks for sub-division."
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "I. SAVAGERY."
33111 ""
33111 "1. Lower Stage. Infancy of the human race. Human beings still dwelt in"
33111 "their original habitation, in tropical or subtropical forests. They"
33111 "lived at least part of the time in trees, for only in this way they"
33111 "could escape the attacks of large beasts of prey and survive. Fruit,"
33111 "nuts, and roots served as food. The formation of articulated speech is"
33111 "the principal result of this period. Not a single one of all the nations"
33111 "that have become known in historic times dates back to this primeval"
33111 "stage."
33111 ""
33111 "Although the latter may extend over thousands of years, we have no means"
33111 "of proving its existence by direct evidence. But once the descent of man"
33111 "from the Animal Kingdom is acknowledged, the acceptance of this stage of"
33111 "transition becomes inevitable."
33111 ""
33111 "2. Middle Stage: Commencing with the utilization of fish (including"
33111 "crabs, mollusks and other aquatic animals) and the use of fire. Both"
33111 "these things belong together, because fish becomes thoroughly palatable"
33111 "by the help of fire only. With this new kind of food, human beings"
33111 "became completely independent of climate and locality. Following the"
33111 "course of rivers and coastlines, they could spread over the greater part"
33111 "of the earth even in the savage state. The so-called palaeolithic"
33111 "implements of the early stone age, made of rough, unsharpened stones,"
33111 "belong almost entirely to this period. Their wide distribution over all"
33111 "the continents testifies to the extent of these wanderings. The"
33111 "unceasing bent for discovery, together with the possession of fire"
33111 "gained by friction, created new products in the lately occupied regions."
33111 "Such were farinaceous roots and tubers, baked in hot ashes or in baking"
33111 "pits (ground ovens). When the first weapons, club and spear, were"
33111 "invented, venison was occasionally added to the bill of fare. Nations"
33111 "subsisting exclusively by hunting, such as we sometimes find mentioned"
33111 "in books, have never existed; for the proceeds of hunting are too"
33111 "uncertain. In consequence of continued precariousness of the sources of"
33111 "sustenance, cannibalism seems to arise at this stage. It continues in"
33111 "force for a long while. Even in our day, Australians and Polynesians"
33111 "still remain in this middle stage of savagery."
33111 ""
33111 "3. Higher Stage: Coming with the invention of bow and arrow, this stage"
33111 "makes venison a regular part of daily fare and hunting a normal"
33111 "occupation. Bow, arrow and cord represent a rather complicated"
33111 "instrument, the invention of which presupposes a long and accumulated"
33111 "experience and increased mental ability; incidentally they are"
33111 "conditioned on the acquaintance with a number of other inventions."
33111 ""
33111 "In comparing the nations that are familiar with the use of bow and"
33111 "arrow, but not yet with the art of pottery (from which Morgan dates the"
33111 "transition to barbarism), we find among them the beginnings of village"
33111 "settlements, a control of food production, wooden vessels and utensils,"
33111 "weaving of bast fibre by hand (without a loom), baskets made of bast or"
33111 "reeds, and sharpened (neolithic) stone implements. Generally fire and"
33111 "the stone ax have also furnished the dugout and, here and there, timbers"
33111 "and boards for house-building. All these improvements are found, e. g.,"
33111 "among the American Indians of the Northwest, who use bow and arrows, but"
33111 "know nothing as yet about pottery. Bow and arrows were for the stage of"
33111 "savagery what the iron sword was for barbarism and the fire-arm for"
33111 "civilization; the weapon of supremacy."
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "II. BARBARISM."
33111 ""
33111 "1. Lower Stage. Dates from the introduction of the art of pottery. The"
33111 "latter is traceable in many cases, and probably attributable in all"
33111 "cases, to the custom of covering wooden or plaited vessels with clay in"
33111 "order to render them fire-proof. It did not take long to find out that"
33111 "moulded clay served the same purpose without a lining of other material."
33111 ""
33111 "Hitherto we could consider the course of evolution as being equally"
33111 "characteristic, in a general way, for all the nations of a certain"
33111 "period, without reference to locality. But with the beginning of"
33111 "barbarism, we reach a stage where the difference in the natural"
33111 "resources of the two great bodies of land makes itself felt. The salient"
33111 "features of this stage of barbarism is the taming and raising of animals"
33111 "and the cultivation of plants. Now the eastern body of land, the"
33111 "so-called old world, contained nearly all the tamable animals and all"
33111 "the cultivable species of grain but one; while the western continent,"
33111 "America, possessed only one tamable mammal, the llama (even this only in"
33111 "a certain part of the South), and only one, although the best, species"
33111 "of grain: the corn. From now on, these different conditions of nature"
33111 "lead the population of each hemisphere along divergent roads, and the"
33111 "landmarks on the boundaries of the various stages differ in both cases."
33111 ""
33111 "2. Middle Stage. Commencing in the East with the domestication of"
33111 "animals, in the West with the cultivation and irrigation of foodplants;"
33111 "also with the use of adobes (bricks baked in the sun) and stones for"
33111 "buildings."
33111 ""
33111 "We begin in the West, because there this stage was never outgrown up to"
33111 "the time of the conquest by Europeans."
33111 ""
33111 "At the time of their discovery, the Indians in the lower stage of"
33111 "barbarism (all those living east of the Mississippi) carried on"
33111 "cultivation on a small scale in gardens. Corn, and perhaps also"
33111 "pumpkins, melons and other garden truck were raised. A very essential"
33111 "part of their sustenance was produced in this manner. They lived in"
33111 "wooden houses, in fortified villages. The tribes of the Northwest,"
33111 "especially those of the region along the Columbia river, were still in"
33111 "the higher stage of savagery, ignorant of pottery and of any cultivation"
33111 "of plants whatever. But the so-called Pueblo Indians in New Mexico, the"
33111 "Mexicans, Central-Americans and Peruvians, were in the middle-stage of"
33111 "barbarism. They lived in fortlike houses of adobe or stone, cultivated"
33111 "corn and other plants suitable to various conditions of localities and"
33111 "climate in artificially irrigated gardens that represented the main"
33111 "source of nourishment, and even kept a few tamed animals--the Mexicans"
33111 "the turkey and other birds, the Peruvians the llama. Furthermore they"
33111 "were familiar with the use of metals--iron excepted, and for this reason"
33111 "they could not get along yet without stone weapons and stone implements."
33111 "The conquest by the Spaniards cut short all further independent"
33111 "development."
33111 ""
33111 "In the East, the middle stage of barbarism began with the taming of milk"
33111 "and meat producing animals, while the cultivation of plants seems to"
33111 "have remained unknown far into this period. It appears that the taming"
33111 "and raising of animals and the formation of large herds gave rise to the"
33111 "separation of Aryans and Semites from the rest of the barbarians. Names"
33111 "of animals are still common to the languages of European and Asian"
33111 "Aryans, while this is almost never the case with the names of cultivated"
33111 "plants."
33111 ""
33111 "In suitable localities, the formation of herds led to a nomadic life, as"
33111 "with the Semites in the grassy plains of the Euphrates and Tigris, the"
33111 "Aryans in the plains of India, of the Oxus, Jaxartes, Don and Dnieper."
33111 "Along the borders of such pasture lands, the taming of animals must have"
33111 "been accomplished first. But later generations conceived the mistaken"
33111 "idea that the nomadic tribes had their origin in regions supposed to be"
33111 "the cradle of humanity, while in reality their savage ancestors and even"
33111 "people in the lower stage of barbarism would have found these regions"
33111 "almost unfit for habitation. On the other hand, once these barbarians of"
33111 "the middle stage were accustomed to nomadic life, nothing could have"
33111 "induced them to return voluntarily from the grassy river plains to the"
33111 "forests that had been the home of their ancestors. Even when Semites and"
33111 "Aryans were forced further to the North and West, it was impossible for"
33111 "them to occupy the forest regions of Western Asia and Europe, until they"
33111 "were enabled by agriculture to feed their animals on this less favorable"
33111 "soil and especially to maintain them during the winter. It is more than"
33111 "probable that the cultivation of grain was due primarily to the demand"
33111 "for stock feed, and became an important factor of human sustenance at a"
33111 "later period."
33111 ""
33111 "The superior development of Aryans and Semites is, perhaps, attributable"
33111 "to the copious meat and milk diet of both races, more especially to the"
33111 "favorable influence of such food on the growth of children. As a matter"
33111 "of fact, the Pueblo Indians of New Mexico who live on an almost purely"
33111 "vegetarian diet, have a smaller brain than the Indians in the lower"
33111 "stage of barbarism who eat more meat and fish. At any rate, cannibalism"
33111 "gradually disappears at this stage and is maintained only as a religious"
33111 "observance or, what is here nearly identical, as a magic remedy.[6]"
33111 ""
33111 "3. Higher Stage. Beginning with the melting of iron ore and merging into"
33111 "civilization by the invention of letter script and its utilization for"
33111 "writing records. This stage which is passed independently only on the"
33111 "Eastern Hemisphere, is richer in improvements of production than all"
33111 "preceding stages together. It is the stage of the Greek heroes, the"
33111 "Italian tribes shortly before the foundation of Rome, the Germans of"
33111 "Tacitus, the Norsemen of the Viking age."
33111 ""
33111 "We are here confronted for the first time with the iron ploughshare"
33111 "drawn by animals, rendering possible agriculture on a large scale, in"
33111 "fields, and hence a practically unlimited increase in the production of"
33111 "food for the time being. The next consequence is the clearing of forests"
33111 "and their transformation into arable land and meadows--which process,"
33111 "however, could not be continued on a larger scale without the help of"
33111 "the iron ax and the iron spade. Naturally, these improvements brought a"
33111 "more rapid increase of population and a concentration of numbers into a"
33111 "small area. Before the time of field cultivation a combination of half a"
33111 "million of people under one central management could have been possible"
33111 "only under exceptionally favorable conditions; most likely this was"
33111 "never the case."
33111 ""
33111 "The greatest attainments of the higher stage of barbarism are presented"
33111 "in Homer's poems, especially in the Iliad. Improved iron tools; the"
33111 "bellows; the hand-mill; the potter's wheel; the preparation of oil and"
33111 "wine; a well developed fashioning of metals verging on artisanship; the"
33111 "wagon and chariot; ship-building with beams and boards; the beginning of"
33111 "artistic architecture; towns surrounded by walls with turrets and"
33111 "battlements; the Homeric epos and the entire mythology--these are the"
33111 "principal bequests transmitted by the Greeks from barbarism to"
33111 "civilization. In comparing these attainments with the description given"
33111 "by Cesar or even Tacitus of Germans, who were in the beginning of the"
33111 "same stage of evolution which the Greeks were preparing to leave for a"
33111 "higher one, we perceive the wealth of productive development comprised"
33111 "in the higher stage of barbarism."
33111 ""
33111 "The sketch which I have here produced after Morgan of the evolution of"
33111 "the human race through savagery and barbarism to the beginning of"
33111 "civilization is even now rich in new outlines. More still, these"
33111 "outlines are incontrovertible, because traced directly from production."
33111 "Nevertheless, this sketch will appear faint and meagre in comparison to"
33111 "the panorama unrolled to our view at the end of our pilgrimage. Not"
33111 "until then will it be possible to show in their true light both the"
33111 "transition from barbarianism to civilization and the striking contrast"
33111 "between them. For the present we can summarize Morgan's arrangement in"
33111 "the following manner: Savagery--time of predominating appropriation of"
33111 "finished natural products; human ingenuity invents mainly tools useful"
33111 "in assisting this appropriation. Barbarism--time of acquiring the"
33111 "knowledge of cattle raising, of agriculture and of new methods for"
33111 "increasing the productivity of nature by human agency. Civilization:"
33111 "time of learning a wider utilization, of natural products, of"
33111 "manufacturing and of art."
33111 ""
33111 "FOOTNOTE:"
33111 ""
33111 "[6] Translator's note."
33111 ""
33111 "Advocates of vegetarianism may, of course, challenge this statement and"
33111 "show that all the testimony of anthropology is not in favor of the"
33111 "meat-eaters. It must also be admitted that diet is not the only"
33111 "essential factor in environment which influences the development of"
33111 "races. And there is no conclusive evidence to prove the absolute"
33111 "superiority of one diet over another. Neither have we any proofs that"
33111 "cannibalism ever was in general practice. It rather seems to have been"
33111 "confined to limited groups of people in especially ill-favored"
33111 "localities or to times of great scarcity of food. Hence we can neither"
33111 "refer to cannibalism as a typical stage in human history, nor are we"
33111 "obliged to accept the vegetarian hypothesis of a transition from a meat"
33111 "diet to a plant diet as a condition sine qua non of higher human"
33111 "development."
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "<CHAPTER II."
33111 ""
33111 "THE FAMILY.>"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "Morgan, who spent the greater part of his life among the Iroquois in the"
33111 "State of New York and who had been adopted into one of their tribes, the"
33111 "Senecas, found among them a system of relationship that was in"
33111 "contradiction with their actual family relations. Among them existed"
33111 "what Morgan terms the syndyasmian or pairing family, a monogamous state"
33111 "easily dissolved by either side. The offspring of such a couple was"
33111 "identified and acknowledged by all the world. There could be no doubt to"
33111 "whom to apply the terms father, mother, son, daughter, brother, sister."
33111 "But the actual use of these words was not in keeping with their"
33111 "fundamental meaning. For the Iroquois addresses as sons and daughters"
33111 "not only his own children, but also those of his brothers; and he is"
33111 "called father by all of them. But the children of his sisters he calls"
33111 "nephews and nieces, and they call him uncle. Vice versa, an Iroquois"
33111 "woman calls her own children as well as those of her sisters sons and"
33111 "daughters and is addressed as mother by them. But the children of her"
33111 "brothers are called nephews and nieces, and they call her aunt. In the"
33111 "same way, the children of brothers call one another brothers and"
33111 "sisters, and so do the children of sisters. But the children of a sister"
33111 "call those of her brother cousins, and vice versa. And these are not"
33111 "simply meaningless terms, but expressions of actually existing"
33111 "conceptions of proximity and remoteness, equality or inequality of"
33111 "consanguinity."
33111 ""
33111 "These conceptions serve as the fundament of a perfectly elaborated"
33111 "system of relationship, capable of expressing several hundred different"
33111 "relations of a single individual. More still, this system is not only"
33111 "fully accepted by all American Indians--no exception has been found so"
33111 "far--but it is also in use with hardly any modifications among the"
33111 "original inhabitants of India, among the Dravidian tribes of the Dekan"
33111 "and the Gaura tribes of Hindostan."
33111 ""
33111 "The terms of relationship used by the Tamils of Southern India and by"
33111 "the Seneca-Iroquois of New York State are to this day identical for more"
33111 "than two hundred different family relations. And among these East Indian"
33111 "tribes also, as among all American Indians, the relations arising out of"
33111 "the prevailing form of the family are not in keeping with the system of"
33111 "kinship."
33111 ""
33111 "How can this be explained? In view of the important role played by"
33111 "kinship in the social order of all the savage and barbarian races, the"
33111 "significance of such a widespread system cannot be obliterated by"
33111 "phrases."
33111 ""
33111 "A system that is generally accepted in America, that also exists in Asia"
33111 "among people of entirely different races, that is frequently found in a"
33111 "more or less modified form all over Africa and Australia, such a system"
33111 "requires a historical explanation and cannot be talked down, as was"
33111 "attempted, e. g., by McLennan. The terms father, child, brother, sister"
33111 "are more than mere honorary titles; they carry in their wake certain"
33111 "well-defined and very serious obligations, the aggregate of which"
33111 "comprises a very essential part of the social constitution of those"
33111 "nations. And the explanation was found. In the Sandwich Islands (Hawaii)"
33111 "there existed up to the first half of the nineteenth century a family"
33111 "form producing just such fathers and mothers, brothers and sisters,"
33111 "uncles and aunts, nephews and nieces, as the old Indo-American system of"
33111 "kinship. But how remarkable! The Hawaiian system of kinship again did"
33111 "not agree with the family form actually prevailing there. For there all"
33111 "the children of brothers and sisters, without any exception, are"
33111 "considered brothers and sisters, and regarded as the common children not"
33111 "only of their mother or her sisters, or their father and his brothers,"
33111 "but of all the brothers and sisters of their parents without"
33111 "distinction. While thus the American system of kinship presupposes an"
33111 "obsolete primitive form of the family, which is still actually existing"
33111 "in Hawaii, the Hawaiian system on the other hand points to a still more"
33111 "primitive form of the family, the actual existence of which cannot be"
33111 "proved any more, but which must have existed, because otherwise such a"
33111 "system of kinship could not have arisen. According to Morgan, the family"
33111 "is the active element; it is never stationary, but in progression from a"
33111 "lower to a higher form in the same measure in which society develops"
33111 "from a lower to a higher stage. But the systems of kinship are passive."
33111 "Only in long intervals they register the progress made by the family in"
33111 "course of time, and only then are they radically changed, when the"
33111 "family has done so. \"And,\" adds Marx, \"it is the same with political,"
33111 "juridical, religious and philosophical systems in general.\" While the"
33111 "family keeps on growing, the system of kinship becomes ossified. The"
33111 "latter continues in this state and the family grows beyond it. With the"
33111 "same certainty which enabled Cuvier to conclude from some bones of"
33111 "Marsupialia found near Paris that extinct marsupialia had lived there,"
33111 "with this same certainty may we conclude from a system of kinship"
33111 "transmitted by history that the extinct form of the family corresponding"
33111 "to this system was once in existence."
33111 ""
33111 "The systems of kinship and forms of the family just mentioned differ"
33111 "from the present systems in that every child has several fathers and"
33111 "mothers. Under the American system to which the Hawaiian system"
33111 "corresponds, brother and sister cannot be father and mother of the same"
33111 "child; but the Hawaiian system presupposes a family, in which, on the"
33111 "contrary, this was the rule. We are here confronted by a series of"
33111 "family forms that are in direct contradiction with those that were"
33111 "currently regarded as alone prevailing. The conventional conception"
33111 "knows only monogamy, furthermore polygamy of one man, eventually also"
33111 "polyandry of one woman. But it passes in silence, as is meet for a"
33111 "moralizing philistine, that the practice silently but without"
33111 "compunction supersedes these barriers sanctioned officially by society."
33111 "The study of primeval history, however, shows us conditions, where men"
33111 "practiced polygamy and women at the same time polyandry, so that their"
33111 "children were considered common to all; conditions that up to their"
33111 "final transition into monogamy underwent a whole series of"
33111 "modifications. These modifications slowly and gradually contract the"
33111 "circle comprised by the common tie of marriage until only the single"
33111 "couple remains which prevails to-day."
33111 ""
33111 "In thus constructing backward the history of the family, Morgan, in"
33111 "harmony with the majority of his colleagues, arrives at a primeval"
33111 "condition, where unrestricted sexual intercourse existed within a tribe,"
33111 "so that every woman belonged to every man, and vice versa."
33111 ""
33111 "Much has been said about this primeval state of affairs since the"
33111 "eighteenth century, but only in general commonplaces. It is one of"
33111 "Bachofen's great merits to have taken the subject seriously and to have"
33111 "searched for traces of this state in historical and religious"
33111 "traditions. To-day we know that these traces, found by him, do not lead"
33111 "back to a stage of unlimited sexual intercourse, but to a much later"
33111 "form, the group marriage. The primeval stage, if it really ever existed,"
33111 "belongs to so remote a period, that we can hardly expect to find direct"
33111 "proofs of its former existence among these social fossils, backward"
33111 "savages. Bachofen's merit consists in having brought this question to"
33111 "the fore.[7]"
33111 ""
33111 "It has lately become a fashion to deny the existence of this early stage"
33111 "of human sex life, in order to spare us this \"shame.\" Apart from the"
33111 "absence of all direct proof, the example of the rest of animal life is"
33111 "invoked. From the latter, Letourneau (Evolution du mariage et de la"
33111 "famille, 1888) quoted numerous facts, alleged to prove that among"
33111 "animals also an absolutely unlimited sexual intercourse belongs to a"
33111 "lower stage. But I can only conclude from all these facts that they"
33111 "prove absolutely nothing for man and the primeval conditions of his"
33111 "life. The mating of vertebrates for a lengthy term is sufficiently"
33111 "explained by physiological causes, e. g., among birds by the"
33111 "helplessness of the female during brooding time. Examples of faithful"
33111 "monogamy among birds do not furnish any proofs for men, for we are not"
33111 "descended from birds."
33111 ""
33111 "And if strict monogamy is the height of virtue, then the palm belongs to"
33111 "the tapeworm that carries a complete male and female sexual apparatus in"
33111 "each of its 50 to 200 sections and passes its whole lifetime in"
33111 "fertilizing itself in every one of its sections. But if we confine"
33111 "ourselves to mammals, we find all forms of sexual intercourse, license,"
33111 "suggestions of group marriage, polygamy and monogamy. Only polyandry is"
33111 "missing;[8] that could be accomplished by men only. Even our next"
33111 "relations, the quadrumana, exhibit all possible differences in the"
33111 "grouping of males and females. And if we draw the line still closer and"
33111 "consider only the four anthropoid apes, Letourneau can only tell us,"
33111 "that they are now monogamous, now polygamous; while Saussure contends"
33111 "according to Giraud-Teulon that they are monogamous. The recent"
33111 "contentions of Westermarck[9] in regard to monogamy among anthropoid"
33111 "apes are far from proving anything. In short, the information is such"
33111 "that honest Letourneau admits: \"There exists no strict relation at all"
33111 "between the degree of intellectual development and the form of sexual"
33111 "intercourse among mammals.\" And Espinas says frankly:[10] \"The herd is"
33111 "the highest social group found among animals. It seems to be composed of"
33111 "families, but from the outset the family and the herd are antagonistic;"
33111 "they develop in directly opposite ratio.\""
33111 ""
33111 "It is evident from the above that we know next to nothing of the family"
33111 "and other social groups of anthropoid apes; the reports are directly"
33111 "contradictory. How full of contradiction, how much in need of critical"
33111 "scrutiny and research are the reports even on savage human tribes! But"
33111 "monkey tribes are far more difficult to observe than human tribes. For"
33111 "the present, therefore, we must decline all final conclusions from such"
33111 "absolutely unreliable reports."
33111 ""
33111 "The quotation from Espinas, however, offers a better clue. Among higher"
33111 "animals, the herd and family are not supplements of one another, but"
33111 "antitheses. Espinas demonstrates very nicely, how the jealousy of the"
33111 "males loosens or temporarily dissolves every herd during mating time."
33111 "\"Where the family is closely organized, herds are formed only in"
33111 "exceptional cases. But wherever free sexual intercourse or polygamy are"
33111 "existing, the herd appears almost spontaneously.... In order that a herd"
33111 "may form, family ties must be loosened and the individual be free. For"
33111 "this reason we so rarely find organized herds among birds.... Among"
33111 "mammals, however, we find groups organized after a fashion, just because"
33111 "here the individual is not merged in the family.... The rising sense of"
33111 "cohesion in a herd cannot, therefore, have a greater enemy than the"
33111 "consciousness of family ties. Let us not shrink from pronouncing it: the"
33111 "development of a higher form of society than the family can be due only"
33111 "to the fact that it admitted families which had undergone a thorough"
33111 "change. This does not exclude the possibility that these same families"
33111 "were thus enabled to reorganize later on under infinitely more favorable"
33111 "circumstances.\"[11]"
33111 ""
33111 "It becomes apparent from this, that animal societies may indeed have a"
33111 "certain value in drawing conclusions in regard to human life--but only"
33111 "negatively. The higher vertebrate knows, so far as we may ascertain,"
33111 "only two forms of the family: polygamy or pairs. In both of them there"
33111 "is only one grown male, only one husband. The jealousy of the male, at"
33111 "the same time tie and limit of the family, creates an opposition between"
33111 "the animal family and the herd. The latter, a higher social form, is"
33111 "here rendered impossible, there loosened or dissolved during mating"
33111 "time, and at best hindered in its development by the jealousy of the"
33111 "male. This in itself is sufficient proof that the animal family and"
33111 "primeval human society are irreconcilable; that ancient man, struggling"
33111 "upward from the animal stage, either had no family at all or at the most"
33111 "one that does not exist among animals. A being so defenceless as"
33111 "evolving man might well survive in small numbers though living in an"
33111 "isolated state, the highest social form of which is that of pairs such"
33111 "as Westermarck, relying on hunter's reports, attributes to the gorilla"
33111 "and the chimpanzee. Another element is necessary for the elevation out"
33111 "of the animal stage, for the realization of the highest progress found"
33111 "in nature: the replacing of the defencelessness of the single individual"
33111 "by the united strength and co-operation of the whole herd. The"
33111 "transition from beast to man out of conditions of the sort under which"
33111 "the anthropoid apes are living to-day would be absolutely unexplainable."
33111 "These apes rather give the impression of stray sidelines gradually"
33111 "approaching extinction, and at all events in process of decline. This"
33111 "alone is sufficient to reject all parallels between their family forms"
33111 "and those of primeval man. But mutual tolerance of the grown males,"
33111 "freedom from jealousy, was the first condition for the formation of such"
33111 "large and permanent groups, within which alone the transformation from"
33111 "beast to man could be accomplished. And indeed, what do we find to be"
33111 "the most ancient and original form of the family, undeniably traceable"
33111 "by history and even found to-day here and there? The group marriage,"
33111 "that form in which whole groups of men and whole groups of women"
33111 "mutually belong to one another, leaving only small scope for jealousy."
33111 "And furthermore we find at a later stage the exceptional form of"
33111 "polyandry which still more supersedes all sentiments of jealousy and"
33111 "hence is unknown to animals."
33111 ""
33111 "But all the forms of the group marriage known to us are accompanied by"
33111 "such peculiarly complicated circumstances that they of necessity point"
33111 "to a preceding simpler form of sexual intercourse and, hence, in the"
33111 "last instance to a period of unrestricted sexual intercourse"
33111 "corresponding to a transition from the animal to man. Therefore the"
33111 "references to animal marriages lead us back to precisely that point,"
33111 "from which they were intended to remove us forever."
33111 ""
33111 "What does the term \"unrestricted sexual intercourse\" mean? Simply, that"
33111 "the restrictions in force now were not observed formerly. We have"
33111 "already seen the barrier of jealousy falling. If anything is certain, it"
33111 "is that jealousy is developed at a comparatively late stage. The same is"
33111 "true of incest. Not only brother and sister were originally man and"
33111 "wife, but also the sexual intercourse between parents and children is"
33111 "permitted to this day among many nations. Bancroft testifies to the"
33111 "truth of this among the Kaviats of the Behring Strait, the Kadiaks of"
33111 "Alaska, the Tinnehs in the interior of British North America; Letourneau"
33111 "compiled reports of the same fact in regard to the Chippeway Indians,"
33111 "the Coocoos in Chile, the Caribeans, the Carens in Indo-China, not to"
33111 "mention the tales of ancient Greeks and Romans about the Parthians,"
33111 "Persians, Scythians, Huns and so forth. Before incest was invented (and"
33111 "it is an invention, a really valuable one indeed), sexual intercourse"
33111 "between parents and children could not be any more repulsive than"
33111 "between other persons belonging to different generations, which takes"
33111 "place even in our day among the most narrow-minded nations without"
33111 "causing any horror. Even old \"maids\" of more than sixty years sometimes,"
33111 "if they are rich enough, marry young men of about thirty. Eliminating"
33111 "from the primeval forms of the family known to us those conceptions of"
33111 "incest--conceptions totally different from ours and often enough in"
33111 "direct contradiction with them--we arrive at a form of sexual"
33111 "intercourse that can only be designated as unrestricted. Unrestricted in"
33111 "the sense that the barriers drawn later on by custom did not yet exist."
33111 "This in no way necessarily implies for practical purposes an injudicious"
33111 "pell-mell intercourse. The separate existence of pairs for a limited"
33111 "time is not out of the question, and even comprises the majority of"
33111 "cases in the group marriage of our days. And if the latest repudiator of"
33111 "such a primeval state, Westermarck, designates as marriage every case,"
33111 "where both sexes remain mated until the birth of the offspring, then"
33111 "this is equivalent to saying that this kind of marriage may well exist"
33111 "during a stage of unrestricted intercourse without contradicting"
33111 "license, i. e., absence of barriers drawn by custom for sexual"
33111 "intercourse. Westermarck bases himself on the opinion that \"license"
33111 "includes the suppression of individual affections\" so that \"prostitution"
33111 "is its most genuine form.\" To me it rather seems that any understanding"
33111 "of primeval conditions is impossible as long as we look at them through"
33111 "brothel spectacles. We shall return to this point in the group marriage."
33111 ""
33111 "According to Morgan, the following forms developed from this primeval"
33111 "state at an apparently early stage:"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "1. THE CONSANGUINE FAMILY."
33111 ""
33111 "The Consanguine Family is the first step toward the family. Here the"
33111 "marriage groups are arranged by generations: all the grand-fathers and"
33111 "grand-mothers within a certain family are mutually husbands and wives;"
33111 "and equally their children, the fathers and mothers, whose children form"
33111 "a third cycle of mutual mates. The children of these again, the"
33111 "great-grandchildren of the first cycle, will form a fourth. In this form"
33111 "of the family, then, only ancestors and descendants are excluded from"
33111 "what we would call the rights and duties of marriage. Brothers and"
33111 "sisters, male and female cousins of the first, second and more remote"
33111 "grades, are all mutually brothers and sisters and for this reason mutual"
33111 "husbands and wives. The relation of brother and sister quite naturally"
33111 "includes at this stage the practice of sexual intercourse.[12]"
33111 ""
33111 "The typical form of such a family would consist of the offspring of one"
33111 "pair, representing again the descendants of each grade as mutual"
33111 "brothers and sisters and, therefore, mutual husbands and wives. The"
33111 "consanguine family is extinct. Even the crudest nations of history do"
33111 "not furnish any proofs of it. But the Hawaiian system of kinship, in"
33111 "force to this day in all Polynesia, compels us to acknowledge its"
33111 "former existence, for it exhibits grades of kinship that could only"
33111 "originate in this form of the family. And the whole subsequent"
33111 "development of the family compels us to admit this form as a necessary"
33111 "step."
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "2. THE PUNALUAN FAMILY."
33111 ""
33111 "While the first step of organization consisted in excluding parents and"
33111 "children from mutual sexual intercourse, the second was the erection of"
33111 "a barrier between brother and sister. This progress was much more"
33111 "important on account of the greater equality in the ages of the parties"
33111 "concerned, but also far more difficult. It was accomplished gradually,"
33111 "probably beginning with the exclusion of the natural sister (i. e., on"
33111 "the mother's side) from sexual intercourse, first in single cases, then"
33111 "becoming more and more the rule (in Hawaii exceptions were still noted"
33111 "during the nineteenth century), and finally ending with the prohibition"
33111 "of marriage even among collateral brothers and sisters, i. e., what we"
33111 "now term brother's and sister's children, grandchildren, and"
33111 "great-grandchildren. This progress offers, according to Morgan, an"
33111 "excellent illustration how the principle of natural selection works."
33111 "Without question, the tribes limiting inbreeding by this progress"
33111 "developed faster and more completely than those retaining the marriage"
33111 "between brothers and sisters as a rule and law. And how powerfully the"
33111 "influence of this progress was felt, is shown by the institution of the"
33111 "gens, directly attributable to it and passing far beyond the goal. The"
33111 "gens is the foundation of the social order of most, if not all,"
33111 "barbarian nations, and in Greece and Rome we step immediately from it to"
33111 "civilization."
33111 ""
33111 "Every primeval family necessarily had to divide after a few generations."
33111 "The originally communistic and collective household existing far into"
33111 "the middle stage of barbarism, involved a certain maximum size of the"
33111 "family, variable according to conditions, but still limited in a degree."
33111 "As soon as the conception of the impropriety of sexual intercourse"
33111 "between children of the same mother arose, it naturally became effective"
33111 "on such occasions as the division of old and the foundation of new"
33111 "household communities (which, however, did not necessarily coincide with"
33111 "the family group). One or more series of sisters became the center of"
33111 "one group, their natural brothers that of another. In this or a similar"
33111 "manner that form which Morgan styles the Punaluan family developed from"
33111 "the consanguine family. According to Hawaiian custom, a number of"
33111 "sisters, natural or more remote (i. e., cousins of the first, second and"
33111 "more remote degrees) were the mutual wives of their mutual husbands,"
33111 "their natural brothers excepted. These men now no longer addressed one"
33111 "another as \"brother\"--which they no longer had to be--but as \"Punalua,\""
33111 "i. e., intimate companion, associate as it were. Likewise a series of"
33111 "natural or more remote brothers lived in mutual marriage with a number"
33111 "of women, not their natural sisters, and these women referred to each"
33111 "other as \"Punalua.\" This is the classical form of a family, which later"
33111 "admitted of certain variations. Its fundamental characteristic was"
33111 "mutual community of husbands and wives within a given family with the"
33111 "exclusion of the natural brothers (or sisters) first, and of the more"
33111 "remote grades later."
33111 ""
33111 "This form of the family, now, furnishes with complete accuracy the"
33111 "degrees of kinship expressed by the American system. The children of the"
33111 "sisters of my mother still are her children; likewise the children of"
33111 "the brothers of my father still his children; and all of them are my"
33111 "brothers and sisters. But the children of the brothers of my mother are"
33111 "now her nephews and nieces, the children of the sisters of my father"
33111 "his nephew and nieces, and they are all my cousins. For while the"
33111 "husbands of the sisters of my mother are still her husbands, and"
33111 "likewise the wives of the brothers of my father still his"
33111 "wives--legally, if not always in fact--the social proscription of sexual"
33111 "intercourse between brothers and sisters has now divided those relatives"
33111 "who were formerly regarded without distinction as brothers and sisters,"
33111 "into two classes. In one category are those who remain (more remote)"
33111 "brothers and sisters as before; in the other the children of the brother"
33111 "on one hand or the sister on the opposite, who can be brothers and"
33111 "sisters no longer. The latter have mutual parents no more, neither"
33111 "father nor mother nor both together. And for this reason the class of"
33111 "nephews and nieces, male and female cousins, here becomes necessary for"
33111 "the first time. Under the former family order this would have been"
33111 "absurd. The American system of kinship, which appears absolutely"
33111 "paradoxical in any family form founded on monogamy, is rationally"
33111 "explained and naturally confirmed in its most minute details by the"
33111 "Punaluan family. Wherever this system of kinship was in force, there the"
33111 "Punaluan family or at least a form akin to it must also have existed."
33111 ""
33111 "This family form, the existence of which in Hawaii was actually"
33111 "demonstrated, would have been transmitted probably by all Polynesia, if"
33111 "the pious missionaries, similar to the Spanish monks in America, could"
33111 "have looked upon such anti-Christian relations as being something more"
33111 "than simply a \"horror.\"[13] Cesar's report to the effect that the"
33111 "Britons, who then were in the middle stage of barbarism, \"have ten or"
33111 "twelve women in common, mostly brothers with brothers and parents with"
33111 "children,\" is best explained by group marriage. Barbarian mothers have"
33111 "not ten or twelve sons old enough to keep women in common, but the"
33111 "American system of kinship corresponding to the Punaluan family"
33111 "furnishes many brothers, because all near and remote cousins of a"
33111 "certain man are his brothers. The term \"parents with children\" may arise"
33111 "from a wrong conception of Cesar, but this system does not absolutely"
33111 "exclude the existence of father and son, mother or daughter in the same"
33111 "group. It does exclude, however, father and daughter or mother and son."
33111 "This or a similar form of group marriage also furnishes the easiest"
33111 "explanation of the reports of Herodotus and other ancient writers"
33111 "concerning community of women among savage and barbarian nations. This"
33111 "is true, furthermore, of Watson's and Kaye's[14] tale about the Tikurs"
33111 "of Audh (north of the Ganges): \"They live together (i. e., sexually)"
33111 "almost indiscriminately in large communities, and though two persons may"
33111 "be considered as being married, still the tie is only nominal.\""
33111 ""
33111 "The institution of the gens seems to have its origin in the majority of"
33111 "cases in the Punaluan family. True, the Australian class system also"
33111 "offers a starting point for it; the Australians have gentes, but not yet"
33111 "a Punaluan family, only a cruder form of group marriage.[15]"
33111 ""
33111 "In all forms of the group family it is uncertain who is the father of a"
33111 "child, but certain, who is its mother. Although she calls all the"
33111 "children of the aggregate family her children and has the duties of a"
33111 "mother toward them, still she knows her natural children from others."
33111 "It is also obvious that, as far as group marriage exists, descent can"
33111 "only be traced on the mother's side and, hence, only female lineage be"
33111 "acknowledged. This is actually the case among all savage tribes and"
33111 "those in the lower stage of barbarism. To have discovered this first is"
33111 "the second great merit of Bachofen. He designates this exclusive"
33111 "recognition of descent from the female line and the hereditary relations"
33111 "resulting therefrom in course of time as \"maternal law.\" I retain this"
33111 "term for the sake of brevity, although it is distorted; for at this"
33111 "social stage there is no sign yet of any law in the juridic sense."
33111 ""
33111 "If we now take one of the two standard groups of a Punaluan family,"
33111 "namely that of a series of natural and remote sisters (i. e., first,"
33111 "second and more remote descendants of natural sisters), their children"
33111 "and their natural or remote brothers on the mother's side (who according"
33111 "to our supposition are not their husbands), we have exactly that circle"
33111 "of persons who later appear as members of a gens, in the original form"
33111 "of this institution. They all have a common ancestress, by virtue of the"
33111 "descent that makes the different female generations sisters. But the"
33111 "husbands of these sisters cannot be chosen among their brothers any"
33111 "more, can no longer come from the same ancestress, and do not,"
33111 "therefore, belong to the consanguineous group of relatives, the gens of"
33111 "a later time. The children of these same sisters, however, do belong to"
33111 "this group, because descent from the female line alone is conclusive,"
33111 "alone is positive. As soon as the proscription of sexual intercourse"
33111 "between all relatives on the mother's side, even the most remote of"
33111 "them, is an accomplished fact, the above named group has become a gens,"
33111 "i. e., constitutes a definite circle of consanguineous relatives of"
33111 "female lineage who are not permitted to marry one another. Henceforth"
33111 "this circle is more and more fortified by other mutual institutions of"
33111 "a social or religious character and thus distinguished from other gentes"
33111 "of the same tribe. Of this more anon."
33111 ""
33111 "Finding, as we do, that the gens not only necessarily, but also as a"
33111 "matter of course, develops from the Punaluan family, it becomes obvious"
33111 "to us to assume as almost practically demonstrated the prior existence"
33111 "of this family form among all those nations where such gentes are"
33111 "traceable, i. e., nearly all barbarian and civilized nations."
33111 ""
33111 "When Morgan wrote his book, our knowledge of group marriage was very"
33111 "limited. We knew very little about the group marriages of the"
33111 "Australians organized in classes, and furthermore Morgan had published"
33111 "as early as 1871 the information he had received about the Punaluan"
33111 "family of Hawaii. This family on one hand furnished a complete"
33111 "explanation of the system of kinship in force among the American"
33111 "Indians, which had been the point of departure for all the studies of"
33111 "Morgan. On the other hand it formed a ready means for the deduction of"
33111 "the maternal law gens. And finally it represented a far higher stage of"
33111 "development than the Australian classes."
33111 ""
33111 "It is, therefore, easy to understand how Morgan could regard this form"
33111 "as the stage necessarily preceding the pairing family and attribute"
33111 "general extension in former times to it. Since then we have learned of"
33111 "several other forms of the group marriage, and we know that Morgan went"
33111 "too far in this respect. But it was nevertheless his good fortune to"
33111 "encounter in his Punaluan family the highest, the classical, form of"
33111 "group marriage, that form which gave the simplest clue for the"
33111 "transition to a higher stage."
33111 ""
33111 "The most essential contribution to our knowledge of the group marriage"
33111 "we owe to the English missionary, Lorimer Fison, who studied this form"
33111 "of the family for years on its classical ground, Australia. He found"
33111 "the lowest stage of development among the Papuans near Mount Gambier in"
33111 "South Australia. Here the whole tribe is divided into two great classes,"
33111 "Kroki and Kumite.[16] Sexual intercourse within each of these classes is"
33111 "strictly prohibited. But every man of one class is by birth the husband"
33111 "of every woman of the other class, and vice versa. Not the individuals"
33111 "are married to one another, but the whole groups, class to class. And"
33111 "mark well, no caution is made anywhere on account of difference of age"
33111 "or special consanguinity, unless it is resulting from the division into"
33111 "two exogamous classes. A Kroki has for his wife every Kumite woman. And"
33111 "as his own daughter, being the daughter of a Kumite woman, is also"
33111 "Kumite according to maternal law, she is therefore the born wife of"
33111 "every Kroki, including her father. At least, the class organization, as"
33111 "we know it, does not exclude this possibility. Hence this organization"
33111 "either arose at a time when, in spite of all dim endeavor to limit"
33111 "inbreeding, sexual intercourse between parents and children was not yet"
33111 "regarded with any particular horror; in this case the class system would"
33111 "be directly evolved from a condition of unrestricted sexual relations."
33111 "Or the intercourse between parents and children was already proscribed"
33111 "by custom, when the classes were formed; and in this case the present"
33111 "condition points back to the consanguine family and is the first step"
33111 "out of it. The latter case is the more probable. So far as I know, no"
33111 "mention is made of any sexual intercourse between parents and children"
33111 "in Australia. Even the later form of exogamy, the maternal law gens, as"
33111 "a rule silently presupposes that the prohibition of this intercourse"
33111 "was an accomplished fact at the time of its institution."
33111 ""
33111 "The system of two classes is not only found near Mount Gambier in South"
33111 "Australia, but also farther east along Darling River, and in the"
33111 "northeast of Queensland. It is, consequently, widespread. It excludes"
33111 "only marriage between brothers and sisters, between brothers' children"
33111 "and between sisters' children of the mother's side, because these belong"
33111 "to the same class; but the children of a sister can marry those of a"
33111 "brother and vice versa. A further step for preventing inbreeding is"
33111 "found among the Kamilaroi on the Darling River in New South Wales, where"
33111 "the two original classes are split into four, and every one of these is"
33111 "married as a whole to a certain other class. The first two classes are"
33111 "husbands and wives by birth. According to the place of the mother in the"
33111 "first or second class, the children belong to the third and fourth. The"
33111 "children of these two classes, who are also married to one another,"
33111 "again belong to the first and second class. So that a certain generation"
33111 "belongs to the first and second class, the next to the third and fourth"
33111 "and the following again to the first and second. Hence the children of"
33111 "natural brothers and sisters (on the mother's side) cannot marry one"
33111 "another, but their grandchildren can do so. This peculiarly complicated"
33111 "order of things is still more entangled by the inoculation--evidently at"
33111 "a later stage--with maternal law gentes. But we cannot discuss this"
33111 "further. Enough, the desire to prevent inbreeding again and again"
33111 "demands recognition, but feeling its way quite spontaneously, without a"
33111 "clear conception of the goal."
33111 ""
33111 "The group marriage is represented in Australia by class marriage, i. e.,"
33111 "mass marriage of a whole class of men frequently scattered over the"
33111 "whole breadth of the continent to an equally widespread class of women."
33111 "A close view of this group marriage does not offer quite such a horrible"
33111 "spectacle as the philistine imagination accustomed to brothel conditions"
33111 "generally pictures to itself. On the contrary, long years passed, before"
33111 "its existence was even suspected, and quite recently it is once more"
33111 "denied. To the casual observer it makes the impression of a loose"
33111 "monogamy and in certain places of polygamy, with occasional breach of"
33111 "faith. Years are required before one can discover, like Fison and"
33111 "Howitt, the law regulating these marital conditions that rather appeal"
33111 "in their practicability to the average European; the law enabling the"
33111 "strange Papuan, thousands of miles from his home and among people whose"
33111 "language he does not understand, to find frequently, from camp to camp"
33111 "and from tribe to tribe, women who will without resistance and"
33111 "guilelessly surrender to him; the law according to which a man with"
33111 "several women offers one to his guest for the night. Where the European"
33111 "sees immorality and lawlessness, there in reality a strict law is"
33111 "observed. The women belong to the marriage class of the stranger and,"
33111 "therefore, they are his wives by birth. The same moral law assigning"
33111 "both to one another forbids under penalty of proscription all sexual"
33111 "intercourse outside of the two marriage classes. Even when women are"
33111 "abducted, as is frequently the case in certain regions, the class law is"
33111 "carefully respected."
33111 ""
33111 "In the abduction of women, by the way, a trace of transition to monogamy"
33111 "is found even here, at least in the form of the pairing family. If a"
33111 "young man has abducted a girl with the help of his friends, they hold"
33111 "sexual intercourse with her one after another. But after that the girl"
33111 "is regarded as the wife of the young man who planned the abduction. And"
33111 "again, if an abducted woman deserts her husband and is caught by another"
33111 "man, she becomes the wife of the latter and the first has lost his"
33111 "privilege. Alongside of and within the generally existing group marriage"
33111 "such exclusive relations are formed, pairing for a shorter or longer"
33111 "term by the side of polygamy, so that here also group marriage is"
33111 "declining. The question is only which will first disappear under the"
33111 "pressure of European influence: group marriage or the Papuans addicted"
33111 "to it."
33111 ""
33111 "The marriage in whole classes, such as is in force in Australia, is no"
33111 "doubt a very low and primitive form of group marriage, while the"
33111 "Punaluan family, so far as we know, is its highest stage of development."
33111 "The former seems to be corresponding to the social stage of roving"
33111 "savages, the latter requires relatively settled communistic bodies and"
33111 "leads directly to the next higher stage of development. Between these"
33111 "two, we shall no doubt find many an intermediate stage. Here lies a"
33111 "barely opened, hardly entered field of investigation.[17]"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "3. THE PAIRING FAMILY."
33111 ""
33111 "A certain pairing for a longer or shorter term took place even during"
33111 "the group marriage or still earlier. A man had his principal wife (one"
33111 "can hardly call it favorite wife as yet) among many women, and he was to"
33111 "her the principal husband among others. This fact in no small degree"
33111 "contributed to the confusion among missionaries, who regarded group"
33111 "marriage now as a disorderly community of women, now as an arbitrary"
33111 "adultery. Such a habitual pairing would gain ground the more the gens"
33111 "developed and the more numerous the classes of \"brothers\" and \"sisters\""
33111 "became who were not permitted to marry one another. The impulse to"
33111 "prevent marriage of consanguineous relatives started by the gens went"
33111 "still further. Thus we find that among the Iroquois and most of the"
33111 "Indians in the lower stage of barbarism marriage is prohibited between"
33111 "all the relatives of their system of kinship, and this comprises several"
33111 "hundred kinds. By this increasing complication of marriage restrictions,"
33111 "group marriage became more and more impossible; it was displaced by the"
33111 "pairing family. At this stage one man lives with one woman, but in such"
33111 "a manner that polygamy, and occasional adultery, remain privileges of"
33111 "men, although the former occurs rarely for economic reasons. Women,"
33111 "however, are generally expected to be strictly faithful during the time"
33111 "of living together, and adultery on their part is cruelly punished. But"
33111 "the marriage-tie may be easily broken by either party, and the children"
33111 "belong to the mother alone, as formerly."
33111 ""
33111 "In this ever more extending restriction of marriage between"
33111 "consanguineous relations, natural selection also remains effective. As"
33111 "Morgan expresses it: \"Marriages between gentes that were not"
33111 "consanguineous produced a more vigorous race, physically and mentally;"
33111 "two progressive tribes intermarried, and the new skulls and brains"
33111 "naturally expanded until they comprised the faculties of both.\" Thus"
33111 "tribes composed of gentes necessarily either gained the supremacy over"
33111 "the backward ones or, by their example, carried them along in their"
33111 "wake."
33111 ""
33111 "The development of the family, then, is founded on the continual"
33111 "contraction of the circle, originally comprising the whole tribe, within"
33111 "which marital intercourse between both sexes was general. By the"
33111 "continual, exclusion, first of near, then of ever remoter relatives,"
33111 "including finally even those who were simply related legally, all group"
33111 "marriage becomes practically impossible. At last only one couple,"
33111 "temporarily and loosely united, remains; that molecule, the dissolution"
33111 "of which absolutely puts an end to marriage. Even from this we may infer"
33111 "how little the sexual love of the individual in the modern sense of the"
33111 "word had to do with the origin of monogamy. The practice of all nations"
33111 "of that stage still more proves this. While in the previous form of the"
33111 "family the men were never embarrassed for women, but rather had more"
33111 "than enough of them, women now became scarce and were sought after. With"
33111 "the pairing family, therefore, the abduction and barter of women"
33111 "began--widespread symptoms, and nothing but that, of a new and much more"
33111 "profound change. The pedantic Scot, McLennan, however, transmuted these"
33111 "symptoms, mere methods of obtaining women, into separate classes of the"
33111 "family under the head of \"marriage by capture\" and \"marriage by barter.\""
33111 "Moreover among American Indians and other nations in the same stage, the"
33111 "marriage agreement is not the business of the parties most concerned,"
33111 "who often are not even asked, but of their mothers. Frequently two"
33111 "persons entirely unknown to one another are thus engaged to be married"
33111 "and receive no information of the closing of the bargain, until the time"
33111 "for the marriage ceremony approaches. Before the wedding, the bridegroom"
33111 "brings gifts to the maternal relatives of the bride (not to her father"
33111 "or his relatives) as an equivalent for ceding the girl to him. Either of"
33111 "the married parties may dissolve the marriage at will. But among many"
33111 "tribes, as, e. g., the Iroquois, public opinion has gradually become"
33111 "averse to such separations. In case of domestic differences the gentile"
33111 "relatives of both parties endeavor to bring about a reconciliation, and"
33111 "not until they are unsuccessful a separation takes place. In this case"
33111 "the woman keeps the children, and both parties are free to marry again."
33111 ""
33111 "The pairing family, being too weak and too unstable to make an"
33111 "independent household necessary or even desirable, in no way dissolves"
33111 "the traditional communistic way of housekeeping. But household communism"
33111 "implies supremacy of women in the house as surely as exclusive"
33111 "recognition of a natural mother and the consequent impossibility of"
33111 "identifying the natural father signify high esteem for women, i. e.,"
33111 "mothers. It is one of the most absurd notions derived from eighteenth"
33111 "century enlightenment, that in the beginning of society woman was the"
33111 "slave of man. Among all savages and barbarians of the lower and middle"
33111 "stages, sometimes even of the higher stage, women not only have freedom,"
33111 "but are held in high esteem. What they were even in the pairing family,"
33111 "let Arthur Wright, for many years a missionary among the Seneca"
33111 "Iroquois, testify: \"As to their families, at a time when they still"
33111 "lived in their old long houses (communistic households of several"
33111 "families) ... a certain clan (gens) always reigned, so that the women"
33111 "choose their husbands from other clans (gentes).... The female part"
33111 "generally ruled the house; the provisions were held in common; but woe"
33111 "to the luckless husband or lover who was too indolent or too clumsy to"
33111 "contribute his share to the common stock. No matter how many children or"
33111 "how much private property he had in the house, he was liable at any"
33111 "moment to receive a hint to gather up his belongings and get out. And he"
33111 "could not dare to venture any resistance; the house was made too hot for"
33111 "him and he had no other choice, but to return to his own clan (gens) or,"
33111 "as was mostly the case, to look for another wife in some other clan. The"
33111 "women were the dominating power in the clans (gentes) and everywhere"
33111 "else. Occasionally they did not hesitate to dethrone a chief and degrade"
33111 "him to a common warrior.\""
33111 ""
33111 "The communistic household, in which most or all the women belong to one"
33111 "and the same gens, while the husbands come from different gentes, is the"
33111 "cause and foundation of the general and widespread supremacy of women in"
33111 "primeval times. The discovery of this fact is the third merit of"
33111 "Bachofen."
33111 ""
33111 "By way of supplement I wish to state that the reports of travelers and"
33111 "missionaries concerning the overburdening of women among savages and"
33111 "barbarians do not in the least contradict the above statements. The"
33111 "division of labor between both sexes is caused by other reasons than the"
33111 "social condition of women. Nations, where women have to work much harder"
33111 "than is proper for them in our opinion, often respect women more highly"
33111 "than Europeans do. The lady of civilized countries, surrounded with sham"
33111 "homage and a stranger to all real work stands on a far lower social"
33111 "level than a hard-working barbarian woman, regarded as a real lady"
33111 "(frowa-lady-mistress) and having the character of such."
33111 ""
33111 "Whether or not the pairing family has in our time entirely supplanted"
33111 "group marriage in America, can be decided only by closer investigations"
33111 "among those nations of northwestern and especially of southern America"
33111 "that are still in the higher stage of savagery. About the latter so many"
33111 "reports of sexual license are current that the assumption of a complete"
33111 "cessation of the ancient group marriage is hardly warranted. Evidently"
33111 "all traces of it have not yet disappeared. In at least forty North"
33111 "American tribes the man marrying an elder sister has the right to make"
33111 "all her sisters his wives as soon as they are of age, a survival of the"
33111 "community of men for the whole series of sisters. And Bancroft relates"
33111 "that the Indians of the Californian peninsula celebrate certain"
33111 "festivities uniting several \"tribes\" for the purpose of unrestricted"
33111 "sexual intercourse. These are evidently gentes that have preserved in"
33111 "these festivities a vague recollection of the time when the women of one"
33111 "gens had for their common husbands all the men of another gens, and vice"
33111 "versa. The same custom is still observed in Australia. Among certain"
33111 "nations it sometimes happens that the older men, the chief and"
33111 "sorcerer-priests, exploit the community of women for their own benefits"
33111 "and monopolize all the women. But in their turn they must restore the"
33111 "old community during certain festivities and great assemblies,"
33111 "permitting their wives to enjoy themselves with the young men. A whole"
33111 "series of examples of such periodical saturnalia restoring for a short"
33111 "time the ancient sexual freedom is quoted by Westermarck:[18] among the"
33111 "Hos, the Santals, the Punjas and Kotars in India, among some African"
33111 "nations, etc. Curiously enough Westermarck concludes that this is a"
33111 "survival, not of group marriage, the existence of which he denies,"
33111 "but--of a rutting season which primitive man had in common with other"
33111 "animals."
33111 ""
33111 "Here we touch Bachofen's fourth great discovery: the widespread form of"
33111 "transition from group marriage to pairing family. What Bachofen"
33111 "represents as a penance for violating the old divine laws--the penalty"
33111 "with which a woman redeems her right to chastity, is in fact only a"
33111 "mystical expression for the penalty paid by a woman for becoming exempt"
33111 "from the ancient community of men and acquiring the right of"
33111 "surrendering to one man only. This penalty consists in a limited"
33111 "surrender: Babylonian women had to surrender once a year in the temple"
33111 "of Mylitta; other nations of Western Asia sent their young women for"
33111 "years to the temple of Anaitis, where they had to practice free love"
33111 "with favorites of their own choice before they were allowed to marry."
33111 "Similar customs in a religious disguise are common to nearly all Asiatic"
33111 "nations between the Mediterranean and the Ganges. The penalty for"
33111 "exemption becomes gradually lighter in course of time, as Bachofen"
33111 "remarks: \"The annually repeated surrender gives place to a single"
33111 "sacrifice; the hetaerism of the matrons is followed by that of the"
33111 "maidens, the promiscuous intercourse during marriage to that before"
33111 "wedding, the indiscriminate intercourse with all to that with certain"
33111 "individuals.\"[19] Among some nations the religious disguise is missing."
33111 "Among others--Thracians, Celts, etc., in classic times, many primitive"
33111 "inhabitants of India, Malay nations, South Sea Islanders and many"
33111 "American Indians to this day--the girls enjoy absolute sexual freedom"
33111 "before marriage. This is especially true almost everywhere in South"
33111 "America, as everybody can confirm who penetrates a little into the"
33111 "interior. Agassiz, e. g., relates[20] an anecdote of a wealthy family of"
33111 "Indian descent. On being introduced to the daughter he asked something"
33111 "about her father, presuming him to be her mother's husband, who was in"
33111 "the war against Paraguay. But the mother replied, smiling: \"Nao tem pai,"
33111 "he filha da fortuna\"--she hasn't any father; she is the daughter of"
33111 "chance. \"It is the way the Indian or half-breed women here always speak"
33111 "of their illegitimate children; and though they say it without an"
33111 "intonation of sadness or of blame, apparently as unconscious of any"
33111 "wrong or shame as if they said the father was absent or dead, it has the"
33111 "most melancholy significance; it seems to speak of such absolute"
33111 "desertion. So far is this from being an unusual case, that among the"
33111 "common people the opposite seems the exception. Children are frequently"
33111 "quite ignorant of their parentage. They know about their mother, for all"
33111 "the care and responsibility falls upon her, but they have no knowledge"
33111 "of their father; nor does it seem to occur to the woman that she or her"
33111 "children have any claim upon him.\" What seems so strange to the"
33111 "civilized man, is simply the rule of maternal law and group marriage."
33111 ""
33111 "Again, among other nations the friends and relatives of the bridegroom"
33111 "or the wedding guests claim their traditional right to the bride, and"
33111 "the bridegroom comes last. This custom prevailed in ancient times on the"
33111 "Baleares and among the African Augilers; it is observed to this day by"
33111 "the Bareas in Abyssinia. In still other cases, an official person--the"
33111 "chief of a tribe or a gens, the cazique, shamane, priest, prince or"
33111 "whatever may be his title--represents the community and exercises the"
33111 "right of the first night. All modern romantic whitewashing"
33111 "notwithstanding, this jus primae noctis, is still in force among most of"
33111 "the natives of Alaska,[21] among the Tahus of northern Mexico[22] and"
33111 "some other nations. And during the whole of the middle ages it was"
33111 "practiced at least in originally Celtic countries, where it was directly"
33111 "transmitted by group marriage, e. g. in Aragonia. While in Castilia the"
33111 "peasant was never a serf, the most disgraceful serfdom existed in"
33111 "Aragonia, until abolished by the decision of Ferdinand the Catholic in"
33111 "1486. In this document we read: \"We decide and declare that the"
33111 "aforesaid 'senyors' (barons) ... shall neither sleep the first night"
33111 "with the wife of a peasant, nor shall they in the first night after the"
33111 "wedding, when the woman has gone to bed, step over said woman or bed as"
33111 "a sign of their authority. Neither shall the aforesaid senyors use the"
33111 "daughter or the son of any peasant, with or without pay, against their"
33111 "will.\" (Quoted in the Catalonian original by Sugenheim, \"Serfdom,\""
33111 "Petersburg, 1861, page 35.)"
33111 ""
33111 "Bachofen, furthermore, is perfectly right in contending that the"
33111 "transition from what he calls \"hetaerism\" or \"incestuous generation\" to"
33111 "monogamy was brought about mainly by women. The more in the course of"
33111 "economic development, undermining the old communism and increasing the"
33111 "density of population, the traditional sexual relations lost their"
33111 "innocent character suited to the primitive forest, the more debasing and"
33111 "oppressive they naturally appeared to women; and the more they"
33111 "consequently longed for relief by the right of chastity, of temporary or"
33111 "permanent marriage with one man. This progress could not be due to men"
33111 "for the simple reason that they never, even to this day, had the least"
33111 "intention of renouncing the pleasures of actual group marriage. Not"
33111 "until the women had accomplished the transition to the pairing family"
33111 "could the men introduce strict monogamy--true, only for women."
33111 ""
33111 "The pairing family arose on the boundary line between savagery and"
33111 "barbarism, generally in the higher stage of savagery, here and there in"
33111 "the lower stage of barbarism. It is the form of the family"
33111 "characteristic for barbarism, as group marriage is for savagery and"
33111 "monogamy for civilization. In order to develop it into established"
33111 "monogamy, other causes than those active hitherto were required. In the"
33111 "pairing family the group was already reduced to its last unit, its"
33111 "biatomic molecule: one man and one woman. Natural selection, had"
33111 "accomplished its purpose by a continually increasing restriction of"
33111 "sexual intercourse. Nothing remained to be done in this direction."
33111 "Unless new social forces became active, there was no reason why a new"
33111 "form of the family should develop out of the pairing family. But these"
33111 "forces did become active."
33111 ""
33111 "We now leave America, the classic soil of the pairing family. No sign"
33111 "permits the conclusion that a higher form of the family was developed"
33111 "here, that any established form of monogamy ever existed anywhere in the"
33111 "New World before the discovery and conquest. Not so in the Old World."
33111 ""
33111 "In the latter, the domestication of animals and the breeding of flocks"
33111 "had developed a hitherto unknown source of wealth and created entirely"
33111 "new social conditions. Up to the lower stage of barbarism, fixed wealth"
33111 "was almost exclusively represented by houses, clothing, rough ornaments"
33111 "and the tools for obtaining and preparing food: boats, weapons and"
33111 "household articles of the simplest kind. Nourishment had to be secured"
33111 "afresh day by day. But now, with their herds of horses, camels, donkeys,"
33111 "cattle, sheep, goats and hogs, the advancing nomadic nations--the Aryans"
33111 "in the Indian Punjab, in the region of the Ganges and the steppes of the"
33111 "Oxus and Jaxartes, then still more rich in water-veins than now; the"
33111 "Semites on the Euphrates and Tigris--had acquired possessions demanding"
33111 "only the most crude attention and care in order to propagate themselves"
33111 "in ever increasing numbers and yield the most abundant store of milk and"
33111 "meat. All former means of obtaining food were now forced to the"
33111 "background. Hunting, once a necessity, now became a sport."
33111 ""
33111 "But who was the owner of this new wealth? Doubtless it was originally"
33111 "the gens. However, private ownership of flocks must have had an early"
33111 "beginning. It is difficult to say whether to the author of the so-called"
33111 "first book of Moses Father Abraham appeared as the owner of his flocks"
33111 "by virtue of his privilege as head of a communistic family or of his"
33111 "capacity as gentile chief by actual descent. So much is certain: we"
33111 "must not regard him as a proprietor in the modern sense of the word. It"
33111 "is furthermore certain that everywhere on the threshold of documentary"
33111 "history we find the flocks in the separate possession of chiefs of"
33111 "families, exactly like the productions of barbarian art, such as metal"
33111 "ware, articles of luxury and, finally, the human cattle--the slaves."
33111 ""
33111 "For now slavery was also invented. To the barbarian of the lower stage a"
33111 "slave was of no use. The American Indians, therefore, treated their"
33111 "vanquished enemies in quite a different way from nations of a higher"
33111 "stage. The men were tortured or adopted as brothers into the tribe of"
33111 "the victors. The women were married or likewise adopted with their"
33111 "surviving children. The human labor power at this stage does not yet"
33111 "produce a considerable amount over and above its cost of subsistence."
33111 "But the introduction of cattle raising, metal industry, weaving and"
33111 "finally agriculture wrought a change. Just as the once easily obtainable"
33111 "wives now had an exchange value and were bought, so labor power was now"
33111 "procured, especially since the flocks had definitely become private"
33111 "property. The family did not increase as rapidly as the cattle. More"
33111 "people were needed for superintending; for this purpose the captured"
33111 "enemy was available and, besides, he could be increased by breeding like"
33111 "the cattle."
33111 ""
33111 "Such riches, once they had become the private property of certain"
33111 "families and augmented rapidly, gave a powerful impulse to society"
33111 "founded on the pairing family and the maternal gens. The pairing family"
33111 "had introduced a new element. By the side of the natural mother it had"
33111 "placed the authentic natural father who probably was better"
33111 "authenticated than many a \"father\" of our day. According to the division"
33111 "of labor in those times, the task of obtaining food and the tools"
33111 "necessary for this purpose fell to the share of the man; hence he owned"
33111 "the latter and kept them in case of a separation, as the women did the"
33111 "household goods. According to the social custom of that time, the man"
33111 "was also the owner of the new source of existence, the cattle, and later"
33111 "on of the new labor power, the slaves. But according to the same custom,"
33111 "his children could not inherit his property, for the following reasons:"
33111 "By maternal law, i. e., while descent was traced only along the female"
33111 "line, and by the original custom of inheriting in the gens, the gentile"
33111 "relatives inherited the property of their deceased gentile relative. The"
33111 "wealth had to remain in the gens. In view of the insignificance of the"
33111 "objects, the property may have gone in practice to the closest gentile"
33111 "relatives, i. e., the consanguine relatives on the mother's side. The"
33111 "children of the dead man, however, did not belong to his gens, but to"
33111 "that of their mother. They inherited first together with the other"
33111 "consanguine relatives of the mother, later on perhaps in preference to"
33111 "the others. But they could not inherit from their father, because they"
33111 "did not belong to his gens, where his property had to remain. Hence,"
33111 "after the death of a cattle owner, the cattle would fall to his"
33111 "brothers, sisters and the children of his sisters, or to the offspring"
33111 "of the sisters of his mother. His own children were disinherited."
33111 ""
33111 "In the measure of the increasing wealth man's position in the family"
33111 "became superior to that of woman, and the desire arose to use this"
33111 "fortified position for the purpose of overthrowing the traditional law"
33111 "of inheritance in favor of his children. But this was not feasible as"
33111 "long as maternal law was valid. This law had to be abolished, and it"
33111 "was. This was by no means as difficult as it appears to us to-day. For"
33111 "this revolution--one of the most radical ever experienced by"
33111 "humanity--did not have to touch a single living member of the gens. All"
33111 "its members could remain what they had always been. The simple"
33111 "resolution was sufficient, that henceforth the offspring of the male"
33111 "members should belong to the gens, while the children of the female"
33111 "members should be excluded by transferring them to the gens of their"
33111 "father. This abolished the tracing of descent by female lineage and the"
33111 "maternal right of inheritance, and instituted descent by male lineage"
33111 "and the paternal right of inheritance. How and when this revolution was"
33111 "accomplished by the nations of the earth, we do not know. It belongs"
33111 "entirely to prehistoric times. That it was accomplished is proven more"
33111 "than satisfactorily by the copious traces of maternal law collected"
