33111 "especially by Bachofen. How easily it is accomplished we may observe in"
33111 "a whole series of Indian tribes, that recently passed through or are"
33111 "still engaged in it, partly under the influence of increasing wealth and"
33111 "changed modes of living (transfer from forests to the prairie), partly"
33111 "through the moral pressure of civilization and missionaries. Six out of"
33111 "eight Missouri tribes have male descent and inheritance, while only two"
33111 "retain female descent and inheritance. The Shawnees, Miamis and"
33111 "Delawares follow the custom of placing their children into the male gens"
33111 "by giving them a gentile name belonging to the father's gens, so that"
33111 "they may be entitled to inherit. \"Innate casuistry of man, to change the"
33111 "objects by changing their names, and to find loopholes for breaking"
33111 "tradition inside of tradition where a direct interest was a sufficient"
33111 "motive.\" (Marx.) This made confusion worse confounded, which could be"
33111 "and partially was remedied alone by paternal law. \"This seems to be the"
33111 "most natural transition.\" (Marx.) As to the opinion of the comparative"
33111 "jurists, how this transition took place among the civilized nations of"
33111 "the old world--although only in hypotheses--compare M. Kovalevsky,"
33111 "Tableau des origines et de l'evolution de la famille et de la"
33111 "propriete, Stockholm, 1890."
33111 ""
33111 "The downfall of maternal law was the historic defeat of the female sex."
33111 "The men seized the reins also in the house, the women were stripped of"
33111 "their dignity, enslaved, tools of men's lust and mere machines for the"
33111 "generation of children. This degrading position of women, especially"
33111 "conspicuous among the Greeks of heroic and still more of classic times,"
33111 "was gradually glossed over and disguised or even clad in a milder form."
33111 "But it is by no means obliterated."
33111 ""
33111 "The first effect of the established supremacy of men became now visible"
33111 "in the reappearance of the intermediate form of the patriarchal family."
33111 "Its most significant feature is not polygamy, of which more anon, but"
33111 "\"the organization of a certain number of free and unfree persons into"
33111 "one family under the paternal authority of the head of the family. In"
33111 "the Semitic form this head of the family lives in polygamy, the unfree"
33111 "members have wife and children, and the purpose of the whole"
33111 "organization is the tending of herds in a limited territory.\" The"
33111 "essential points are the assimilation of the unfree element and the"
33111 "paternal authority. Hence the ideal type of this form of the family is"
33111 "the Roman family. The word familia did not originally signify the"
33111 "composite ideal of sentimentality and domestic strife in the present day"
33111 "philistine mind. Among the Romans it did not even apply in the beginning"
33111 "to the leading couple and its children, but to the slaves alone. Famulus"
33111 "means domestic slave, and familia is the aggregate number of slaves"
33111 "belonging to one man. At the time of Gajus, the familia, id est"
33111 "patrimonium (i. e., paternal legacy), was still bequeathed by testament."
33111 "The expression was invented by the Romans in order to designate a new"
33111 "social organism, the head of which had a wife, children and a number of"
33111 "slaves under his paternal authority and according to Roman law the"
33111 "right of life and death over all of them. \"The word is, therefore, not"
33111 "older than the ironclad family system of the Latin tribes, which arose"
33111 "after the introduction of agriculture and of lawful slavery, and after"
33111 "the separation of the Aryan Itali from the Greeks.\" Marx adds: \"The"
33111 "modern family contains the germ not only of slavery (servitus), but also"
33111 "of serfdom, because it has from the start a relation to agricultural"
33111 "service. It comprises in miniature all those contrasts that later on"
33111 "develop more broadly in society and the state.\""
33111 ""
33111 "Such a form of the family shows the transition from the pairing family"
33111 "to monogamy. In order to secure the faithfulness of the wife, and hence"
33111 "the reliability of paternal lineage, the women are delivered absolutely"
33111 "into the power of the men; in killing his wife, the husband simply"
33111 "exercises his right."
33111 ""
33111 "With the patriarchal family we enter the domain of written history, a"
33111 "field in which comparative law can render considerable assistance. And"
33111 "here it has brought about considerable progress indeed. We owe to Maxim"
33111 "Kovalevsky (Tableau etc. de la famille et de la propriete, Stockholm,"
33111 "1890, p. 60-100) the proof, that the patriarchal household community,"
33111 "found to this day among Serbians and Bulgarians under the names of"
33111 "Zadruga (friendly bond) and Bratstvo (fraternity), and in a modified"
33111 "form among oriental nations, formed the stage of transition between the"
33111 "maternal family derived from group marriage and the monogamous family of"
33111 "the modern world. This seems at least established for the historic"
33111 "nations of the old world, for Aryans and Semites."
33111 ""
33111 "The Zadruga of southern Slavonia offers the best still existing"
33111 "illustration of such a family communism. It comprises several"
33111 "generations of the father's descendants, together with their wives, all"
33111 "living together on the same farm, tilling their fields in common,"
33111 "living and clothing themselves from the same stock, and possessing"
33111 "collectively the surplus of their earnings. The community is managed by"
33111 "the master of the house (domacin), who acts as its representative, may"
33111 "sell inferior objects, has charge of the treasury and is responsible for"
33111 "it as well as for a proper business administration. He is chosen by vote"
33111 "and is not necessarily the oldest man. The women and their work are"
33111 "directed by the mistress of the house (domacica), who is generally the"
33111 "wife of the domacin. She also has an important, and often final, voice"
33111 "in choosing a husband for the girls. But the highest authority is vested"
33111 "in the family council, the assembly of all grown companions, male and"
33111 "female. The domacin is responsible to this council. It takes all"
33111 "important resolutions, sits in judgment on the members of the household,"
33111 "decides the question of important purchases and sales, especially of"
33111 "land, etc."
33111 ""
33111 "It is only about ten years since the existence of such family communism"
33111 "in the Russia of to-day was proven. At present it is generally"
33111 "acknowledged to be rooted in popular Russian custom quite as much as the"
33111 "obscina or village community."
33111 ""
33111 "It is found in the oldest Russian code, the Pravda of Jaroslav, under"
33111 "the same name (vervj) as in the Dalmatian code, and may also be traced"
33111 "in Polish and Czech historical records."
33111 ""
33111 "Likewise among Germans, the economic unit according to Heussler"
33111 "(Institutions of German law) is not originally the single family, but"
33111 "the \"collective household,\" comprising several generations or single"
33111 "families and, besides, often enough unfree individuals. The Roman family"
33111 "is also traced to this type, and hence the absolute authority of the"
33111 "master of the house and the defenselessness of the other members in"
33111 "regard to him is strongly questioned of late. Similar communities are"
33111 "furthermore said to have existed among the Celts of Ireland. In France"
33111 "they were preserved up to the time of the Revolution in Nivernais under"
33111 "the name of \"parconneries,\" and in the Franche Comte they are not quite"
33111 "extinct yet. In the region of Louhans (Saone et Loire) we find large"
33111 "farmhouses with a high central hall for common use reaching up to the"
33111 "roof and surrounded by sleeping rooms accessible by the help of stairs"
33111 "with six to eight steps. Several generations of the same family live"
33111 "together in such a house."
33111 ""
33111 "In India, the household community with collective agriculture is already"
33111 "mentioned by Nearchus at the time of Alexander the Great, and it exists"
33111 "to this day in the same region, in the Punjab and the whole Northwest of"
33111 "the country. In the Caucasus it was located by Kovalevski himself."
33111 ""
33111 "In Algeria it is still found among the Kabyles. Even in America it is"
33111 "said to have existed. It is supposed to be identical with the"
33111 "\"Calpullis\" described by Zurita in ancient Mexico. In Peru, however,"
33111 "Cunow (Ausland, 1890, No. 42-44) has demonstrated rather clearly that at"
33111 "the time of the conquest a sort of a constitution in marks (called"
33111 "curiously enough marca), with a periodical allotment of arable soil, and"
33111 "consequently individual tillage, was in existence."
33111 ""
33111 "At any rate, the patriarchal household community with collective tillage"
33111 "and ownership of land now assumes an entirely different meaning than"
33111 "heretofore. We can no longer doubt that it played an important role"
33111 "among the civilized and some other nations of the old world in the"
33111 "transition from the maternal to the single family. Later on we shall"
33111 "return to Kovaleski's further conclusion that it was also the stage of"
33111 "transition from which developed the village or mark community with"
33111 "individual tillage and first periodical, then permanent allotment of"
33111 "arable and pasture lands."
33111 ""
33111 "In regard to the family life within these household communities it must"
33111 "be remarked that at least in Russia the master of the house has the"
33111 "reputation of strongly abusing his position against the younger women of"
33111 "the community, especially his daughters-in-law, and of transforming them"
33111 "into a harem for himself. Russian popular songs are very eloquent on"
33111 "this point."
33111 ""
33111 "Before taking up monogamy, which rapidly developed after the downfall of"
33111 "maternal law, let me say a few words about polygamy and polyandry. Both"
33111 "forms of the family can only be exceptions, historical products of"
33111 "luxury so to speak, unless they could be found side by side in the same"
33111 "country, which is apparently not the case. As the men excluded from"
33111 "polygamy cannot find consolation in the women left over by polyandry,"
33111 "the number of men and women being hitherto approximately equal without"
33111 "regard to social institutions, it becomes of itself impossible to confer"
33111 "on any one of these two forms the distinction of general preference."
33111 "Indeed, the polygamy of one man was evidently the product of slavery,"
33111 "confined to certain exceptional positions. In the Semitic patriarchal"
33111 "family, only the patriarch himself, or at best a few of his sons,"
33111 "practice polygamy, the others must be satisfied with one wife. This is"
33111 "the case to-day in the whole Orient. Polygamy is a privilege of the"
33111 "wealthy and distinguished, and is mainly realized by purchase of female"
33111 "slaves. The mass of the people live in monogamy. Polyandry in India and"
33111 "Thibet is likewise an exception. Its surely not uninteresting origin"
33111 "from group marriage requires still closer investigation. In its practice"
33111 "it seems, by the way, much more tolerant than the jealous Harem"
33111 "establishment of the Mohammedans. At least among the Nairs of India,"
33111 "three, four or more men have indeed one woman in common; but every one"
33111 "of them may have a second woman in common with three or more other men;"
33111 "and in the same way a third, fourth, etc. It is strange that McLennan"
33111 "did not discover the new class of \"club marriage\" in these marital"
33111 "clubs, in several of which one may be a member and which he himself"
33111 "describes. This marriage club business is, however, by no means actual"
33111 "polyandry. It is on the contrary, as Giraud-Teulon already remarks, a"
33111 "specialized form of group marriage. The men live in polygamy, the women"
33111 "in polyandry."
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "4. THE MONOGAMOUS FAMILY."
33111 ""
33111 "It develops from the pairing family, as we have already shown, during"
33111 "the time of transition from the middle to the higher stage of barbarism."
33111 "Its final victory is one of the signs of beginning civilization. It is"
33111 "founded on male supremacy for the pronounced purpose of breeding"
33111 "children of indisputable paternal lineage. The latter is required,"
33111 "because these children shall later on inherit the fortune of their"
33111 "father. The monogamous family is distinguished from the pairing family"
33111 "by the far greater durability of wedlock, which can no longer be"
33111 "dissolved at the pleasure of either party. As a rule, it is only the man"
33111 "who can still dissolve it and cast off his wife. The privilege of"
33111 "conjugal faithlessness remains sanctioned for men at least by custom"
33111 "(the Code Napoleon concedes it directly to them, as long as they do not"
33111 "bring their concubines into the houses of their wives). This privilege"
33111 "is more and more enjoyed with the increasing development of society. If"
33111 "the woman remembers the ancient sexual practices and attempts to revive"
33111 "them, she is punished more severely than ever."
33111 ""
33111 "The whole severity of this new form of the family confronts us among the"
33111 "Greeks. While, as Marx observes, the position of the female gods in"
33111 "mythology shows an earlier period, when women still occupied a freer"
33111 "and more respected plane, we find woman already degraded by the"
33111 "supremacy of man and the competition of slaves during the time of the"
33111 "heroes. Read in the Odysseia how Telemachos reproves and silences his"
33111 "mother. The captured young women, according to Homer, are delivered to"
33111 "the sensual lust of the victors. The leaders in the order of their rank"
33111 "select the most beautiful captives. The whole Iliad notoriously revolves"
33111 "around the quarrel between Achilles and Agamemnon about such a captured"
33111 "woman. In mentioning any hero of importance, the captured girl sharing"
33111 "his tent and bed is never omitted. These girls are also taken into the"
33111 "hero's home country and his house, as Kassandra by Agamemnon in"
33111 "Aeschylos. Boys born by these female slaves receive a small share of the"
33111 "paternal heirloom and are regarded as free men. Teukros is such an"
33111 "illegitimate son and may use his father's name. The wife is expected to"
33111 "put up with everything, while herself remaining chaste and faithful."
33111 "Although the Greek woman of heroic times is more highly respected than"
33111 "she of the civilized period, still she is for her husband only the"
33111 "mother of his legal heirs, his first housekeeper and the superintendent"
33111 "of the female slaves, whom he can and does make his concubines at will."
33111 ""
33111 "It is this practice of slavery by the side of monogamy, the existence of"
33111 "young and beautiful female slaves belonging without any restriction to"
33111 "their master, which from the very beginning gives to monogamy the"
33111 "specific character of being monogamy for women only, but not for men."
33111 "And this character remains to this day."
33111 ""
33111 "For the Greeks of later times we must make a distinction between Dorians"
33111 "and Ionians. The former, with Sparta as their classic example, have in"
33111 "many respects still more antiquated marriage customs than even Homer"
33111 "illustrates. In Sparta existed a form of the pairing family modified by"
33111 "the contemporaneous ideas of the state and still recalling group"
33111 "marriage in many ways. Sterile marriages were dissolved. King"
33111 "Anaxandridas (about 650 before Christ) took another wife besides his"
33111 "childless one and kept two households. About the same time King Ariston"
33111 "added another wife to two childless ones, one of which he dismissed."
33111 "Furthermore, several brothers could have one wife in common; a friend"
33111 "who liked his friend's wife better than his own could share her with"
33111 "him, and it was not considered indecent to place a wife at the disposal"
33111 "of a sturdy \"stallion,\" as Bismarck would have said, even though he"
33111 "might not be a citizen. A certain passage in Plutarch, where a Spartan"
33111 "matron refers a lover, who persists in making offers to her, to her"
33111 "husband, seems to indicate--according to Schoemann--even a still greater"
33111 "sexual freedom. Also adultery, faithlessness of a wife behind her"
33111 "husband's back, was unheard of. On the other hand, domestic slavery in"
33111 "Sparta, at least during the best time, was unknown, and the serf Helots"
33111 "lived on separate country seats. Hence there was less temptation for a"
33111 "Spartan to hold intercourse with other women. As was to be expected"
33111 "under such circumstances, the women of Sparta occupied a more highly"
33111 "respected place than those of other Greeks. Spartan women and the"
33111 "Athenian hetaerae were the only Greek women of whom the ancients speak"
33111 "respectfully and whose remarks they considered worthy of notice."
33111 ""
33111 "Quite a different condition among Ionians, whose representative is"
33111 "Athens. The girls learned only to spin, weave and sew, at the most a"
33111 "little reading and writing. They were practically shut in and had only"
33111 "the company of other women."
33111 ""
33111 "The women's room formed a separate part of the house, on the upper floor"
33111 "or in a rear building, where men, especially strangers, did not easily"
33111 "enter and whither the women retreated when male visitors came. The"
33111 "women did not leave the house without being accompanied by a female"
33111 "slave. At home they were strictly guarded. Aristophanes speaks of"
33111 "Molossian dogs that were kept to frighten off adulterers. And at least"
33111 "in the Asiatic towns, eunuchs were kept for guarding women. Even at"
33111 "Herodotus' time these eunuchs were manufactured for the trade, and"
33111 "according to Wachsmuth not for barbarians alone. By Euripides woman is"
33111 "designated as \"oikurema,\" a neuter signifying an object for"
33111 "housekeeping, and beside the business of breeding children she served to"
33111 "the Athenian for nothing but his chief house maid. The man had his"
33111 "gymnastic exercises, his public meetings, from which the women were"
33111 "excluded. Besides, the man very often had female slaves at his disposal,"
33111 "and during the most flourishing time of Athens an extensive prostitution"
33111 "which was at least patronized by the state. It was precisely on the"
33111 "basis of this prostitution that the unique type of Ionic women"
33111 "developed; the hetaerae. They rose by esprit and artistic taste as far"
33111 "above the general level of antique womanhood as the Spartan women by"
33111 "their character. But that it was necessary to become a hetaera before"
33111 "one could be a woman, constitutes the severest denunciation of the"
33111 "Athenian family."
33111 ""
33111 "The Athenian family became in the course of time the model after which"
33111 "not only the rest of the Ionians, but gradually all the Greeks at home"
33111 "and abroad molded their domestic relations. Nevertheless, in spite of"
33111 "all seclusion and watching, the Grecian ladies found sufficient"
33111 "opportunity for deceiving their husbands. The latter who would have been"
33111 "ashamed of betraying any love for their wives, found recreation in all"
33111 "kinds of love affairs with hetaerae. But the degradation of the women"
33111 "was avenged in the men and degraded them also, until they sank into the"
33111 "abomination of boy-love. They degraded their gods and themselves by the"
33111 "myth of Ganymedes."
33111 ""
33111 "Such was the origin of monogamy, as far as we may trace it in the most"
33111 "civilized and most highly developed nation of antiquity. It was by no"
33111 "means a fruit of individual sex-love and had nothing to do with the"
33111 "latter, for the marriages remained as conventional as ever. Monogamy was"
33111 "the first form of the family not founded on natural, but on economic"
33111 "conditions, viz.: the victory of private property over primitive and"
33111 "natural collectivism. Supremacy of the man in the family and generation"
33111 "of children that could be his offspring alone and were destined to be"
33111 "the heirs of his wealth--these were openly avowed by the Greeks to be"
33111 "the sole objects of monogamy. For the rest it was a burden to them, a"
33111 "duty to the gods, the state and their own ancestors, a duty to be"
33111 "fulfilled and no more. In Athens the law enforced not only the marriage,"
33111 "but also the fulfillment of a minimum of the so-called matrimonial"
33111 "duties on the man's part."
33111 ""
33111 "Monogamy, then, does by no means enter history as a reconciliation of"
33111 "man and wife and still less as the highest form of marriage. On the"
33111 "contrary, it enters as the subjugation of one sex by the other, as the"
33111 "proclamation of an antagonism between the sexes unknown in all preceding"
33111 "history. In an old unpublished manuscript written by Marx and myself in"
33111 "1846, I find the following passage: \"The first division of labor is that"
33111 "of man and wife in breeding children.\" And to-day I may add: The first"
33111 "class antagonism appearing in history coincides with the development of"
33111 "the antagonism of man and wife in monogamy, and the first class"
33111 "oppression with that of the female by the male sex. Monogamy was a great"
33111 "historical progress. But by the side of slavery and private property it"
33111 "marks at the same time that epoch which, reaching down to our days,"
33111 "takes with all progress also a step backwards, relatively speaking, and"
33111 "develops the welfare and advancement of one by the woe and submission of"
33111 "the other. It is the cellular form of civilized society which enables us"
33111 "to study the nature of its now fully developed contrasts and"
33111 "contradictions."
33111 ""
33111 "The old relative freedom of sexual intercourse by no means disappeared"
33111 "with the victory of the pairing or even of the monogamous family. \"The"
33111 "old conjugal system, now reduced to narrower limits by the gradual"
33111 "disappearance of the punaluan groups, still environed the advancing"
33111 "family, which it was to follow to the verge of civilization.... It"
33111 "finally disappeared in the new form of hetaerism, which still follows"
33111 "mankind in civilization as a dark shadow upon the family.\"[23]"
33111 ""
33111 "By hetaerism Morgan designates sexual intercourse of men with unmarried"
33111 "women outside of the monogamous family, flourishing, as is well known,"
33111 "during the whole period of civilization in many different forms and"
33111 "tending more and more to open prostitution. This hetaerism is directly"
33111 "derived from group marriage, from the sacrificial surrender of women for"
33111 "the purpose of obtaining the right to chastity. The surrender for money"
33111 "was at first a religious act; it took place in the temple of the goddess"
33111 "of love and the money flowed originally into the treasury of the temple."
33111 "The hierodulae of Anaitis in Armenia, of Aphrodite in Corinth and the"
33111 "religious dancing girls of India attached to the temples, the so-called"
33111 "bajaderes (derived from the Portuguese \"bailadera,\" dancing girl), were"
33111 "the first prostitutes. The surrender, originally the duty of every"
33111 "woman, was later on practiced by these priestesses alone in"
33111 "representation of all others. Among other nations, hetaerism is derived"
33111 "from the sexual freedom permitted to girls before marriage--also a"
33111 "survival of the group marriage, only transmitted by another route. With"
33111 "the rise of different property relations, in the higher stage of"
33111 "barbarism, wage labor appears sporadically by the side of slavery, and"
33111 "at the same time its unavoidable companion, professional prostitution of"
33111 "free women by the side of the forced surrender of female slaves. It is"
33111 "the heirloom bequeathed by group marriage to civilization, a gift as"
33111 "ambiguous as everything else produced by ambiguous, double-faced,"
33111 "schismatic and contradictory civilization. Here monogamy, there"
33111 "hetaerism and its most extreme form, prostitution. Hetaerism is as much"
33111 "a social institution as all others. It continues the old sexual"
33111 "freedom--for the benefit of the men. In reality not only permitted, but"
33111 "also assiduously practised by the ruling class, it is denounced only"
33111 "nominally. Still in practice this denunciation strikes by no means the"
33111 "men who indulge in it, but only the women. These are ostracised and cast"
33111 "out by society, in order to proclaim once more the fundamental law of"
33111 "unconditional male supremacy over the female sex."
33111 ""
33111 "However, a second contradiction is thereby developed within monogamy"
33111 "itself. By the side of the husband, who is making his life pleasant by"
33111 "hetaerism, stands the neglected wife. And you cannot have one side of"
33111 "the contradiction without the other, just as you cannot have the whole"
33111 "apple after eating half of it. Nevertheless this seems to have been the"
33111 "idea of the men, until their wives taught them a lesson. Monogamy"
33111 "introduces two permanent social characters that were formerly unknown:"
33111 "the standing lover of the wife and the cuckold. The men had gained the"
33111 "victory over the women, but the vanquished magnanimously provided the"
33111 "coronation. In addition to monogamy and hetaerism, adultery became an"
33111 "unavoidable social institution--denounced, severely punished, but"
33111 "irrepressible. The certainty of paternal parentage rested as of old on"
33111 "moral conviction at best, and in order to solve the unreconcilable"
33111 "contradiction, the code Napoleon decreed in its article 312: \"L'enfant"
33111 "concu pendant le mariage a pour pere le mari;\" the child conceived"
33111 "during marriage has for its father--the husband. This is the last result"
33111 "of three thousand years of monogamy."
33111 ""
33111 "Thus we have in the monogamous family, at least in those cases that"
33111 "remain true to historical development and clearly express the conflict"
33111 "between man and wife created by the exclusive supremacy of men, a"
33111 "miniature picture of the contrasts and contradictions of society at"
33111 "large. Split by class-differences since the beginning of civilization,"
33111 "society has been unable to reconcile and overcome these antitheses. Of"
33111 "course, I am referring here only to those cases of monogamy, where"
33111 "matrimonial life actually remains in accord with the original character"
33111 "of the whole institution, but where the wife revolts against the rule of"
33111 "the man. Nobody knows better than your German philistine that not all"
33111 "marriages follow such a course. He does not understand how to maintain"
33111 "the control of his own home any better than that of the State, and his"
33111 "wife is, therefore, fully entitled to wearing the trousers, which he"
33111 "does not deserve. But he thinks himself far superior to his French"
33111 "companion in misery, who more frequently fares far worse."
33111 ""
33111 "The monogamous family, by the way, did not everywhere and always appear"
33111 "in the classic severe form it had among the Greeks. Among the Romans,"
33111 "who as future conquerors of the world had a sharper although less"
33111 "refined eye than the Greeks, the women were freer and more respected. A"
33111 "Roman believed that the conjugal faith of his wife was sufficiently"
33111 "safeguarded by his power over her life and death. Moreover, the women"
33111 "could voluntarily dissolve the marriage as well as the men. But the"
33111 "highest progress in the development of monogamy was doubtless due to the"
33111 "entrance of the Germans into history, probably because on account of"
33111 "their poverty their monogamy had not yet fully outgrown the pairing"
33111 "family. Three facts mentioned by Tacitus favor this conclusion: In the"
33111 "first place, although marriage was held very sacred--\"they are satisfied"
33111 "with one wife, the women are protected by chastity\"--still polygamy was"
33111 "in use among the distinguished and the leaders of the tribes, as was the"
33111 "case in the pairing families of the American Indians. Secondly, the"
33111 "transition from maternal to paternal law could have taken place only a"
33111 "short while before, because the mother's brother--the next male relative"
33111 "in the gens by maternal law--was still considered almost a closer"
33111 "relative than the natural father, also in accordance with the standpoint"
33111 "of the American Indians. The latter furnished to Marx, according to his"
33111 "own testimony, the key to the comprehension of German primeval history."
33111 "And thirdly, the German women were highly respected and also influenced"
33111 "public affairs, a fact directly opposed to monogamic male supremacy. In"
33111 "all these things the Germans almost harmonize with the Spartans, who, as"
33111 "we saw, also had not fully overcome the pairing family. Hence in this"
33111 "respect an entirely new element succeeded to the world's supremacy with"
33111 "the Germans. The new monogamy now developing the ruins of the Roman"
33111 "world from the mixture of nations endowed male rule with a milder form"
33111 "and accorded to women a position that was at least outwardly far more"
33111 "respected and free than classical antiquity ever knew. Not until now"
33111 "was there a possibility of developing from monogamy--in it, by the side"
33111 "of it or against it, as the case might be--the highest ethical progress"
33111 "we owe to it: the modern individual sexlove, unknown to all previous"
33111 "ages."
33111 ""
33111 "This progress doubtless arose from the fact that the Germans still lived"
33111 "in the pairing family and inoculated monogamy as far as possible with"
33111 "the position of women corresponding to the former. It was in no way due"
33111 "to the legendary and wonderfully pure natural qualities of the Germans."
33111 "These qualities were limited to the simple fact that the pairing family"
33111 "indeed does not create the marked moral contrasts of monogamy. On the"
33111 "contrary, the Germans, especially those who wandered southeast among the"
33111 "nomadic nations of the Black Sea, had greatly degenerated morally."
33111 "Beside the equestrian tricks of the inhabitants of the steppe they had"
33111 "also acquired some very unnatural vices. This is expressly confirmed of"
33111 "the Thaifali by Ammianus and of the Heruli by Prokop."
33111 ""
33111 "Although monogamy was the only one of all known forms of the family in"
33111 "which modern sexlove could develop, this does not imply that it"
33111 "developed exclusively or even principally as mutual love of man and"
33111 "wife. The very nature of strict monogamy under man's rule excluded this."
33111 "Among all historically active, i. e., ruling, classes matrimony remained"
33111 "what it had been since the days of the pairing family--a conventional"
33111 "matter arranged by the parents. And the first historical form of sexlove"
33111 "as a passion, as an attribute of every human being (at least of the"
33111 "ruling classes), the specific character of the highest form of the"
33111 "sexual impulse, this first form, the love of the knights in the middle"
33111 "ages, was by no means matrimonial love, but quite the contrary. In its"
33111 "classic form, among the Provencals, it heads with full sails for"
33111 "adultery and their poets extol the latter. The flower of Provencal love"
33111 "poetry, the Albas, describe in glowing colors how the knight sleeps with"
33111 "his adored--the wife of another--while the watchman outside calls him at"
33111 "the first faint glow of the morning (alba) and enables him to escape"
33111 "unnoticed. The poems culminate in the parting scene. Likewise the"
33111 "Frenchmen of the north and also the honest Germans adopted this style of"
33111 "poetry and the manner of knightly love corresponding to it. Old Wolfram"
33111 "von Eschenbach has left us three wonderful \"day songs\" treating this"
33111 "same questionable subject, and I like them better than his three heroic"
33111 "epics."
33111 ""
33111 "Civil matrimony in our day is of two kinds. In Catholic countries, the"
33111 "parents provide a fitting spouse for their son as of old, and the"
33111 "natural consequence is the full development of the contradictions"
33111 "inherent to monogamy: voluptuous hetaerism on the man's part, voluptuous"
33111 "adultery of the woman. Probably the Catholic church has abolished"
33111 "divorce for the simple reason that it had come to the conclusion, there"
33111 "was as little help for adultery as for death. In Protestant countries,"
33111 "again, it is the custom to give the bourgeois son more or less liberty"
33111 "in choosing his mate. Hence a certain degree of love may be at the"
33111 "bottom of such a marriage and for the sake of propriety this is always"
33111 "assumed, quite in keeping with Protestant hypocrisy. In this case"
33111 "hetaerism is carried on less strenuously and adultery on the part of the"
33111 "woman is not so frequent. But as human beings remain under any form of"
33111 "marriage what they were before marrying, and as the citizens of"
33111 "Protestant countries are mostly philistines, this Protestant monogamy on"
33111 "the average of the best cases confines itself to the community of a"
33111 "leaden ennui, labeled wedded bliss. The best mirror of these two species"
33111 "of marriage is the novel, the French novel for the Catholic, the German"
33111 "novel for the Protestant brand. In both of these novels they \"get one"
33111 "another:\" in the German novel the man gets the girl, in the French novel"
33111 "the husband gets the horns. It does not always go without saying which"
33111 "of the two deserves the most pity. For this reason the tediousness of"
33111 "the German novels is abhorred as much by the French bourgeois as the"
33111 "\"immorality\" of the French novels by the German philistine. Of late,"
33111 "since Berlin became cosmopolitan, the German novel begins to treat"
33111 "somewhat timidly of the hetaerism and adultery that a long time ago"
33111 "became familiar features of that city."
33111 ""
33111 "In both cases the marriage is influenced by the class environment of the"
33111 "participants, and in this respect it always remains conventional. This"
33111 "conventionalism often enough results in the most pronounced"
33111 "prostitution--sometimes of both parties, more commonly of the woman. She"
33111 "is distinguished from a courtisane only in that she does not offer her"
33111 "body for money by the hour like a commodity, but sells it into slavery"
33111 "for once and all. Fourier's words hold good with respect to all"
33111 "conventional marriages: \"As in grammar two negatives make one"
33111 "affirmative, so in matrimonial ethics, two prostitutions are considered"
33111 "as one virtue.\" Sexual love in man's relation to woman becomes and can"
33111 "become the rule among the oppressed classes alone, among the"
33111 "proletarians of our day--no matter whether this relation is officially"
33111 "sanctioned or not."
33111 ""
33111 "Here all the fundamental conditions of classic monogamy have been"
33111 "abolished. Here all property is missing and it was precisely for the"
33111 "protection and inheritance of this that monogamy and man rule were"
33111 "established. Hence all incentive to make this rule felt is wanting here."
33111 "More still, the funds are missing. Civil law protecting male rule"
33111 "applies only to the possessing classes and their intercourse with"
33111 "proletarians. Law is expensive and therefore the poverty of the laborer"
33111 "makes it meaningless for his relation to his wife. Entirely different"
33111 "personal and social conditions decide in this case. And finally, since"
33111 "the great industries have removed women from the home to the labor"
33111 "market and to the factory, the last remnant of man rule in the"
33111 "proletarian home has lost its ground--except, perhaps, a part of the"
33111 "brutality against women that has become general since the advent of"
33111 "monogamy. Thus the family of the proletarian is no longer strictly"
33111 "monogamous, even with all the most passionate love and the most"
33111 "unalterable loyalty of both parties, and in spite of any possible"
33111 "clerical or secular sanction. Consequently the eternal companions of"
33111 "monogamy, hetaerism and adultery, play an almost insignificant role"
33111 "here. The woman has practically regained the right of separation, and if"
33111 "a couple cannot agree, they rather separate. In short, the proletarian"
33111 "marriage is monogamous in the etymological sense of the word, but by no"
33111 "means in a historical sense."
33111 ""
33111 "True, our jurists hold that the progress of legislation continually"
33111 "lessens all cause of complaint for women. The modern systems of civil"
33111 "law recognize, first that marriage, in order to be legal, must be a"
33111 "contract based on voluntary consent of both parties, and secondly that"
33111 "during marriage the relations of both parties shall be founded on equal"
33111 "rights and duties. These two demands logically enforced will, so they"
33111 "claim, give to women everything they could possibly ask."
33111 ""
33111 "This genuinely juridical argumentation is exactly the same as that used"
33111 "by the radical republican bourgeois to cut short and dismiss the"
33111 "proletarian. The labor contract is said to be voluntarily made by both"
33111 "parties. But it is considered as voluntary when the law places both"
33111 "parties on equal terms on paper. The power conferred on one party by the"
33111 "division of classes, the pressure thereby exerted on the other party,"
33111 "the actual economic relation of the two--all this does not concern the"
33111 "law. Again, during the term of the contract both parties are held to"
33111 "have equal rights, unless one has expressly renounced his right. That"
33111 "the economic situation forces the laborer to give up even the last"
33111 "semblance of equality, that is not the fault of the law."
33111 ""
33111 "In regard to marriage, even the most advanced law is completely"
33111 "satisfied after both parties have formally declared their willingness."
33111 "What passes behind the juridical scenes where the actual process of"
33111 "living is going on, and how this willingness is brought about, that"
33111 "cannot be the business of the law and the jurist. Yet the simplest legal"
33111 "comparison should show to the jurist what this willingness really means."
33111 "In those countries where a legitimate portion of the parental wealth is"
33111 "assured to children and where these cannot be disinherited--in Germany,"
33111 "in countries with French law, etc.--the children are bound to secure the"
33111 "consent of their parents for marrying. In countries with English law,"
33111 "where the consent of the parents is by no means a legal qualification of"
33111 "marriage, the parents have full liberty to bequeath their wealth to"
33111 "anyone and may disinherit their children at will. Hence it is clear that"
33111 "among classes having any property to bequeath the freedom to marry is"
33111 "not a particle greater in England and America than in France and"
33111 "Germany."
33111 ""
33111 "The legal equality of man and woman in marriage is by no means better"
33111 "founded. Their legal inequality inherited from earlier stages of society"
33111 "is not the cause, but the effect of the economic oppression of women. In"
33111 "the ancient communistic household comprising many married couples and"
33111 "their children, the administration of the household entrusted to women"
33111 "was just as much a public function, a socially necessary industry, as"
33111 "the procuring of food by men. In the patriarchal and still more in the"
33111 "monogamous family this was changed. The administration of the household"
33111 "lost its public character. It was no longer a concern of society. It"
33111 "became a private service. The woman became the first servant of the"
33111 "house, excluded from participation in social production. Only by the"
33111 "great industries of our time the access to social production was again"
33111 "opened for women--for proletarian women alone, however. This is done in"
33111 "such a manner that they remain excluded from public production and"
33111 "cannot earn anything, if they fulfill their duties in the private"
33111 "service of the family; or that they are unable to attend to their family"
33111 "duties, if they wish to participate in public industries and earn a"
33111 "living independently. As in the factory, so women are situated in all"
33111 "business departments up to the medical and legal professions. The modern"
33111 "monogamous family is founded on the open or disguised domestic slavery"
33111 "of women, and modern society is a mass composed of molecules in the form"
33111 "of monogamous families. In the great majority of cases the man has to"
33111 "earn a living and to support his family, at least among the possessing"
33111 "classes. He thereby obtains a superior position that has no need of any"
33111 "legal special privilege. In the family, he is the bourgeois, the woman"
33111 "represents the proletariat. In the industrial world, however, the"
33111 "specific character of the economic oppression weighing on the"
33111 "proletariat appears in its sharpest outlines only after all special"
33111 "privileges of the capitalist class are abolished and the full legal"
33111 "equality of both classes is established. A democratic republic does not"
33111 "abolish the distinction between the two classes. On the contrary, it"
33111 "offers the battleground on which this distinction can be fought out."
33111 "Likewise the peculiar character of man's rule over woman in the modern"
33111 "family, the necessity and the manner of accomplishing the real social"
33111 "equality of the two, will appear in broad daylight only then, when both"
33111 "of them will enjoy complete legal equality. It will then be seen that"
33111 "the emancipation of women is primarily dependent on the re-introduction"
33111 "of the whole female sex into the public industries. To accomplish this,"
33111 "the monogamous family must cease to be the industrial unit of society."
33111 ""
33111 "       *       *       *       *       *"
33111 ""
33111 "We have, then, three main forms of the family, corresponding in general"
33111 "to the three main stages of human development. For savagery group"
33111 "marriage, for barbarism the pairing family, for civilization monogamy"
33111 "supplemented by adultery and prostitution. Between the pairing family"
33111 "and monogamy, in the higher stage of barbarism, the rule of men over"
33111 "female slaves and polygamy is inserted."
33111 ""
33111 "As we proved by our whole argument, the progress visible in this chain"
33111 "of phenomena is connected with the peculiarity of more and more"
33111 "curtailing the sexual freedom of the group marriage for women, but not"
33111 "for men. And group marriage is actually practised by men to this day."
33111 "What is considered a crime for women and entails grave legal and social"
33111 "consequences for them, is considered honorable for men or in the worst"
33111 "case a slight moral blemish born with pleasure. But the more traditional"
33111 "hetaerism is changed in our day by capitalistic production and conforms"
33111 "to it, the more hetaerism is transformed into undisguised prostitution,"
33111 "the more demoralizing are its effects. And it demoralizes men far more"
33111 "than women. Prostitution does not degrade the whole female sex, but only"
33111 "the luckless women that become its victims, and even those not to the"
33111 "extent generally assumed. But it degrades the character of the entire"
33111 "male world. Especially a long engagement is in nine cases out of ten a"
33111 "perfect training school of adultery."
33111 ""
33111 "We are now approaching a social revolution, in which the old economic"
33111 "foundations of monogamy will disappear just as surely as those of its"
33111 "complement, prostitution. Monogamy arose through the concentration of"
33111 "considerable wealth in one hand--a man's hand--and from the endeavor to"
33111 "bequeath this wealth to the children of this man to the exclusion of all"
33111 "others. This necessitated monogamy on the woman's, but not on the man's"
33111 "part. Hence this monogamy of women in no way hindered open or secret"
33111 "polygamy of men. Now, the impending social revolution will reduce this"
33111 "whole care of inheritance to a minimum by changing at least the"
33111 "overwhelming part of permanent and inheritable wealth--the means of"
33111 "production--into social property. Since monogamy was caused by economic"
33111 "conditions, will it disappear when these causes are abolished?"
33111 ""
33111 "One might reply, not without reason: not only will it not disappear, but"
33111 "it will rather be perfectly realized. For with the transformation of the"
33111 "means of production into collective property, wage labor will also"
33111 "disappear, and with it the proletariat and the necessity for a certain,"
33111 "statistically ascertainable number of women to surrender for money."
33111 "Prostitution disappears and monogamy, instead of going out of existence,"
33111 "at last becomes a reality--for men also."
33111 ""
33111 "At all events, the situation will be very much changed for men. But also"
33111 "that of women, and of all women, will be considerably altered. With the"
33111 "transformation of the means of production into collective property the"
33111 "monogamous family ceases to be the economic unit of society. The private"
33111 "household changes to a social industry. The care and education of"
33111 "children becomes a public matter. Society cares equally well for all"
33111 "children, legal or illegal. This removes the care about the"
33111 "\"consequences\" which now forms the essential social factor--moral and"
33111 "economic--hindering a girl to surrender unconditionally to the beloved"
33111 "man. Will not this be sufficient cause for a gradual rise of a more"
33111 "unconventional intercourse of the sexes and a more lenient public"
33111 "opinion regarding virgin honor and female shame? And finally, did we not"
33111 "see that in the modern world monogamy and prostitution, though"
33111 "antitheses, are inseparable and poles of the same social condition? Can"
33111 "prostitution disappear without engulfing at the same time monogamy?"
33111 ""
33111 "Here a new element becomes active, an element which at best existed only"
33111 "in the germ at the time when monogamy developed: individual sexlove."
33111 ""
33111 "Before the middle ages we cannot speak of individual sexlove. It goes"
33111 "without saying that personal beauty, intimate intercourse, harmony of"
33111 "inclinations, etc., awakened a longing for sexual intercourse in persons"
33111 "of different sex, and that it was not absolutely immaterial to men and"
33111 "women, with whom they entered into such most intimate intercourse. But"
33111 "from such a relation to our sexlove there is a long way yet. All through"
33111 "antiquity marriages were arranged for the participants by the parents,"
33111 "and the former quietly submitted. What little matrimonial love was known"
33111 "to antiquity was not subjective inclination, but objective duty; not"
33111 "cause, but corollary of marriage. Love affairs in a modern sense"
33111 "occurred in classical times only outside of official society. The"
33111 "shepherds whose happiness and woe in love is sung by Theocritos and"
33111 "Moschus, such as Daphnis and Chloe of Longos, all these were slaves who"
33111 "had no share in the state and in the daily sphere of the free citizen."
33111 "Outside of slave circles we find love affairs only as products of"
33111 "disintegration of the sinking old world. Their objects are women who"
33111 "also are standing outside of official society, hetaerae that are either"
33111 "foreigners or liberated slaves: in Athens since the beginning of its"
33111 "decline, in Rome at the time of the emperors. If love affairs really"
33111 "occurred between free male and female citizens, it was only in the form"
33111 "of adultery. And to the classical love poet of antiquity, the old"
33111 "Anakreon, sexlove in our sense was so immaterial, that he did not even"
33111 "care a fig for the sex of the beloved being."
33111 ""
33111 "Our sexlove is essentially different from the simple sexual craving, the"
33111 "Eros, of the ancients. In the first place it presupposes mutual love. In"
33111 "this respect woman is the equal of man, while in the antique Eros her"
33111 "permission is by no means always asked. In the second place our sexlove"
33111 "has such a degree of intensity and duration that in the eyes of both"
33111 "parties lack of possession and separation appear as a great, if not the"
33111 "greatest, calamity. In order to possess one another they play for high"
33111 "stakes, even to the point of risking their lives, a thing heard of only"
33111 "in adultery during the classical age. And finally a new moral standard"
33111 "is introduced for judging sexual intercourse. We not only ask: \"Was it"
33111 "legal or illegal?\" but also: \"Was it caused by mutual love or not?\" Of"
33111 "course, this new standard meets with no better fate in feudal or"
33111 "bourgeois practice than all other moral standards--it is simply ignored."
33111 "But neither does it fare worse. It is recognized just as much as the"
33111 "others--in theory, on paper. And that is all we can expect at present."
33111 ""
33111 "Where antiquity left off with its attempts at sexual love, there the"
33111 "middle ages resumed the thread: with adultery. We have already described"
33111 "the love of the knights that invented the day songs. From this love"
33111 "endeavoring to break through the bonds of marriage to the love destined"
33111 "to found marriage, there is a long distance which was never fully"
33111 "traversed by the knights. Even in passing on from the frivolous Romanic"
33111 "race to the virtuous Germans, we find in the Nibelungen song Kriemhild,"
33111 "who secretly is no less in love with Siegfried than he with her, meekly"
33111 "replying to Gunther's announcement that he has pledged her in troth to a"
33111 "certain knight whom he does not name: \"You need not beg for my consent;"
33111 "as you will demand, so I shall ever be; whomever you, sir, will select"
33111 "for my husband, I shall willingly take in troth.\" It does not enter her"
33111 "head at all that her love could find any consideration. Gunther asks for"
33111 "Brunhild, Etzel for Kriemhild without ever having seen one another. The"
33111 "same is true of the suit of Gutrun Sigebant of Ireland for the Norwegian"
33111 "Ute and of Hetel of Hegelingen for Hilda of Ireland. When Siegfried of"
33111 "Morland, Hartmut of Oranien and Herwig of Sealand court Gutrun, then it"
33111 "happens for the first time that the lady voluntarily decides, favoring"
33111 "the last named knight. As a rule the bride of the young prince is"
33111 "selected by his parents. Only when the latter are no longer alive, he"
33111 "chooses his own bride with the advice of the great feudal lords who in"
33111 "all cases of this kind have a decisive voice. Nor could it be otherwise."
33111 "For the knight and the baron as well as for the ruler of the realm"
33111 "himself, marriage is a political act, an opportunity for increasing"
33111 "their power by new federations. The interest of the house must decide,"
33111 "not the arbitrary inclination of the individual. How could love have a"
33111 "chance to decide the question of marriage in the last instance under"
33111 "such conditions?"
33111 ""
33111 "The same held good for the bourgeois of the medieval towns, the members"
33111 "of the guilds. Precisely the privileges protecting them, the clauses and"
33111 "restrictions of the guild charters, the artificial lines of division"
33111 "separating them legally, here from the other guilds, there from their"
33111 "journeymen and apprentices, drew a sufficiently narrow circle for the"
33111 "selection of a fitting bourgeois spouse. Under such a complicated"
33111 "system, the question of fitness was unconditionally decided, not by"
33111 "individual inclination, but by family interests."
33111 ""
33111 "In the overwhelming majority of cases the marriage contract thus"
33111 "remained to the end of the middle ages what it had been from the outset:"
33111 "a matter that was not decided by the parties most interested. In the"
33111 "beginning one was already married from his birth--married to a whole"
33111 "group of the other sex. In the later forms of group marriage, a similar"
33111 "relation was probably maintained, only under a continual narrowing of"
33111 "the group. In the pairing family it is the rule for mothers to exchange"
33111 "mutual pledges for the marriage of their children. Here also the main"
33111 "consideration is given to new ties of relationship that will strengthen"
33111 "the position of the young couple in the gens and the tribe. And when"
33111 "with the preponderance of private property over collective property and"
33111 "with the interest for inheritance paternal law and monogamy assumed the"
33111 "supremacy, then marriage became still more dependent on economic"
33111 "considerations. The form of purchase marriage disappears, but the"
33111 "essence of the transaction is more and more intensified, so that not"
33111 "only the woman, but also the man have a fixed price--not according to"
33111 "his qualities, but to his wealth. That mutual fondness of the marrying"
33111 "parties should be the one factor dominating all others had always been"
33111 "unheard of in the practice of the ruling classes. Such a thing occurred"
33111 "at best in romances or--among the oppressed classes that were not"
33111 "counted."
33111 ""
33111 "This was the situation encountered by capitalist production when it"
33111 "began to prepare, since the epoch of geographical discoveries, for the"
33111 "conquest of the world by international trade and manufacture. One would"
33111 "think that this mode of making the marriage contract would have been"
33111 "extremely acceptable to capitalism, and it was. And yet--the irony of"
33111 "fate is inexplicable--capitalist production had to make the decisive"
33111 "breach through this mode. By changing all things into commodities, it"
33111 "dissolved all inherited and traditional relations and replaced time"
33111 "hallowed custom and historical right by purchase and sale, by the \"free"
33111 "contract.\" And the English jurist, H. S. Maine, thought he had made a"
33111 "stupendous discovery by saying that our whole progress over former"
33111 "epochs consisted in arriving from status to contract, from inherited to"
33111 "voluntarily contracted conditions. So far as this is correct, it had"
33111 "already been mentioned in the Communist Manifesto."
33111 ""
33111 "But in order to make contracts, people must have full freedom over their"
33111 "persons, actions and possessions. They must furthermore be on terms of"
33111 "mutual equality. The creation of these \"free\" and \"equal\" people was"
33111 "precisely one of the main functions of capitalistic production. What"
33111 "though this was done at first in a half-conscious way and, moreover, in"
33111 "a religious disguise? Since the Lutheran and Calvinist reformation the"
33111 "thesis was accepted that a human being is fully responsible for his"
33111 "actions only then, when these actions were due to full freedom of will."
33111 "And it was held to be a moral duty to resist any compulsion for an"
33111 "immoral action. How did this agree with the prevailing practice of"
33111 "match-making? Marriage according to bourgeois conception was a contract,"
33111 "a legal business affair, and the most important one at that, because it"
33111 "decided the weal and woe of body and spirit of two beings for life. At"
33111 "that time the agreement was formally voluntary; without the consent of"
33111 "the contracting parties nothing could be done. But it was only too well"
33111 "known how this consent was obtained and who were really the contracting"
33111 "parties. If, however, perfect freedom of decision is demanded for all"
33111 "other contracts, why not for this one? Did not the two young people who"
33111 "were to be coupled together have the right freely to dispose of"
33111 "themselves, of their bodies and the organs of these? Had not sexual love"
33111 "become the custom through the knights and was not, in opposition to"
33111 "knightly adultery, the love of married couples its proper bourgeois"
33111 "form? And if it was the duty of married couples to love one another, was"
33111 "it not just as much the duty of lovers to marry each other and nobody"
33111 "else? Stood not the right of lovers higher than the right of parents,"
33111 "relatives and other customary marriage brokers and matrimonial agents?"
33111 "If the right of free personal investigation made its way unchecked into"
33111 "the church and religion, how could it bear with the insupportable claims"
33111 "of the older generation on the body, soul, property, happiness and"
33111 "misfortune of the younger generation?"
33111 ""
33111 "These questions had to be raised at a time when all the old ties of"
33111 "society were loosened and all traditional conceptions tottering. The"
33111 "size of the world had increased tenfold at a bound. Instead of one"
33111 "quadrant of one hemisphere, the whole globe now spread before the eyes"
33111 "of West Europeans who hastened to take possession of the other seven"
33111 "quadrants. And the thousand-year-old barriers of conventional medieval"
33111 "thought fell like the old narrow obstacles to marriage. An infinitely"
33111 "wider horizon opened out before the outer and inner eyes of humanity."
33111 "What mattered the well-meaning propriety, what the honorable privilege"
33111 "of the guild overcome through generations to the young man tempted by"
33111 "the gold and silver mines of Mexico and Potosi?"
33111 ""
33111 "It was the knight errant time of the bourgeoisie. It had its own"
33111 "romances and love dreams, but on a bourgeois footing and, in the last"
33111 "instance, with bourgeois aims."
33111 ""
33111 "Thus it came about that the rising bourgeoisie more and more recognized"
33111 "the freedom of contracting in marriage and carried it through in the"
33111 "manner described above, especially in Protestant countries, where"
33111 "existing institutions were most strongly shaken. Marriage remained class"
33111 "marriage, but within the class a certain freedom of choice was accorded"
33111 "to the contracting parties. And on paper, in moral theory as in poetical"
33111 "description, nothing was more unalterably established than the idea that"
33111 "every marriage was immoral unless founded on mutual sex-love and"
33111 "perfectly free agreement of husband and wife. In short, the love match"
33111 "was proclaimed as a human right, not only as droit de l'homme--man's"
33111 "right--but also for once as droit de femme--woman's right."
33111 ""
33111 "However, this human right differed from all other so-called human rights"
33111 "in one respect. While in practice other rights remained the privileges"
33111 "of the ruling class, the bourgeoisie, and were directly or indirectly"
33111 "curtailed for proletarians, the irony of history once more asserted"
33111 "itself in this case. The ruling class remains subject to well-known"
33111 "economic influences and, therefore, shows marriage by free selection"
33111 "only in exceptional cases. But among the oppressed class, love matches"
33111 "are the rule, as we have seen."
33111 ""
33111 "Hence the full freedom of marriage can become general only after all"
33111 "minor economic considerations, that still exert such a powerful"
33111 "influence on the choice of a mate for life, have been removed by the"
33111 "abolition of capitalistic production and of the property relations"
33111 "created by it. Then no other motive will remain but mutual fondness."
33111 ""
33111 "Since sexlove is exclusive by its very nature--although this"
33111 "exclusiveness is at present realized for women alone--marriage founded"
33111 "on sexlove must be monogamous. We have seen that Bachofen was perfectly"
33111 "right in regarding the progress from group marriage to monogamy mainly"
33111 "as the work of women. Only the advance from the pairing family to"
33111 "monogamy must be charged to the account of men. This advance implied,"
33111 "historically, a deterioration in the position of women and a greater"
33111 "opportunity for men to be faithless. Remove the economic considerations"
33111 "that now force women to submit to the customary disloyalty of men, and"
33111 "you will place women on a equal footing with men. All present"
33111 "experiences prove that this will tend much more strongly to make men"
33111 "truly monogamous, than to make women polyandrous."
33111 ""
33111 "However, those peculiarities that were stamped upon the face of monogamy"
33111 "by its rise through property relations, will decidedly vanish, namely"
33111 "the supremacy of men and the indissolubility of marriage. The supremacy"
33111 "of man in marriage is simply the consequence of his economic superiority"
33111 "and will fall with the abolition of the latter."
33111 ""
33111 "The indissolubility of marriage is partly the consequence of economic"
33111 "conditions, under which monogamy arose, partly tradition from the time"
33111 "where the connection between this economic situation and monogamy, not"
33111 "yet clearly understood, was carried to extremes by religion. To-day, it"
33111 "has been perforated a thousand times. If marriage founded on love is"
33111 "alone moral, then it follows that marriage is moral only as long as love"
33111 "lasts. The duration of an attack of individual sexlove varies"
33111 "considerably according to individual disposition, especially in men. A"
33111 "positive cessation of fondness or its replacement by a new passionate"
33111 "love makes a separation a blessing for both parties and for society."
33111 "But humanity will be spared the useless wading through the mire of a"
33111 "divorce case."
33111 ""
33111 "What we may anticipate about the adjustment of sexual relations after"
33111 "the impending downfall of capitalist production is mainly of a negative"
33111 "nature and mostly confined to elements that will disappear. But what"
33111 "will be added? That will be decided after a new generation has come to"
33111 "maturity: a race of men who never in their lives have had any occasion"
33111 "for buying with money or other economic means of power the surrender of"
33111 "a woman; a race of women who have never had any occasion for"
33111 "surrendering to any man for any other reason but love, or for refusing"
33111 "to surrender to their lover from fear of economic consequences. Once"
33111 "such people are in the world, they will not give a moment's thought to"
33111 "what we to-day believe should be their course. They will follow their"
33111 "own practice and fashion their own public opinion about the individual"
33111 "practice of every person--only this and nothing more."
33111 ""
33111 "But let us return to Morgan from whom we moved away a considerable"
33111 "distance. The historical investigation of social institutions developed"
33111 "during the period of civilization exceeds the limits of his book. Hence"
33111 "the vicissitudes of monogamy during this epoch occupy him very briefly."
33111 "He also sees in the further development of the monogamous family a"
33111 "progress, an approach to perfect equality of the sexes, without"
33111 "considering this aim fully realized. But he says: \"When the fact is"
33111 "accepted that the family has passed through four successive forms, and"
33111 "is now in a fifth, the question at once arises whether this form can be"
33111 "permanent in the future. The only answer that can be given is that it"
33111 "must advance as society advances, and change as society changes, even as"
33111 "it has done in the past. It is the creature of the social system, and"
33111 "will reflect its culture. As the monogamian family has improved greatly"
33111 "since the commencement of civilization, and very sensibly in modern"
33111 "times, it is at least supposable that it is capable of still farther"
33111 "improvement until the equality of the sexes is attained. Should the"
33111 "monogamian family in the distant future fail to answer the requirements"
33111 "of society, assuming the continuous progress of civilization, it is"
33111 "impossible to predict the nature of its successor.\""
33111 ""

33111 ""
33111 "How little Bachofen understood what he had discovered, or rather"
33111 "guessed, is proved by the term \"hetaerism,\" which he applies to this"
33111 "primeval stage. Hetaerism designated among the Greeks an intercourse of"
33111 "men, single or living in monogamy, with unmarried women. It always"
33111 "presupposes the existence of a well defined form of marriage, outside of"
33111 "which this intercourse takes place, and includes the possibility of"
33111 "prostitution. In another sense this word was never used, and I use it in"
33111 "this sense with Morgan. Bachofen's very important discoveries are"
33111 "everywhere mystified in the extreme by his idea that the historical"
33111 "relations of man and wife have their source in the religious conceptions"
33111 "of a certain period, not in the economic conditions of life."
33111 ""

33111 "The female of the European cuckoo (cuculus canorus) keeps intercourse"
33111 "with several males in different districts during the same season. Still,"
33111 "this is far from the human polyandry, in which the men and one women all"
33111 "live together in the same place, the men mutually tolerating one"
33111 "another, which male cuckoos do not."

33111 "[12] Author's note."
33111 ""
33111 "In the spring of 1882, Marx expressed himself in the"
33111 "strongest terms on the total misrepresentation of primeval times by"
33111 "Wagner's Nibelungen text: \"Who ever heard of a brother embracing his"
33111 "sister as a bride?\" To these lascivious Wagnerian gods who in truly"
33111 "modern style are rendering their love quarrels more spicy by a little"
33111 "incest, Marx replies: \"In primeval times the sister was the wife and"
33111 "that was moral.\" (To the fourth edition.) A French friend and admirer of"
33111 "Wagner does not consent to this foot note, and remarks that even in the"
33111 "Oegisdrecka, the more ancient Edda on which Wagner built, Loki denounces"
33111 "Freya: \"Before the gods you embraced your own brother.\" This, he says,"
33111 "proves that marriage between brother and sister was interdicted even"
33111 "then. But the Oegisdrecka is the expression of a time when the belief in"
33111 "the old myths was totally shaken; it is a truly Lucian satire on the"
33111 "gods. If Loki as Mephisto denounces Freya in this manner, then it is"
33111 "rather a point against Wagner. Loki also says, a few verses further on,"
33111 "to Niordhr: \"With your sister you generated (such) a son\" (\"vidh systur"
33111 "thinni gatzu slikan mog\"). Niordhr is not an Asa, but a Vana, and says"
33111 "in the Ynglinga Saga that marriages between brothers and sisters are"
33111 "sanctioned in Vanaland, which is not the case among the Asas. This would"
33111 "indicate that the Vanas are older gods than the Asas. At any rate"
33111 "Niordhr lived on equal terms with the Asas, and the Oegisdrecka is thus"
33111 "rather a proof that at the time of the origin of the Norwegian mythology"
33111 "the marriage of brother and sister was not yet repulsive, at least not"
33111 "to the gods. In trying to excuse Wagner it might be better to quote"
33111 "Goethe instead of the Edda. This poet commits a similar error in his"
33111 "ballad of the god and the bajadere in regard to the religious surrender"
33111 "of women and approaches modern prostitution far too closely."
33111 ""
33111 "[13] There is no longer any doubt that the traces of unrestricted sexual"
33111 "intercourse, which Bachofen alleges to have found--called \"incestuous"
33111 "generation\" by him--are traceable to group marriage. If Bachofen"
33111 "considers those Punaluan marriages \"lawless,\" a man of that period would"
33111 "look upon most of our present marriages between near and remote cousins"
33111 "on the father's or mother's side as incestuous, being marriages between"
33111 "consanguineous relatives.--Marx."

33111 "Verwandschaftsorganizationen der Australneger,\" by Heinrich Cunow."
33111 "Stuttgart, Dietz Verlag, 1894."
33111 ""
33111 "[17] Translator's note."
33111 ""
33111 "Heinrich Cunow has given us the results of his most recent"
33111 "investigations in his \"Verwandschaftsorganisationen der Australneger.\""
33111 "He sums up his studies in these words: \"While Morgan and Fison regard"
33111 "the system of marriage classes as an original organization preceding the"
33111 "so-called Punaluan family, I have found that the class is indeed older"
33111 "than the gens, having its origin in the different strata of generations"
33111 "characteristic of the \"consanguine family\" of Morgan; but the present"
33111 "mode of classification in force among Kamilaroi, Kabi, Yuipera, etc.,"
33111 "cannot have arisen until a much later time, when the gentile institution"
33111 "had already grown out of the horde. This system of classification does"
33111 "not represent the first timid steps of evolution; it is not the most"
33111 "primitive of any known forms of social organization, but an intermediate"
33111 "form that takes shape together with the gentile society, a stage of"
33111 "transition to a pure gentile organization. In this stage, the generic"
33111 "classification in strata of different ages belonging to the so-called"
33111 "consanguine family runs parallel for a while with the gentile order...."
33111 ""
33111 "It would have been easy for me to quote the testimony of travelers and"
33111 "ethnologists in support of the conclusions drawn by me from the forms of"
33111 "relationship among Australian negroes. But I purposely refrain from"
33111 "doing this, with a few exceptions, first because I do not wish to write"
33111 "a general history of the primitive family, and, secondly, because I"
33111 "consider all references of this kind as very doubtful testimony, unless"
33111 "they are accompanied by an analysis of the entire organization. We"
33111 "frequently find analogies to the institutions of a lower stage in a high"
33111 "stage, and yet they are founded on radically different premises and"
33111 "causes. The evolution of the Australian aborigines shows that. Among the"
33111 "Australians of the lower stage, e. g., the hordes are endogamous, among"
33111 "those of the middle stage they are exogamous, and in the higher stage"
33111 "they are again endogamous. But while in the one instance the marriage in"
33111 "the horde is conditioned on the fact that the more remote relatives are"
33111 "not yet excluded from sexual intercourse, it is founded in the other"
33111 "case on the difference between local and sexual organization."
33111 "Furthermore, the marriage between daughter and father is permitted in"
33111 "the lower stage, and again in that higher stage, where the class"
33111 "organization of the Kamilaroi is on the verge of dissolution. But in"
33111 "both cases the circle of those who are regarded as fathers is entirely"
33111 "different. The character of an institution can only be perfectly"
33111 "understood, if we examine its connection with the entire organization,"
33111 "and, if possible, trace its metamorphoses in the preceding stages...."
33111 ""
33111 "The characteristic feature of the class system is that by the side of"
33111 "the gentile order, such as is found among the North American Indians,"
33111 "there is always another system of four marriage classes for the purpose"
33111 "of limiting sexual intercourse between certain groups of relatives."
33111 "Neither the phratry nor the gens of the Kamilaroi forms a distinct"
33111 "territorial community. Their members are scattered among different"
33111 "roving hordes, and they only meet occasionally, e. g., to celebrate a"
33111 "feast or dance...."
33111 ""
33111 "The origin of gentile systems out of Punaluan groups has never been"
33111 "proven, while we see among the Australian negroes that the classes are"
33111 "clearly and irrefutably in existence among the first traces of"
33111 "gentilism...."
33111 ""
33111 "The class system in its original form is a conclusive proof of Morgan's"
33111 "theory, that the first step in the formation of systems of relationship"
33111 "consisted in prohibiting sexual intercourse between parents and children"
33111 "(in a wider sense)...."
33111 ""
33111 "It has been often disputed that the Punaluan family ever existed outside"
33111 "of the Sandwich Islands. But the marriage institutions of certain"
33111 "Australian tribes named by me prove the contrary. The Pirrauru of the"
33111 "Dieyerie is absolutely identical with the Punalua of the Hawaiians; and"
33111 "these institutions were not described by travelers who rushed through"
33111 "the territories of those tribes without knowing their language, but by"
33111 "men who lived among them for decades and fully mastered their"
33111 "dialects...."
33111 ""
33111 "I have shown how far the class system corresponds to the Hawaiian"
33111 "system. It is and remains a fact, that it contains a long series of"
33111 "terms that cannot be explained by the relations in the so-called"
33111 "consanguine family, and the use of which creates confusion, if applied"
33111 "to this family. But that simply shows that Morgan was mistaken about the"
33111 "age and present structure of the Hawaiian system. It does not prove that"
33111 "it could not have grown on the basis assumed by him...."
33111 ""
33111 "If the opponents of Morgan dispute that the so-called consanguine family"
33111 "is based on blood kinship, they are right, unless we wish to assign an"
33111 "exceptional position to the Australian strata of generations. But if"
33111 "they go further and declare that the subsequent restrictions of"
33111 "inbreeding and the gentile order have arisen independently of"
33111 "relationships, they commit a far greater mistake than Morgan. They block"
33111 "their way to an understanding of subsequent organizations and force"
33111 "themselves to all sorts of queer assumptions that at once appear as the"
33111 "fruits of imagination, when compared with the actual institutions of"
33111 "primitive peoples."
33111 ""
33111 "This explanation of the phases of development of family institutions"
33111 "contradicts present day views on the matter. Since the scientific"
33111 "investigations of the last decade have demonstrated beyond doubt that"
33111 "the so-called patriarchal family was preceded by the matriarchal family,"
33111 "it has become the custom to regard descent by females as a natural"
33111 "institution belonging to the very first stages of development which is"
33111 "explained by the modes of existence and thought among savages. Paternity"
33111 "being a matter of speculation, maternity of actual observation, it is"
33111 "supposed to follow that descent by females was always recognized. But"
33111 "the development of the Australian systems of relationship shows that"
33111 "this is not true, at least not in regard to Australians. The fact cannot"
33111 "be disputed away, that we find female lineage among all those higher"
33111 "developed tribes that have progressed to the formation of gentile"
33111 "organizations, but male lineage among all those that have no gentile"
33111 "organizations or where these are only in process of formation. Not a"
33111 "single tribe has been discovered so far, where female lineage was not"
33111 "combined with gentile organization, and I doubt that any will ever be"
33111 "found.\""
33111 ""
33111 "[18] The History of Human Marriage, p. 28-29."
33111 ""
33111 "[19] Mutterrecht, p. xix."
33111 ""
33111 "[20] A Journey in Brazil. Boston and New York, 1886. Page 266."
33111 ""
33111 "[21] Bancroft, Native Races, I., 81."
33111 ""
33111 "[22] Ibidem, p. 584."
33111 ""
33111 "[23] Morgan, Ancient Society, p. 504."
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "<CHAPTER III."
33111 ""
33111 "THE IROQUOIS GENS.>"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "We now come to another discovery of Morgan that is at least as important"
33111 "as the reconstruction of the primeval form of the family from the"
33111 "systems of kinship. It is the proof that the sex organizations within"
33111 "the tribe of North American Indians, designated by animal names, are"
33111 "essentially identical with the genea of the Greeks and the gentes of the"
33111 "Romans; that the American form is the original from which the Greek and"
33111 "Roman forms were later derived; that the whole organization of Greek and"
33111 "Roman society during primeval times in gens, phratry and tribe finds its"
33111 "faithful parallel in that of the American Indians; that the gens is an"
33111 "institution common to all barbarians up to the time of civilization--at"
33111 "least so far as our present sources of information reach. This"
33111 "demonstration has cleared at a single stroke the most difficult passages"
33111 "of remotest ancient Greek and Roman history. At the same time it has"
33111 "given us unexpected information concerning the fundamental outlines of"
33111 "the constitution of society in primeval times--before the introduction"
33111 "of the state. Simple as the matter is after we have once found it out,"
33111 "still it was only lately discovered by Morgan. In his work of 1871 he"
33111 "had not yet unearthed this mystery. Its revelation has completely"
33111 "silenced for the time being those generally so overconfident English"
33111 "authorities on primeval history."
33111 ""
33111 "The Latin word gens, used by Morgan generally for the designation of"
33111 "this sex organization, is derived, like the equivalent Greek word"
33111 "genos, from the common Aryan root gan, signifying to beget. Gens, genos,"
