33111 "Sanskrit dschanas, Gothic kuni, ancient Norse and Anglesaxon kyn,"
33111 "English kin, Middle High German kuenne, all signify lineage, descent."
33111 "Gens in Latin, genos in Greek, specially designate that sex organization"
33111 "which boasted of common descent (from a common sire) and was united into"
33111 "a separate community by certain social and religious institutions, but"
33111 "the origin and nature of which nevertheless remained obscure to all our"
33111 "historians."
33111 ""
33111 "Elsewhere, in speaking of the Punaluan family, we saw how the gens was"
33111 "constituted in its original form. It consisted of all individuals who by"
33111 "means of the Punaluan marriage and in conformity with the conceptions"
33111 "necessarily arising in it made up the recognized offspring of a certain"
33111 "ancestral mother, the founder of that gens. Since fatherhood is"
33111 "uncertain in this form of the family, female lineage is alone valid. And"
33111 "as brothers must not marry their sisters, but only women of foreign"
33111 "descent, the children bred from these foreign women do not belong to the"
33111 "gens, according to maternal law. Hence only the offspring of the"
33111 "daughters of every generation remain in the same sex organization. The"
33111 "descendants of the sons are transferred to the gentes of the new"
33111 "mothers. What becomes of this group of kinship when it constitutes"
33111 "itself a separate group, distinct from similar groups in the same tribe?"
33111 ""
33111 "As the classical form of this original gens Morgan selects that of the"
33111 "Iroquois, more especially that of the Seneca tribe. This tribe has eight"
33111 "gentes named after animals: 1. Wolf. 2. Bear. 3. Turtle. 4. Beaver. 5."
33111 "Deer. 6. Snipe. 7. Heron. 8. Hawk. Every gens observes the following"
33111 "customs:"
33111 ""
33111 "1. The gens elects its sachem (official head during peace) and its chief"
33111 "(leader in war). The sachem must be selected within the gens and his"
33111 "office was in a sense hereditary. It had to be filled immediately after"
33111 "a vacancy occurred. The chief could be selected outside of the gens, and"
33111 "his office could even be temporarily vacant. The son never followed his"
33111 "father in the office of sachem, because the Iroquois observed maternal"
33111 "law, in consequence of which the son belonged to another gens. But the"
33111 "brother or the son of a sister was often elected as a successor. Men and"
33111 "women both voted in elections. The election, however, had to be"
33111 "confirmed by the other seven gentes, and then only the sachem-elect was"
33111 "solemnly invested, by the common council of the whole Iroquois"
33111 "federation. The significance of this will be seen later. The power of"
33111 "the sachem within the tribe was of a paternal, purely moral nature. He"
33111 "had no means of coercion at his command. He was besides by virtue of his"
33111 "office a member of the tribal council of the Senecas and of the federal"
33111 "council of the whole Iroquois nation. The Chief had the right to command"
33111 "only in times of war."
33111 ""
33111 "2. The gens can retire the sachem and the chief at will. This again is"
33111 "done by men and women jointly. The retired men are considered simple"
33111 "warriors and private persons like all others. The tribal council, by the"
33111 "way, can also retire the sachems, even against the will of the tribe."
33111 ""
33111 "3. No member is permitted to marry within the gens. This is the"
33111 "fundamental rule of the gens, the tie that holds it together. It is the"
33111 "negative expression of the very positive blood relationship, by virtue"
33111 "of which the individuals belonging to it become a gens. By the discovery"
33111 "of this simple fact Morgan for the first time revealed the nature of the"
33111 "gens. How little the gens had been understood before him is proven by"
33111 "former reports on savages and barbarians, in which the different"
33111 "organizations of which the gentile order is composed are jumbled"
33111 "together without understanding and distinction as tribe, clan, thum,"
33111 "etc. Sometimes it is stated that intermarrying within these"
33111 "organizations is forbidden. This gave rise to the hopeless confusion, in"
33111 "which McLennan could pose as Napoleon and establish order by the decree:"
33111 "All tribes are divided into those that forbid intermarrying (exogamous)"
33111 "and those that permit it (endogamous). And after he had thus made"
33111 "confusion worse confounded, he could indulge in deep meditations which"
33111 "of his two preposterous classes was the older: exogamy or endogamy. By"
33111 "the discovery of the gens founded on affinity of blood and the resulting"
33111 "impossibility of its members to intermarry, this nonsense found a"
33111 "natural end. It is self understood that the marriage interdict within"
33111 "the gens was strictly observed at the stage in which we find the"
33111 "Iroquois."
33111 ""
33111 "4. The property of deceased members fell to the share of the other"
33111 "gentiles; it had to remain in the gens. In view of the insignificance of"
33111 "the objects an Iroquois could leave behind, the nearest gentile"
33111 "relations divided the heritage. Was the deceased a man, then his natural"
33111 "brothers, sisters and the brothers of the mother shared in his property."
33111 "Was it a woman, then her children and natural sisters shared, but not"
33111 "her brothers. For this reason husband and wife could not inherit from"
33111 "one another, nor the children from the father."
33111 ""
33111 "5. The gentile members owed to each other help, protection and"
33111 "especially assistance in revenging injury inflicted by strangers. The"
33111 "individual relied for his protection on the gens and could be assured of"
33111 "it. Whoever injured the individual, injured the whole gens. From this"
33111 "blood kinship arose the obligation to blood revenge that was"
33111 "unconditionally recognized by the Iroquois. If a stranger killed a"
33111 "gentile member, the whole gens of the slain man was pledged to revenge"
33111 "his death. First mediation was tried. The gens of the slayer deliberated"
33111 "and offered to the gentile council of the slain propositions for"
33111 "atonement, consisting generally in expressions of regret and presents of"
33111 "considerable value. If these were accepted, the matter was settled. In"
33111 "the opposite case the injured gens appointed one or more avengers who"
33111 "were obliged to pursue the slayer and to kill him. If they succeeded,"
33111 "the gens of the slayer had no right to complain. The account was"
33111 "squared."
33111 ""
33111 "6. The gens had certain distinct names or series of names, which no"
33111 "other gens in the whole tribe could use, so that the name of the"
33111 "individual indicated to what gens he belonged. A gentile name at the"
33111 "same time bestowed gentile rights."
33111 ""
33111 "7. The gens may adopt strangers who thereby are adopted into the whole"
33111 "tribe. The prisoners of war who were not killed became by adoption into"
33111 "a gens tribal members of the Senecas and thus received full gentile and"
33111 "tribal rights. The adoption took place on the motion of some gentile"
33111 "members, of men who accepted the stranger as a brother or sister, of"
33111 "women who accepted him as a child. The solemn introduction into the gens"
33111 "was necessary to confirm the adoption. Frequently certain gentes that"
33111 "had shrunk exceptionally were thus strengthened by mass adoptions from"
33111 "another gens with the consent of the latter. Among the Iroquois the"
33111 "solemn introduction into the gens took place in a public meeting of the"
33111 "tribal council, whereby it actually became a religious ceremony."
33111 ""
33111 "The existence of special religious celebrations among Indian gentes can"
33111 "hardly be demonstrated. But the religious rites of the Indians are more"
33111 "or less connected with the gens. At the six annual religious festivals"
33111 "of the Iroquois the sachems and chiefs of the different gentes were"
33111 "added to the \"Keepers of the Faith\" and had the functions of priests."
33111 ""
33111 "9. The gens had a common burial place. Among the Iroquois of the State"
33111 "of New York, who are crowded by white men all around them, the burial"
33111 "place has disappeared, but it existed formerly. Among other Indians it"
33111 "is still in existence, e. g., among the Tuscaroras, near relatives of"
33111 "the Iroquois, where every gens has a row by itself in the burial place,"
33111 "although they are Christians. The mother is buried in the same row as"
33111 "her children, but not the father. And among the Iroquois the whole gens"
33111 "of the deceased attends the funeral, prepares the grave and provides the"
33111 "addresses, etc."
33111 ""
33111 "10. The gens had a council, the democratic assembly of all male and"
33111 "female gentiles of adult age, all with equal suffrage. This council"
33111 "elected and deposed its sachems and chiefs; likewise the other \"Keepers"
33111 "of the Faith.\" It deliberated on gifts of atonement or blood revenge for"
33111 "murdered gentiles and it adopted strangers into the gens. In short, it"
33111 "was the sovereign power in the gens."
33111 ""
33111 "The following are the rights and privileges of the typical Indian gens,"
33111 "according to Morgan: \"All the members of an Iroquois gens were"
33111 "personally free, and they were bound to defend each other's freedom;"
33111 "they were equal in privileges and in personal rights, the sachems and"
33111 "chiefs claiming no superiority; and they were a brotherhood bound"
33111 "together by ties of kin. Liberty, equality and fraternity, though never"
33111 "formulated, were cardinal principles of the gens. These facts are"
33111 "material, because the gens was the unit of a social and governmental"
33111 "system, the foundation upon which Indian society was organized. A"
33111 "structure composed of such units would of necessity bear the impress of"
33111 "their character, for as the unit, so the compound. It serves to explain"
33111 "that sense of independence and personal dignity universally an"
33111 "attribute of Indian character.\""
33111 ""
33111 "At the time of the discovery the Indians of entire North America were"
33111 "organized in gentes by maternal law. Only \"in some tribes, as among the"
33111 "Dakotas, the gentes had fallen out; in others as among the Ojibwas, the"
33111 "Omahas and the Mayas of Yucatan, descent had been changed from the"
33111 "female to the male line.\""
33111 ""
33111 "Among many Indian tribes with more than five or six gentes we find"
33111 "three, four or more gentes united into a separate group, called phratry"
33111 "by Morgan in accurate translation of the Indian name by its Greek"
33111 "equivalent. Thus the Senecas have two phratries, the first comprising"
33111 "gentes one to four, the second gentes five to eight. Closer"
33111 "investigation shows that these phratries generally represent the"
33111 "original gentes that formed the tribe in the beginning. For the marriage"
33111 "interdict necessitated the existence of at least two gentes in a tribe"
33111 "in order to realize its separate existence. As the tribe increased,"
33111 "every gens segmented into two or more new gentes, while the original"
33111 "gens comprising all the daughter gentes, lived on in the phratry. Among"
33111 "the Senecas and most of the other Indians \"the gentes in the same"
33111 "phratry are brother gentes to each other, and cousin gentes to those of"
33111 "the other phratry\"--terms that have a very real and expressive meaning"
33111 "in the American system of kinship, as we have seen. Originally no Seneca"
33111 "was allowed to marry within his phratry, but this custom has long become"
33111 "obsolete and is now confined to the gens. According to the tradition"
33111 "among the Senecas, the bear and the deer were the two original gentes,"
33111 "from which the others were formed by segmentation. After this new"
33111 "institution had become well established it was modified according to"
33111 "circumstances. If certain gentes became extinct, it sometimes happened"
33111 "that by mutual consent the members of one gens were transferred in a"
33111 "body from other phratries. Hence we find the gentes of the same name"
33111 "differently grouped in the phratries of the different tribes."
33111 ""
33111 "\"The phratry, among the Iroquois, was partly for social and partly for"
33111 "religious objects.\" 1. In the ball game one phratry plays against"
33111 "another. Each one sends its best players, the other members, upon"
33111 "different sides of the field, watch the game and bet against one another"
33111 "on the result. 2. In the tribal council the sachems and chiefs of each"
33111 "phratry are seated opposite one another, every speaker addressing the"
33111 "representatives of each phratry as separate bodies. 3. When a murder had"
33111 "been committed in the tribe, the slayer and the slain belonging to"
33111 "different phratries, the injured gens often appealed to its brother"
33111 "gentes. These held a phratry council which in a body addressed itself to"
33111 "the other phratry, in order to prevail on the latter to assemble in"
33111 "council and effect a condonation of the matter. In this case the phratry"
33111 "re-appears in its original gentile capacity, and with a better prospect"
33111 "of success than the weaker gens, its daughter. 4. At the funeral of"
33111 "prominent persons the opposite phratry prepared the interment and the"
33111 "burial rites, while the phratry of the deceased attended the funeral as"
33111 "mourners. If a sachem died, the opposite phratry notified the central"
33111 "council of the Iroquois that the office of the deceased had become"
33111 "vacant. 5. In electing a sachem the phratry council also came into"
33111 "action. Endorsement by the brother gentes was generally considered a"
33111 "matter of fact, but the gentes of the other phratry might oppose. In"
33111 "such a case the council of this phratry met, and if it maintained its"
33111 "opposition, the election was null and void. 6. Formerly the Iroquois had"
33111 "special religious mysteries, called medicine lodges by the white men."
33111 "These mysteries were celebrated among the Senecas by two religious"
33111 "societies that had a special form of initiation for new members; each"
33111 "phratry was represented by one of these societies. 7. If, as is almost"
33111 "certain, the four lineages occupying the four quarters of Tlascala at"
33111 "the time of the conquest were four phratries, then it is proved that the"
33111 "phratries were at the same time military units, as were the Greek"
33111 "phratries and similar sex organizations of the Germans. Each of these"
33111 "four lineages went into battle as a separate group with its special"
33111 "uniform and flag and its own leader."
33111 ""
33111 "Just as several genres form a phratry so in the classical form several"
33111 "phratries form a tribe. In some cases the middle group, the phratry, is"
33111 "missing in strongly decimated tribes."
33111 ""
33111 "What constitutes an Indian tribe in America? 1. A distinct territory and"
33111 "a distinct name. Every tribe had a considerable hunting and fishing"
33111 "ground beside the place of its actual settlement. Beyond this territory"
33111 "there was a wide neutral strip of land reaching over to the boundaries"
33111 "of the next tribe; a smaller strip between tribes of related languages,"
33111 "a larger between tribes of foreign languages. This corresponds to the"
33111 "boundary forest of the Germans, the desert created by Caesar's Suevi"
33111 "around their territory, the isarnholt (Danish jarnved, Latin limei"
33111 "Danicus) between Danes and Germans, the sachsen wald (Saxon forest) and"
33111 "the Slavish branibor between Slavs and Germans giving the province of"
33111 "Brandenburg its name. The territory thus surrounded by neutral ground"
33111 "was the collective property of a certain tribe, recognized as such by"
33111 "other tribes and defended against the invasion of others. The"
33111 "disadvantage of undefined boundaries became of practical importance"
33111 "only after the population had increased considerably."
33111 ""
33111 "The tribal names generally seem to be more the result of chance than of"
33111 "intentional selection. In course of time it frequently happened that a"
33111 "tribe designated a neighboring tribe by another name than that chosen by"
33111 "itself. In this manner the Germans received their first historical name"
33111 "from the Celts."
33111 ""
33111 "2. A distinct dialect peculiar to this tribe. As a matter of fact the"
33111 "tribe and the dialect are co-extensive. In America, the formation of new"
33111 "tribes and dialects by segmentation was in progress until quite"
33111 "recently, and doubtless it is still going on. Where two weak tribes"
33111 "amalgamated into one, there it exceptionally happened that two closely"
33111 "related dialects were simultaneously spoken in the same tribe. The"
33111 "average strength of American tribes is less than 2,000 members. The"
33111 "Cherokees, however, number about 26,000, the greatest number of Indians"
33111 "in the United States speaking the same dialect."
33111 ""
33111 "3. The right to solemnly invest the sachems and chiefs elected by the"
33111 "gentes, and"
33111 ""
33111 "4. The right to depose them, even against the will of the gens. As these"
33111 "sachems and chiefs are members of the tribal council, these rights of"
33111 "the tribe explain themselves. Where a league of tribes had been formed"
33111 "and all the tribes were represented in a feudal council, the latter"
33111 "exercised these rights."
33111 ""
33111 "5. The possession of common religious conceptions (mythology) and rites."
33111 "\"After the fashion of barbarians the American Indians were a religious"
33111 "people.\" Their mythology has not yet been critically investigated. They"
33111 "materialized their religious conceptions--spirits of all sorts--in human"
33111 "shapes, but the lower stage of barbarism in which they lived, knows"
33111 "nothing as yet of so-called idols. It is a cult of nature and of the"
33111 "elements, in process of evolution to pantheism. The different tribes"
33111 "had regular festivals with prescribed forms of worship, mainly dances"
33111 "and games. Especially dancing was an essential part of all religious"
33111 "celebrations. Every tribe celebrated by itself."
33111 ""
33111 "6. A tribal council for public affairs. It was composed of all the"
33111 "sachems and chiefs of the different gentes, real representatives because"
33111 "they could be deposed at any moment. It deliberated in public,"
33111 "surrounded by the rest of the tribal members, who had a right to take"
33111 "part in the discussions and claim attention. The council decided. As a"
33111 "rule any one present gained a hearing on his demand. The women could"
33111 "also present their views by a speaker of their choice. Among the"
33111 "Iroquois the final resolution had to be passed unanimously, as was also"
33111 "the case in some resolutions of German mark (border) communities. It was"
33111 "the special duty of the tribal council to regulate the relations with"
33111 "foreign tribes. The council received and despatched legations, declared"
33111 "war and made peace. War was carried on principally by volunteers."
33111 "\"Theoretically, each tribe was at war with every other tribe with which"
33111 "it had not formed a treaty of peace.\""
33111 ""
33111 "Expeditions against such enemies were generally organized by certain"
33111 "prominent warriors. They started a war dance, and whoever took part in"
33111 "it thereby declared his intention to join the expedition. Ranks were"
33111 "formed and the march began immediately. The defense of the attacked"
33111 "tribal territory was also generally carried on by volunteers. The exodus"
33111 "and the return of such columns was always the occasion of public"
33111 "festivities. The consent of the tribal council for such expeditions was"
33111 "not required, and was neither asked nor given. This corresponds to the"
33111 "private war expeditions of German followers described by Tacitus. Only"
33111 "these German groups of followers had already assumed a more permanent"
33111 "character, forming a standing center organized during peace, around"
33111 "which the other volunteers gathered in case of war. Such war columns"
33111 "were rarely strong in numbers. The most important expeditions of the"
33111 "Indians, even for long distances, were undertaken by insignificant"
33111 "forces. If more than one group joined for a great expedition, every"
33111 "group obeyed its own leader. The uniformity of the campaign plan was"
33111 "secured as well as possible by a council of these leaders. This is the"
33111 "mode of warfare among the Allemani in the fourth century on the Upper"
33111 "Rhine, as described by Ammianus Marcellinus."
33111 ""
33111 "7. In some tribes we find a head chief, whose power, however, is"
33111 "limited. He is one of the sachems who has to take provisional measures"
33111 "in cases requiring immediate action, until the council can assemble and"
33111 "decide. He represents a feeble, but generally undeveloped prototype of"
33111 "an official with executive power. The latter, as we shall see, developed"
33111 "in most cases out of the highest war chief."
33111 ""
33111 "The great majority of American Indians did not go beyond the league of"
33111 "tribes. With a few tribes of small membership, separated by wide"
33111 "boundary tracts, weakened by unceasing warfare, they occupied an immense"
33111 "territory. Leagues were now and then formed by kindred tribes as the"
33111 "result of momentary necessity and dissolved again under more favorable"
33111 "conditions. But in certain districts, tribes of the same kin had again"
33111 "found their way out of disbandment into permanent federations, making"
33111 "the first step towards the formation of nations. In the United States we"
33111 "find the highest form of such a league among the Iroquois. Emigrating"
33111 "from their settlements west of the Mississippi, where they probably"
33111 "formed a branch of the great Dakota family, they settled at last after"
33111 "long wanderings in the present State of New York. They had five tribes:"
33111 "Senecas, Cayugas, Onondagas, Oneidas and Mohawks. They lived on fish,"
33111 "venison, and the products of rough gardening, inhabiting villages"
33111 "protected by stockades. Their number never exceeded 20,000, and certain"
33111 "gentes were common to all five tribes. They spoke closely related"
33111 "dialects of the same language and occupied territories contiguous to one"
33111 "another. As this land was won by conquest, it was natural for these"
33111 "tribes to stand together against the expelled former inhabitants. This"
33111 "led, not later than the beginning of the fifteenth century, to a regular"
33111 "\"eternal league,\" a sworn alliance that immediately assumed an"
33111 "aggressive character, relying on its newly won strength. About 1675, at"
33111 "the summit of its power, it had conquered large districts round about"
33111 "and partly expelled the inhabitants, partly made them tributary. The"
33111 "Iroquois League represented the most advanced social organization"
33111 "attained by Indians that had not passed the lower stage of barbarism."
33111 "This excludes only the Mexicans, New Mexicans and Peruvians."
33111 ""
33111 "The fundamental provisions of the league were:"
33111 ""
33111 "1. Eternal federation of the five consanguineous tribes on the basis of"
33111 "perfect equality and independence in all internal tribal matters. This"
33111 "consanguinity formed the true fundament of the league. Three of these"
33111 "tribes, called father tribes, were brothers to one another; the other"
33111 "two, also mutual brothers, were called son tribes. The three oldest"
33111 "gentes were represented by living members in all five tribes, and these"
33111 "members were all regarded as brothers. Three other gentes were still"
33111 "alive in three tribes, and all of their members called one another"
33111 "brothers. The common language, only modified by variations of dialect,"
33111 "was the expression and proof of their common descent."
33111 ""
33111 "2. The official organ of the league was a federal council of fifty"
33111 "sachems, all equal in rank and prominence. This council had the supreme"
33111 "decision in all federal matters."
33111 ""
33111 "3. On founding this league the fifty sachems had been assigned to the"
33111 "different tribes and gentes as holders of new offices created especially"
33111 "for federal purposes. Vacancies were filled by new elections in the"
33111 "gens, and the holders of these offices could be deposed at will. But the"
33111 "right of installation belonged to the federal council."
33111 ""
33111 "4. These federal sachems were at the same time sachems of their tribe"
33111 "and had a seat and a vote in the tribal council."
33111 ""
33111 "5. All decisions of the federal council had to be unanimous."
33111 ""
33111 "6. The votes were cast by tribes, so that every tribe and the council"
33111 "members of each tribe had to vote together in order to adopt a final"
33111 "resolution."
33111 ""
33111 "7. Any one of the five tribes could convoke the federal council, but the"
33111 "council could not convene itself."
33111 ""
33111 "8. Federal meetings were held publicly in the presence of the assembled"
33111 "people. Every Iroquois could have the word, but the final decision"
33111 "rested with the council."
33111 ""
33111 "9. The league had no official head, no executive chief."
33111 ""
33111 "10. It had, however, two high chiefs of war, both with equal functions"
33111 "and power (the two \"kings\" of Sparta, the two consuls of Rome)."
33111 ""
33111 "This was the whole constitution, under which the Iroquois lived over"
33111 "four hundred years and still live. I have described it more fully after"
33111 "Morgan, because we have here an opportunity for studying the"
33111 "organization of a society that does not yet know a state. The state"
33111 "presupposes a public power of coercion separated from the aggregate"
33111 "body of its members. Maurer, with correct intuition, recognized the"
33111 "constitution of the German Mark as a purely social institution,"
33111 "essentially different from that of a state, though furnishing the"
33111 "fundament on which a state constitution could be erected later on. Hence"
33111 "in all of his writings, he traced the gradual rise of the public power"
33111 "of coercion from and by the side of primordial constitutions of marks,"
33111 "villages, farms and towns. The North American Indians show how an"
33111 "originally united tribe gradually spreads over an immense continent; how"
33111 "tribes by segmentation become nations, whole groups of tribes; how"
33111 "languages change so that they not only become unintelligible to one"
33111 "another, but also lose every trace of former unity; how at the same time"
33111 "one gens splits up into several gentes, how the old mother gentes are"
33111 "preserved in the phratries and how the names of these oldest gentes"
33111 "still remain the same in widely distant and long separated tribes. Wolf"
33111 "and bear still are gentile names in a majority of all Indian tribes. And"
33111 "the above named constitution is essentially applicable to all of them,"
33111 "except that many did not reach the point of forming leagues of related"
33111 "tribes."
33111 ""
33111 "But once the gens was given as a social unit, we also see how the whole"
33111 "constitution of gentes, phratries and tribes developed with almost"
33111 "unavoidable necessity--because naturally--from the gens. All three of"
33111 "them are groups of differentiated consanguine relations. Each is"
33111 "complete in itself, arranges its own local affairs and supplements the"
33111 "other groups. And the cycle of functions performed by them includes the"
33111 "aggregate of the public affairs of men in the lower stage of barbarism."
33111 ""
33111 "Wherever we find the gens as the social unit of a nation, we are"
33111 "justified in searching for a tribal organization similar to the one"
33111 "described above. And whenever sufficient material is at hand, as in"
33111 "Greek and Roman history, there we shall not only find such an"
33111 "organization, but we may also be assured, that the comparison with the"
33111 "American sex organizations will assist us in solving the most perplexing"
33111 "doubts and riddles in places where the material forsakes us."
33111 ""
33111 "How wonderful this gentile constitution is in all its natural"
33111 "simplicity! No soldiers, gendarmes and policemen, no nobility, kings,"
33111 "regents, prefects or judges, no prisons, no lawsuits, and still affairs"
33111 "run smoothly. All quarrels and disputes are settled by the entire"
33111 "community involved in them, either the gens or the tribe or the various"
33111 "gentes among themselves. Only in very rare cases the blood revenge is"
33111 "threatened as an extreme measure. Our capital punishment is simply a"
33111 "civilized form of it, afflicted with all the advantages and drawbacks of"
33111 "civilization. Not a vestige of our cumbersome and intricate system of"
33111 "administration is needed, although there are more public affairs to be"
33111 "settled than nowadays: the communistic household is shared by a number"
33111 "of families, the land belongs to the tribe, only the gardens are"
33111 "temporarily assigned to the households. The parties involved in a"
33111 "question settle it and in most cases the hundred-year-old traditions"
33111 "have settled everything beforehand. There cannot be any poor and"
33111 "destitute--the communistic households and the gentes know their duties"
33111 "toward the aged, sick and disabled. All are free and equal--the women"
33111 "included. There is no room yet for slaves, nor for the subjugation of"
33111 "foreign tribes. When about 1651 the Iroquois had vanquished the Eries"
33111 "and the \"Neutral Nation,\" they offered to adopt them into the league on"
33111 "equal terms. Only when the vanquished declined this offer they were"
33111 "driven out of their territory."
33111 ""
33111 "What splendid men and women were produced by such a society! All the"
33111 "white men who came into contact with unspoiled Indians admired the"
33111 "personal dignity, straightforwardness, strength of character and bravery"
33111 "of these barbarians."
33111 ""
33111 "We lately received proofs of such bravery in Africa. A few years ago the"
33111 "Zulus, and some months ago the Nubians, both of which tribes still"
33111 "retain the gentile organization, did what no European army can do. Armed"
33111 "only with lances and spears, without any firearms, they advanced under a"
33111 "hail of bullets from breechloaders up to the bayonets of the English"
33111 "infantry--the best of the world for fighting in closed ranks--and threw"
33111 "them into confusion more than once, yea, even forced them to retreat in"
33111 "spite of the immense disparity of weapons, and in spite of the fact that"
33111 "they have no military service and don't know anything about drill. How"
33111 "enduring and able they are, is proved by the complaints of the English"
33111 "who admit that a Kaffir can cover a longer distance in twenty-four hours"
33111 "than a horse. The smallest muscle springs forth, hard and tough like a"
33111 "whiplash, says an English painter."
33111 ""
33111 "Such was human society and its members, before the division into classes"
33111 "had taken place. And a comparison of that social condition with the"
33111 "condition of the overwhelming majority of present day society shows the"
33111 "enormous chasm that separates our proletarian and small farmer from the"
33111 "free gentile of old."
33111 ""
33111 "That is one side of the question. We must not overlook, however, that"
33111 "this organization was doomed. It did not pass beyond the tribe. The"
33111 "league of tribes marked the beginning of its downfall, as we shall see,"
33111 "and as the attempts of the Iroquois at subjugating others showed."
33111 "Whatever went beyond the tribe, went outside of gentilism. Where no"
33111 "direct peace treaty existed, there war reigned from tribe to tribe. And"
33111 "this war was carried on with the particular cruelty that distinguishes"
33111 "man from other animals, and that was modified later on simply by"
33111 "self-interest."
33111 ""
33111 "The gentile constitution in its most flourishing time, such as we saw it"
33111 "in America, presupposed a very undeveloped state of production, hence a"
33111 "population thinly scattered over a wide area. Man was almost completely"
33111 "dominated by nature, a strange and incomprehensible riddle to him. His"
33111 "simple religious conceptions clearly reflect this. The tribe remained"
33111 "the boundary line for man, as well in regard to himself as to strangers"
33111 "outside. The gens, the tribe and their institutions were holy and"
33111 "inviolate. They were a superior power instituted by nature, and the"
33111 "feelings, thoughts and actions of the individual remained"
33111 "unconditionally subject to them. Commanding as the people of this epoch"
33111 "appear to us, nothing distinguishes one from another. They are still"
33111 "attached, as Marx has it, to the navel string of the primordial"
33111 "community."
33111 ""
33111 "The power of these natural and spontaneous communities had to be broken,"
33111 "and it was. But it was done by influences that from the very beginning"
33111 "bear the mark of degradation, of a downfall from the simple moral"
33111 "grandeur of the old gentile society. The new system of classes is"
33111 "inaugurated by the meanest impulses: vulgar covetousness, brutal lust,"
33111 "sordid avarice, selfish robbery of common wealth. The old gentile"
33111 "society without classes is undermined and brought to fall by the most"
33111 "contemptible means: theft, violence, cunning, treason. And during all"
33111 "the thousands of years of its existence, the new society has never been"
33111 "anything else but the development of the small minority at the expense"
33111 "of the exploited and oppressed majority. More than ever this is true at"
33111 "present."
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "<CHAPTER IV."
33111 ""
33111 "THE GRECIAN GENS.>"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "Greeks, Pelasgians and other nations of the same tribal origin were"
33111 "constituted since prehistoric times on the same systematic plan as the"
33111 "Americans: gens, phratry, tribe, league of tribes. The phratry might be"
33111 "missing, as e. g. among the Dorians; the league of tribes might not be"
33111 "fully developed in every case; but the gens was everywhere the unit. At"
33111 "the time of their entrance into history, the Greeks were on the"
33111 "threshold of civilization. Two full periods of evolution are stretching"
33111 "between the Greeks and the above named American tribes. The Greeks of"
33111 "the heroic age are by so much ahead of the Iroquois. For this reason the"
33111 "Grecian gens no longer retains the archaic character of the Iroquois"
33111 "gens. The stamp of group marriage is becoming rather blurred. Maternal"
33111 "law had given way to paternal lineage. Rising private property had thus"
33111 "made its first opening in the gentile constitution. A second opening"
33111 "naturally followed the first: Paternal law being now in force, the"
33111 "fortune of a wealthy heiress would have fallen to her husband in the"
33111 "case of her marriage. That would have meant the transfer of her wealth"
33111 "from her own gens to that of her husband. In order to avoid this, the"
33111 "fundament of gentile law was shattered. In such a case, the girl was not"
33111 "only permitted, but obliged to intermarry within the gens, in order to"
33111 "retain the wealth in the latter."
33111 ""
33111 "According to Grote's History of Greece, the gens of Attica was held"
33111 "together by the following bonds:"
33111 ""
33111 "1. Common religious rites and priests installed exclusively in honor of"
33111 "a certain divinity, the alleged gentile ancestor, who was designated by"
33111 "a special by-name in this capacity."
33111 ""
33111 "2. A common burial ground. (See Demosthenes' Eubulides.)"
33111 ""
33111 "3. Right of mutual inheritance."
33111 ""
33111 "4. Obligation to mutually help, protect and assist one another in case"
33111 "of violence."
33111 ""
33111 "5. Mutual right and duty to intermarry in the gens in certain cases,"
33111 "especially for orphaned girls or heiresses."
33111 ""
33111 "6. Possession of common property, at least in some cases, and an archon"
33111 "(supervisor) and treasurer elected for this special case."
33111 ""
33111 "The phratry united several gentes, but rather loosely. Still we find in"
33111 "it similar rights and duties, especially common religious rites and the"
33111 "right of avenging the death of a phrator. Again, all the phratries of a"
33111 "tribe had certain religious festivals in common that recurred at regular"
33111 "intervals and were celebrated under the guidance of a phylobasileus"
33111 "(tribal head) selected from the ranks of the nobles (eupatrides)."
33111 ""
33111 "So far Grote. And Marx adds: \"The savage (e. g. the Iroquois) is still"
33111 "plainly visible in the Grecian gens.\" On further investigation we find"
33111 "additional proofs of this. For the Grecian gens has also the following"
33111 "attributes:"
33111 ""
33111 "7. Paternal Lineage."
33111 ""
33111 "8. Prohibition of intermarrying in the gens except in the case of"
33111 "heiresses. This exception formulated as a law clearly proves the"
33111 "validity of the old rule. This is further substantiated by the"
33111 "universally accepted custom that a woman in marrying renounced the"
33111 "religious rites of her gens and accepted those of her husband's gens."
33111 "She was also registered in his phratry. According to this custom and to"
33111 "a famous quotation in Dikaearchos, marriage outside of the gens was the"
33111 "rule. Becker in \"Charikles\" directly assumes that nobody was permitted"
33111 "to intermarry in the gens."
33111 ""
33111 "9. The right to adopt strangers in the gens. It was exercised by"
33111 "adoption into the family under public formalities; but it was used"
33111 "sparingly."
33111 ""
33111 "10. The right to elect and depose the archons. We know that every gens"
33111 "had its archon. As to the heredity of the office, there is no reliable"
33111 "information. Until the end of barbarism, the probability is always"
33111 "against strict heredity. For it is absolutely incompatible with"
33111 "conditions where rich and poor had perfectly equal rights in the gens."
33111 ""
33111 "Not alone Grote, but also Niebuhr, Mommsen and all other historians of"
33111 "classical antiquity, were foiled by the gens. Though they chronicled"
33111 "many of its distinguishing marks correctly, still they always regarded"
33111 "it as a group of families and thus prevented their understanding the"
33111 "nature and origin of gentes. Under the gentile constitution, the family"
33111 "never was a unit of organization, nor could it be so, because man and"
33111 "wife necessarily belonged to two different gentes. The gens was wholly"
33111 "comprised in the phratry, the phratry in the tribe. But the family"
33111 "belonged half to the gens of the man, and half to that of the woman. Nor"
33111 "does the state recognize the family in public law. To this day, the"
33111 "family has only a place in private law. Yet all historical records take"
33111 "their departure from the absurd supposition, which was considered almost"
33111 "inviolate during the eighteenth century, that the monogamous family, an"
33111 "institution scarcely older than civilization, is the nucleus around"
33111 "which society and state gradually crystallized."
33111 ""
33111 "\"Mr. Grote will also please note,\" throws in Marx, \"that the gentes,"
33111 "which the Greeks traced to their mythologies, are older than the"
33111 "mythologies. The latter together with their gods and demi-gods were"
33111 "created by the gentes.\""
33111 ""
33111 "Grote is quoted with preference by Morgan as a prominent and quite"
33111 "trustworthy witness. He relates that every Attic gens had a name derived"
33111 "from its alleged ancestor; that before Solon's time, and even after, it"
33111 "was customary for the gentiles (gennetes) to inherit the fortunes of"
33111 "their intestate deceased; and that in case of murder first the relatives"
33111 "of the victim had the duty and the right to prosecute the criminal,"
33111 "after them the gentiles and finally the phrators. \"Whatever we may learn"
33111 "about the oldest Attic laws is founded on the organization in gentes and"
33111 "phratries.\""
33111 ""
33111 "The descent of the gentes from common ancestors has caused the"
33111 "\"schoolbred philistines,\" as Marx has it, much worry. Representing this"
33111 "descent as purely mythical, they are at a loss to explain how the gentes"
33111 "developed out of independent and wholly unrelated families. But this"
33111 "explanation must be given, if they wish to explain the existence of the"
33111 "gentes. They then turn around in a circle of meaningless gibberish and"
33111 "do not get beyond the phrase: the pedigree is indeed a fable, but the"
33111 "gens is a reality. Grote finally winds up--the parenthetical remarks are"
33111 "by Marx: \"We rarely hear about this pedigree, because it is used in"
33111 "public only on certain very festive occasions. But the less prominent"
33111 "gentes had their common religious rites (very peculiar, Mr. Grote!) and"
33111 "their common superhuman ancestor and pedigree just like the more"
33111 "prominent gentes (how very peculiar this, Mr. Grote, in less prominent"
33111 "gentes!); and the ground plan and the ideal fundament (my dear sir! Not"
33111 "ideal, but carnal, anglice \"fleshly\") was the same in all of them.\""
33111 ""
33111 "Marx sums up Morgan's reply to this as follows: \"The system of"
33111 "consanguinity corresponding to the archaic form of the gens--which the"
33111 "Greeks once possessed like other mortals--preserved the knowledge of the"
33111 "mutual relation of all members of the gens. They learned this important"
33111 "fact by practice from early childhood. With the advent of the monogamous"
33111 "family this was gradually forgotten. The gentile name created a pedigree"
33111 "by the side of which that of the monogamous family seemed insignificant."
33111 "This name had now the function of preserving the memory of the common"
33111 "descent of its bearers. But the pedigree of the gens went so far back"
33111 "that the gentiles could no longer actually ascertain their mutual"
33111 "kinship, except in a limited number of more recent common ancestors. The"
33111 "name itself was the proof of a common descent and sufficed always except"
33111 "in cases of adoption. To actually dispute all kinship between gentiles"
33111 "after the manner of Grote and Niebuhr, who thus transform the gens into"
33111 "a purely hypothetical and fictitious creation of the brain, is indeed"
33111 "worthy of \"ideal\" scientists, that is book worms. Because the relation"
33111 "of the generations, especially on the advent of monogamy, is removed to"
33111 "the far distance, and the reality of the past seems reflected in"
33111 "phantastic imaginations, therefore the brave old philistines concluded"
33111 "and conclude that the imaginary pedigree created real gentes!\""
33111 ""
33111 "The phratry was, as among the Americans, a mother-gens comprising"
33111 "several daughter gentes, and often traced them all to the same ancestor."
33111 "According to Grote \"all contemporaneous members of the phratry of"
33111 "Hekataeos were descendants in the sixteenth degree of one and the same"
33111 "divine ancestor.\" All the gentes of this phratry were therefore"
33111 "literally brother gentes. The phratry is mentioned by Homer as a"
33111 "military unit in that famous passage where Nestor advises Agamemnon:"
33111 "\"Arrange the men by phratries and tribes so that phratry may assist"
33111 "phratry, and tribe the tribe.\" The phratry has the right and the duty to"
33111 "prosecute the death of a phrator, hence in former times the duty of"
33111 "blood revenge. It has, furthermore, common religious rites and"
33111 "festivals. As a matter of fact, the development of the entire Grecian"
33111 "mythology from the traditional old Aryan cult of nature was essentially"
33111 "due to the gentes and phratries and took place within them. The phratry"
33111 "had an official head (phratriarchos) and also, according to De"
33111 "Coulanges, meetings and binding resolutions, a jurisdiction and"
33111 "administration. Even the state of a later period, while ignoring the"
33111 "gens, left certain public functions to the phratry."
33111 ""
33111 "The tribe consisted of several kindred phratries. In Attica there were"
33111 "four tribes of three phratries each; the number of gentes in each"
33111 "phratry was thirty. Such an accurate division of groups reveals the fact"
33111 "of a conscious and well-planned interference with the natural order."
33111 "How, when and why this was done is not disclosed by Grecian history. The"
33111 "historical memory of the Greeks themselves does not reach beyond the"
33111 "heroic age."
33111 ""
33111 "Closely packed in a comparatively small territory as the Greeks were,"
33111 "their dialectic differences were less conspicuous than those developed"
33111 "in the wide American forests. Yet even here we find only tribes of the"
33111 "same main dialect united in a larger organization. Little Attica had its"
33111 "own dialect which later on became the prevailing language in Grecian"
33111 "prose."
33111 ""
33111 "In the epics of Homer we generally find the Greek tribes combined into"
33111 "small nations, but so that their gentes, phratries and tribes retained"
33111 "their full independence. They already lived in towns fortified by walls."
33111 "The population increased with the growth of the herds, with agriculture"
33111 "and the beginnings of the handicrafts. At the same time the differences"
33111 "in wealth became more marked and gave rise to an aristocratic element"
33111 "within the old primordial democracy. The individual little nations"
33111 "carried on an unceasing warfare for the possession of the best land and"
33111 "also for the sake of looting. Slavery of the prisoners of war was"
33111 "already well established."
33111 ""
33111 "The constitution of these tribes and nations was as follows:"
33111 ""
33111 "1. A permanent authority was the council (bule), originally composed of"
33111 "the gentile archons, but later on, when their number became too great,"
33111 "recruited by selection in such a way that the aristocratic element was"
33111 "developed and strengthened. Dionysios openly speaks of the council at"
33111 "the time of the heroes as being composed of nobles (kratistoi). The"
33111 "council had the final decision in all important matters. In Aeschylos,"
33111 "e. g. the council of Thebes decides that the body of Eteokles be buried"
33111 "with full honors, the body of Polynikes, however, thrown out to be"
33111 "devoured by the dogs. With the rise of the state this council was"
33111 "transformed into the senate."
33111 ""
33111 "2. The public meeting (agora). We saw how the Iroquois, men and women,"
33111 "attended the council meetings, taking an orderly part in the discussions"
33111 "and influencing them. Among the Homeric Greeks, this attendance had"
33111 "developed to a complete public meeting. This was also the case with the"
33111 "Germans of the archaic period. The meeting was called by the council."
33111 "Every man could demand the word. The final vote was taken by hand"
33111 "raising (Aeschylos in \"The Suppliants,\" 607), or by acclamation. The"
33111 "decision of the meeting was supreme and final. \"Whenever a matter is"
33111 "discussed,\" says Schoemann in \"Antiquities of Greece,\" \"which requires"
33111 "the participation of the people for its execution, Homer does not"
33111 "indicate any means by which the people could be forced to it against"
33111 "their will.\" It is evident that at a time when every able-bodied member"
33111 "of the tribe was a warrior, there existed as yet no public power apart"
33111 "from the people that might have been used against them. The primordial"
33111 "democracy was still in full force, and by this standard the influence"
33111 "and position of the council and of the basileus must be judged."
33111 ""
33111 "3. The military chief (basileus). Marx makes the following comment: \"The"
33111 "European scientists, mostly born servants of princes, represent the"
33111 "basileus as a monarch in the modern sense. The Yankee republican Morgan"
33111 "objects to this. Very ironically but truthfully he says of the oily"
33111 "Gladstone and his \"Juventus Mundi\": 'Mr. Gladstone, who presents to his"
33111 "readers the Grecian chiefs of the heroic age as kings and princes, with"
33111 "the superadded qualities of gentlemen, is forced to admit that, on the"
33111 "whole we seem to have the custom or law of primogeniture sufficiently,"
33111 "but not oversharply defined.' As a matter of fact, Mr. Gladstone himself"
33111 "must have perceived that a primogeniture resting on a clause of"
33111 "'sufficient but not oversharp' definition is as bad as none at all.\""
33111 ""
33111 "We saw how the law of heredity was applied to the offices of sachems and"
33111 "chiefs among the Iroquois and other Indians. All offices were subject to"
33111 "the vote of the gentiles and for this reason hereditary in the gens. A"
33111 "vacancy was filled preferably by the next gentile relative--the brother"
33111 "or the sister's son--unless good reasons existed for passing him. That"
33111 "in Greece, under paternal law, the office of basileus was generally"
33111 "transmitted to the son or one of the sons, indicates only that the"
33111 "probability of succession by public election was in favor of the sons."
33111 "It implies by no means a legal succession without a vote of the people."
33111 "We here perceive simply the first rudiments of segregated families of"
33111 "aristocrats among Iroquois and Greeks, which led to a hereditary"
33111 "leadership or monarchy in Greece. Hence the facts are in favor of the"
33111 "opinion that among Greeks the basileus was either elected by the people"
33111 "or at last was subject to the indorsement of their appointed organs, the"
33111 "council or agora, as was the case with the Roman king (rex)."
33111 ""
33111 "In the Iliad the ruler of men, Agamemnon, does not appear as the"
33111 "supreme king of the Greeks, but as general in chief of a federal army"
33111 "besieging a city. And when dissensions had broken out among the Greeks,"
33111 "it is this quality which Odysseus points out in a famous passage: \"Evil"
33111 "is the rule of the many; let one be the ruler, one the chief\" (to which"
33111 "the popular verse about the scepter was added later on). Odysseus does"
33111 "not lecture on the form of government, but demands obedience to the"
33111 "general in chief."
33111 ""
33111 "Considering that the Greeks before Troy appear only in the character of"
33111 "an army, the proceedings of the agora are sufficiently democratic. In"
33111 "referring to presents, that is the division of the spoils, Achilles"
33111 "always leaves the division, not to Agamemnon or some other basileus, but"
33111 "to the \"sons of the Achaeans,\" the people. The attributes, descendant of"
33111 "Zeus, bred by Zeus, do not prove anything, because every gens is"
33111 "descended from some god--the gens of the leader of the tribe from a"
33111 "\"prominent\" god, in this case Zeus. Even those who are without personal"
33111 "freedom, as the swineherd Eumaeos and others, are \"divine\" (dioi or"
33111 "theioi), even in the Odyssey, which belongs to a much later period than"
33111 "the Iliad. In the same Odyssey, the name of \"heros\" is given to the"
33111 "herald Mulios as well as to the blind bard Demodokos. In short, the word"
33111 "\"basileia,\" with which the Greek writers designate the so-called"
33111 "monarchy of Homer (because the military leadership is its distinguishing"
33111 "mark, by the side of which the council and the agora are existing),"
33111 "means simply--military democracy (Marx)."
33111 ""
33111 "The basileus had also sacerdotal and judiciary functions beside those of"
33111 "a military leader. The judiciary functions are not clearly defined, but"
33111 "the functions of priesthood are due to his position of chief"
33111 "representative of the tribe or of the league of tribes. There is never"
33111 "any mention of civil, administrative functions. But it seems that he"
33111 "was ex-officio a member of the council. The translation of basileus by"
33111 "king is etymologically quite correct, because king (Kuning) is derived"
33111 "from Kuni, Kuenne, and signifies chief of a gens. But the modern meaning"
33111 "of the word king in no way designates the functions of the Grecian"
33111 "basileus. Thucydides expressly refers to the old basileia as patrike,"
33111 "that is \"derived from the gens,\" and states that it had well defined"
33111 "functions. And Aristotle says that the basileia of heroic times was a"
33111 "leadership of free men and that the basileus was a military chief, a"
33111 "judge and a high priest. Hence the basileus had no governmental power in"
33111 "a modern sense.[24]"
33111 ""
33111 "In the Grecian constitution of heroic times, then, we still find the old"
33111 "gentilism fully alive, but we also perceive the beginnings of the"
33111 "elements that undermine it; paternal law and inheritance of property by"
33111 "the father's children, favoring accumulation of wealth in the family and"
33111 "giving to the latter a power apart from the gens; influence of the"
33111 "difference of wealth on the constitution by the formation of the first"
33111 "rudiments of hereditary nobility and monarchy; slavery, first limited to"
33111 "prisoners of war, but already paving the way to the enslavement of"
33111 "tribal and gentile associates; degeneration of the old feuds between"
33111 "tribes a regular mode of existing by systematic plundering on land and"
33111 "sea for the purpose of acquiring cattle, slaves, and treasures. In"
33111 "short, wealth is praised and respected as the highest treasure, and the"
33111 "old gentile institutions are abused in order to justify the forcible"
33111 "robbery of wealth. Only one thing was missing: an institution that not"
33111 "only secured the newly acquired property of private individuals against"
33111 "the communistic traditions of the gens, that not only declared as sacred"
33111 "the formerly so despised private property and represented the protection"
33111 "of this sacred property as the highest purpose of human society, but"
33111 "that also stamped the gradually developing new forms of acquiring"
33111 "property, of constantly increasing wealth, with the universal sanction"
33111 "of society. An institution that lent the character of perpetuity not"
33111 "only to the newly rising division into classes, but also to the right of"
33111 "the possessing classes to exploit and rule the non-possessing classes."
33111 ""
33111 "And this institution was found. The state arose."
33111 ""
33111 "FOOTNOTE:"
33111 ""
33111 "[24] Author's note."
33111 ""
33111 "Just as the Grecian basileus, so the Aztec military chief was"
33111 "misrepresented as a modern prince. Morgan was the first to submit to"
33111 "historical criticism the reports of the Spaniards who first"
33111 "misapprehended and exaggerated, and later on consciously misrepresented"
33111 "the functions of this office. He showed that the Mexicans were in the"
33111 "middle stage of barbarism, but on a higher plane than the New Mexican"
33111 "Pueblo Indians, and that their constitution, so far as the garbled"
33111 "accounts show, corresponded to this stage: a league of three tribes"
33111 "which had made a number of others tributary and was administered by a"
33111 "federal council and a federal chief of war, whom the Spaniards construed"
33111 "into an \"emperor.\""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "<CHAPTER V."
33111 ""
33111 "ORIGIN OF THE ATTIC STATE.>"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "How the state gradually developed by partly transforming the organs of"
33111 "the gentile constitution, partly replacing them by new organs and"
33111 "finally installing real state authorities; how the place of the nation"
33111 "in arms defending itself through its gentes, phratries and tribes, was"
33111 "taken by an armed public power of coercion in the hands of these"
33111 "authorities and available against the mass of the people; nowhere can we"
33111 "observe the first act of this drama so well as in ancient Athens. The"
33111 "essential stages of the various transformations are outlined by Morgan,"
33111 "but the analysis of the economic causes producing them is largely added"
33111 "by myself."
33111 ""
33111 "In the heroic period, the four tribes of the Athenians were still"
33111 "installed in separate parts of Attica. Even the twelve phratries"
33111 "composing them seem to have had separate seats in the twelve different"
33111 "towns of Cecrops. The constitution was in harmony with the period: a"
33111 "public meeting (agora), a council (bule) and a basileus."
33111 ""
33111 "As far back as we can trace written history we find the land divided up"
33111 "and in the possession of private individuals. For during the last period"
33111 "of the higher stage of barbarism the production of commodities and the"
33111 "resulting trade had well advanced. Grain, wine and oil were staple"
33111 "articles. The sea trade on the Aegean Sea drifted more and more out of"
33111 "the hands of the Phoenicians into those of the Athenians. By the"
33111 "purchase and sale of land, by continued division of labor between"
33111 "agriculture and industry, trade and navigation, the members of gentes,"
33111 "phratries and tribes very soon intermingled. The districts of the"
33111 "phratry and the tribe received inhabitants who did not belong to these"
33111 "bodies and, therefore, were strangers in their own homes, although they"
33111 "were countrymen. For during times of peace, every phratry and every"
33111 "tribe administered its own affairs without consulting the council of"
33111 "Athens or the basileus. But inhabitants not belonging to the phratry or"
33111 "the tribe could not take part in the administration of these bodies."
33111 ""
33111 "Thus the well-regulated functions of the gentile organs became so"
33111 "disarranged that relief was already needed during the heroic period. A"
33111 "constitution attributed to Theseus was introduced. The main feature of"
33111 "this change was the institution of central administration in Athens. A"
33111 "part of the affairs that had so long been conducted autonomously by the"
33111 "tribes was declared collective business and transferred to a general"
33111 "council in Athens. This step of the Athenians went farther than any ever"
33111 "taken by the nations of America. For the simple federation of autonomous"
33111 "tribes was now replaced by the conglomeration of all tribes into one"
33111 "single body. The next result was a common Athenian law, standing above"
33111 "the legal traditions of the tribes and gentes. It bestowed on the"
33111 "citizens of Athens certain privileges and legal protection, even in a"
33111 "territory that did not belong to their tribe. This meant another blow to"
33111 "the gentile constitution; for it opened the way to the admission of"
33111 "citizens who were not members of any Attic tribe and stood entirely"
33111 "outside of the Athenian gentile constitution."
33111 ""
33111 "A second institution attributed to Theseus was the division of the"
33111 "entire nation into three classes regardless of the gentes, phratries and"
33111 "tribes: eupatrides or nobles, geomoroi or farmers, and demiurgoi or"
33111 "tradesmen. The exclusive privilege of the nobles to fill the offices"
33111 "was included in this innovation. Apart from this privilege the new"
33111 "division remained ineffective, as it did not create any legal"
33111 "distinctions between the classes. But it is important, because it shows"
33111 "us the new social elements that had developed in secret. It shows that"
33111 "the habitual holding of gentile offices by certain families had already"
33111 "developed into a practically uncontested privilege; that these families,"
33111 "already powerful through their wealth, began to combine outside of their"
33111 "gentes into a privileged class; and that the just arising state"
33111 "sanctioned this assumption. It shows furthermore that the division of"
33111 "labor between farmers and tradesmen had grown strong enough to contest"
33111 "the supremacy of the old gentile and tribal division of society. And"
33111 "finally it proclaims the irreconcilable opposition of gentile society to"
33111 "the state. The first attempt to form a state broke up the gentes by"
33111 "dividing their members against one another and opposing a privileged"
33111 "class to a class of disowned belonging to two different branches of"
33111 "production."
33111 ""
33111 "The ensuing political history of Athens up to the time of Solon is only"
33111 "incompletely known. The office of basileus became obsolete. Archons"
33111 "elected from the ranks of the nobility occupied the leading position in"
33111 "the state. The power of the nobility increased continually, until it"
33111 "became unbearable about the year 600 before Christ. The principal means"
33111 "for stifling the liberty of the people were--money and usury. The main"
33111 "seat of the nobility was in and around Athens. There the sea trade and"
33111 "now and then a little convenient piracy enriched them and concentrated"
33111 "the money into their hands. From this point the gradually arising money"
33111 "power penetrated like corrugating acid into the traditional modes of"
33111 "rural existence founded on natural economy. The gentile constitution is"
33111 "absolutely irreconcilable with money rule. The ruin of the Attic"
33111 "farmers coincided with the loosening of the old gentile bonds that"
33111 "protected them. The debtor's receipt and the pawning of the"
33111 "property--for the mortgage was also invented by the Athenians--cared"
33111 "neither for the gens nor for the phratry. But the old gentile"
33111 "constitution knew nothing of money, advance and debt. Hence the ever"
33111 "more virulently spreading money rule of the nobility developed a new"
33111 "legal custom, securing the creditor against the debtor and sanctioning"
33111 "the exploitation of the small farmer by the wealthy. All the rural"
33111 "districts of Attica were crowded with mortgage columns bearing the"
33111 "legend that the lot on which they stood was mortgaged to such and such"
33111 "for so much. The fields that were not so designated had for the most"
33111 "part been sold on account of overdue mortgages or interest and"
33111 "transferred to the aristocratic usurers. The farmer could thank his"
33111 "stars, if he was granted permission to live as a tenant on one-sixth of"
33111 "the product of his labor and to pay five-sixths to his new master in the"
33111 "form of rent. Worse still, if the sale of the lot did not bring"
33111 "sufficient returns to cover the debt, or if such a debt had been"
33111 "contracted without a lien, then the debtor had to sell his children into"
33111 "slavery abroad in order to satisfy the claim of the creditor. The sale"
33111 "of the children by the father--that was the first fruit of paternal law"
33111 "and monogamy! And if that did not satisfy the bloodsuckers, they could"
33111 "sell the debtor himself into slavery. Such was the pleasant dawn of"
33111 "civilization among the people of Attica."
33111 ""
33111 "Formerly, while the condition of the people was in keeping with gentile"
33111 "traditions, a similar downfall would have been impossible. But here it"
33111 "had come about, nobody knew how. Let us return for a moment to the"
33111 "Iroquois. The state of things that had imposed itself on the Athenians"
33111 "almost without their doing, so to say, and assuredly against their will,"
33111 "was inconceivable among the Indians. There the ever unchanging mode of"
33111 "production could at no time generate such conflicts as a distinction"
33111 "between rich and poor, exploiters and exploited, caused by external"
33111 "conditions. The Iroquois were far from controlling the forces of nature,"
33111 "but within the limits drawn for them by nature they dominated their own"
33111 "production. Apart from a failure of the crops in their little gardens,"
33111 "the exhaustion of the fish supply in their lakes and rivers or of the"
33111 "game stock in their forests, they always knew what would be the outcome"
33111 "of their mode of gaining a living. A more or less abundant supply of"
33111 "food, that would come of it. But the outcome could never be any"
33111 "unpremeditated social upheavals, breaking of gentile bonds or division"
33111 "of the gentiles against one another by conflicting class interests."
33111 "Production was carried on in the most limited manner; but--the producers"
33111 "controlled their own product. This immense advantage of barbarian"
33111 "production was lost in the transition to civilization. To win it back on"
33111 "the basis of man's present gigantic control of nature and of the free"
33111 "association rendered possible by it, that will be the task of the next"
33111 "generations."
33111 ""
33111 "Not so among the Greeks. The advent of private property in herds of"
33111 "cattle and articles of luxury led to an exchange between individuals, to"
33111 "a transformation of products into commodities. Here is the root of the"
33111 "entire revolution that followed. When the producers did no longer"
33111 "consume their own product, but released their hold of it in exchange for"
33111 "another's product, then they lost the control of it. They did not know"
33111 "any more what became of it. There was a possibility that the product"
33111 "might be turned against the producers for the purpose of exploiting and"
33111 "oppressing them. No society can, therefore, retain for any length of"
33111 "time the control of its own production and of the social effects of the"
33111 "mode of production, unless it abolishes exchange between individuals."
33111 ""
33111 "How rapidly after the establishment of individual exchange and after the"
33111 "transformation of products into commodities the product manifests its"
33111 "rule over the producer, the Athenians were soon to learn. Along with the"
33111 "production of marketable commodities came the tilling of the soil by"
33111 "individual cultivators for their own account, soon followed by"
33111 "individual ownership of the land. Along came also the money, that"
33111 "general commodity for which all others could be exchanged. But when men"
33111 "invented money they little suspected that they were creating a new"
33111 "social power, that one universal power before which the whole of society"
33111 "must bow down. It was this new power, suddenly sprung into existence"
33111 "without the forethought and intention of its own creators, that vented"
33111 "its rule on the Athenians with the full brutality of youth."
33111 ""
33111 "What was to be done? The old gentile organization had not only proved"
33111 "impotent against the triumphant march of money: it was also absolutely"
33111 "incapable of containing within its confines any such thing as money,"
33111 "creditors, debtors and forcible collection of debts. But the new social"
33111 "power was upon them and neither pious wishes nor a longing for the"
33111 "return of the good old times could drive money and usury from the face"
33111 "of the earth. Moreover, gentile constitution had suffered a number of"
33111 "minor defeats. The indiscriminate mingling of the gentiles and phrators"
33111 "in the whole of Attica, and especially in Athens, had assumed larger"
33111 "proportions from generation to generation. Still even now a citizen of"
33111 "Athens was not allowed to sell his residence outside of his gens,"
33111 "although he could do so with plots of land. The division of labor"
33111 "between the different branches of production--agriculture, trades,"
33111 "numberless specialties within the trades, commerce, navigation,"
33111 "etc.--had developed more fully with the progress of industry and"
33111 "traffic. The population was now divided according to occupations into"
33111 "rather well defined groups, everyone of which had separate interests not"
33111 "guarded by the gens or phratry and therefore necessitating the creation"
33111 "of new offices. The number of slaves had increased considerably and must"
33111 "have surpassed by far that of the free Athenians even at this early"
33111 "stage. Gentile society originally knew no slavery and was, therefore,"
33111 "ignorant of any means to hold this mass of bondsmen in check. And"
33111 "finally, commerce had attracted a great many strangers who settled in"
33111 "Athens for the sake of the easier living it afforded. According to the"
33111 "old constitution, the strangers had neither civil rights nor the"
33111 "protection of the law. Though tacitly admitted by tradition, they"
33111 "remained a disturbing and foreign element."
33111 ""
33111 "In short, gentile constitution approached its doom. Society was daily"
33111 "growing more and more beyond it. It was powerless to stop or allay even"
33111 "the most distressing evils that had grown under its very eyes. But in"
33111 "the meantime the state had secretly developed. The new groups formed by"
33111 "division of labor, first between city and country, then between the"
33111 "various branches of city industry, had created new organs for the care"
33111 "of their interests. Public offices of every description had been"
33111 "instituted. And above all the young state needed its own fighting"
33111 "forces. Among the seafaring Athenians this had to be at first only a"
33111 "navy, for occasional short expeditions and the protection of the"
33111 "merchant vessels. At some uncertain time before Solon, the naukrariai"
33111 "were instituted, little territorial districts, twelve in each tribe."
33111 "Every naukraria had to furnish, equip and man a war vessel and to detail"
33111 "two horsemen. This arrangement was a twofold attack on the gentile"
33111 "constitution. In the first place it created a public power of coercion"
33111 "that did no longer absolutely coincide with the entirety of the armed"
33111 "nation. In the second place it was the first division of the people for"
33111 "public purposes, not by groups of kinship, but by local residence. We"
33111 "shall soon see what that signified."
33111 ""
33111 "As the gentile constitution could not come to the assistance of the"
33111 "exploited people, they could look only to the rising state. And the"
33111 "state brought help in the form of the constitution of Solon. At the same"
33111 "time it added to its own strength at the expense of the old"
33111 "constitution. Solon opened the series of so-called political revolutions"
33111 "by an infringement on private property. We pass over the means by which"
33111 "this reform was accomplished in the year 594 B. C. or thereabout. Ever"
33111 "since, all revolutions have been revolutions for the protection of one"
33111 "kind of property against another kind of property. They cannot protect"
33111 "one kind without violating another. In the great French revolution the"
33111 "feudal property was sacrificed for the sake of saving bourgeois"
33111 "property. In Solon's revolution, the property of the creditors had to"
33111 "make concessions to the property of the debtors. The debts were simply"
33111 "declared illegal. We are not acquainted with the accurate details, but"
33111 "Solon boasts in his poems that he removed the mortgage columns from the"
33111 "indented lots and enabled all who had fled or been sold abroad for debts"
33111 "to return home. This was only feasible by an open violation of private"
33111 "property. And indeed, all so-called political revolutions were started"
33111 "for the protection of one kind of property by the confiscation, also"
33111 "called theft, of another kind of property. It is absolutely true that"
33111 "for more than 2,500 years private property could only be protected by"
33111 "the violation of private property."
33111 ""
33111 "But now a way had to be found to avoid the return of such an enslavement"
33111 "of the free Athenians. This was first attempted by general measures, e."
33111 "g., the prohibition of contracts giving the person of the debtor in"
33111 "lien. Furthermore a maximum limit was fixed for the amount of land any"
33111 "one individual could own, in order to keep the craving of the nobility"
33111 "for the land of the farmers within reasonable bounds. Constitutional"
33111 "amendments were next in order. The following deserve special"
33111 "consideration:"
33111 ""
33111 "The council was increased to four hundred members, one hundred from each"
33111 "tribe. Here, then, the tribe still served as a basis. But this was the"
33111 "only remnant of the old constitution that was transferred to the new"
33111 "body politic. For otherwise Solon divided the citizens into four classes"
33111 "according to their property in land and its yield. Five hundred, three"
33111 "hundred and one hundred and fifty medimnoi of grain (1 medimnos equals"
33111 "1.16 bushels) were the minimum yields of the first three classes."
33111 "Whoever had less land or none at all belonged to the fourth class. Only"
33111 "members of the first three classes could hold office; the highest"
33111 "offices were filled by the first class. The fourth class had only the"
33111 "right to speak and vote in the public council. But here all officials"
33111 "were elected, here they had to give account, here all the laws were"
33111 "made, and here the fourth class was in the majority. The aristocratic"
33111 "privileges were partly renewed in the form of privileges of wealth, but"
33111 "the people retained the decisive power. The four classes also formed the"
33111 "basis for the reorganization of the fighting forces. The first two"
33111 "classes furnished the horsemen; the third had to serve as heavy"
33111 "infantry; the fourth was employed as light unarmored infantry and had to"
33111 "man the navy. Probably the last class also received wages in this case."
33111 ""
33111 "An entirely new element is thus introduced into the constitution:"
33111 "private property. The rights and duties of the citizens are graduated"
33111 "according to their property in land. Wherever the classification by"
33111 "property gains ground, there the old groups of blood relationship give"
33111 "way. Gentile constitution has suffered another defeat."
33111 ""
33111 "However, the gradation of political rights according to private property"
33111 "was not one of those institutions without which a state cannot exist. It"
33111 "may have been ever so important in the constitutional development of"
33111 "some states. Still a good many others, and the most completely developed"
33111 "at that, had no need of it. Even in Athens it played only a passing"
33111 "role. Since the time of Aristides, all offices were open to all the"
33111 "citizens."
33111 ""
33111 "During the next eighty years the Athenian society gradually drifted into"
33111 "the course on which it further developed in the following centuries. The"
33111 "outrageous land speculation of the time before Solon had been fettered,"
33111 "likewise the excessive concentration of property in land. Commerce,"
33111 "trades and artisan handicrafts, which were carried on in an ever larger"
33111 "scale as slave labor increased, became the ruling factors in gaining a"
33111 "living. Public enlightenment advanced. Instead of exploiting their own"
33111 "fellow citizens in the old brutal style, the Athenians now exploited"
33111 "mainly the slaves and the customers outside. Movable property, wealth in"
33111 "money, slaves and ships, increased more and more. But instead of being a"
33111 "simple means for the purchase of land, as in the old stupid times, it"
33111 "had now become an end in itself. The new class of industrial and"
33111 "commercial owners of wealth now waged a victorious competition against"
33111 "the old nobility. The remnants of the old gentile constitution lost"
33111 "their last hold. The gentes, phratries and tribes, the members of which"
33111 "now were dispersed all over Attica and completely intermixed, had thus"
33111 "become unavailable as political groups. A great many citizens of Athens"
33111 "did not belong to any gens. They were immigrants who had been adopted"
33111 "into citizenship, but not into any of the old groups of kinship."
33111 "Besides, there was a steadily increasing number of foreign immigrants"
33111 "who were only protected by traditional sufferance."
33111 ""
33111 "Meanwhile the struggles of the parties proceeded. The nobility tried to"
33111 "regain their former privileges and for a short time recovered their"
33111 "supremacy, until the revolution of Kleisthenes (509 B. C.) brought their"
33111 "final downfall and completed the ruin of gentile law."
33111 ""
33111 "In his new constitution, Kleisthenes ignored the four old tribes founded"
33111 "on the gentes and phratries. Their place was taken by an entirely new"
33111 "organization based on the recently attempted division of the citizens"
33111 "into naukrariai according to residence. No longer was membership in a"
33111 "group of kindred the dominant fact, but simply local residence. Not the"
33111 "nation, but the territory was now divided; the inhabitants became mere"
33111 "political fixtures of the territory."
33111 ""
33111 "The whole of Attica was divided into one hundred communal districts,"
33111 "so-called demoi, every one of which was autonomous. The citizens living"
33111 "in a demos (demotoi) elected their official head (demarchos), treasurer"
33111 "and thirty judges with jurisdiction in minor cases. They also received"
33111 "their own temple and divine guardian or heros, whose priest they"
33111 "elected. The control of the demos was in the hands of the council of"
33111 "demotoi. This is, as Morgan correctly remarks, the prototype of the"
33111 "autonomous American township. The modern state in its highest"
33111 "development ended in the same unit with which the rising state began its"
33111 "career in Athens."
33111 ""
33111 "Ten of these units (demoi) formed a tribe, which, however, was now"
33111 "designated as local tribe in order to distinguish it from the old sex"
