33111 "tribe. The local tribe was not only an autonomous political, but also a"
33111 "military group. It elected the phylarchos or tribal head who commanded"
33111 "the horsemen, the taxiarchos commanding the infantry and the strategic"
33111 "leader, who was in command of the entire contingent raised in the tribal"
33111 "territory by conscription. The local tribe furthermore furnished,"
33111 "equipped and fully manned five war vessels. It was designated by the"
33111 "name of the Attic hero who was its guardian deity. It elected fifty"
33111 "councilmen into the council of Athens."
33111 ""
33111 "Thus we arrive at the Athenian state, governed by a council of five"
33111 "hundred elected by and representing the ten tribes and subject to the"
33111 "vote of the public meeting, where every citizen could enter and vote."
33111 "Archons and other officials attended to the different departments of"
33111 "administration and justice."
33111 ""
33111 "By this new constitution and by the admission of a large number of"
33111 "aliens, partly freed slaves, partly immigrants, the organs of gentile"
33111 "constitution were displaced in public affairs. They became mere private"
33111 "and religious clubs. But their moral influence, the traditional"
33111 "conceptions and views of the old gentile period, survived for a long"
33111 "time and expired only gradually. This was evident in another state"
33111 "institution."
33111 ""
33111 "We have seen that an essential mark of the state consists in a public"
33111 "power of coercion divorced from the mass of the people. Athens possessed"
33111 "at that time only a militia and a navy equipped and manned directly by"
33111 "the people. These afforded protection against external enemies and held"
33111 "the slaves in check, who at that time already made up the large majority"
33111 "of the population. For the citizens, this coercive power at first only"
33111 "existed in the shape of the police, which is as old as the state. The"
33111 "innocent Frenchmen of the 18th century, therefore, had the habit of"
33111 "speaking not of civilized, but of policed nations (nations policees)."
33111 "The Athenians, then, provided for a police in their new state, a"
33111 "veritable \"force\" of bowmen on foot and horseback. This police force"
33111 "consisted--of slaves. The free Athenian regarded this police duty as so"
33111 "degrading that he preferred being arrested by an armed slave rather than"
33111 "lending himself to such an ignominious service. That was still a sign of"
33111 "the old gentile spirit. The state could not exist without a police, but"
33111 "as yet it was too young and did not command sufficient moral respect to"
33111 "give prestige to an occupation that necessarily appeared ignominious to"
33111 "the old gentiles."
33111 ""
33111 "How well this state, now completed in its main outlines, suited the"
33111 "social condition of the Athenians was apparent by the rapid growth of"
33111 "wealth, commerce and industry. The distinction of classes on which the"
33111 "social and political institutions are resting was no longer between"
33111 "nobility and common people, but between slaves and freemen, aliens and"
33111 "citizens. At the time of the greatest prosperity the whole number of"
33111 "free Athenian citizens, women and children included, amounted to about"
33111 "90,000; the slaves of both sexes numbered 365,000 and the"
33111 "aliens--foreigners and freed slaves--45,000. Per capita of each adult"
33111 "citizen there were, therefore, at least eighteen slaves and more than"
33111 "two aliens. The great number of slaves is explained by the fact that"
33111 "many of them worked together in large factories under supervision. The"
33111 "development of commerce and industry brought about an accumulation and"
33111 "concentration of wealth in a few hands. The mass of the free citizens"
33111 "were impoverished and had to face the choice of either competing with"
33111 "their own labor against slave labor, which was considered ignoble and"
33111 "vile, besides promising little success, or to be ruined. Under the"
33111 "prevailing circumstances they necessarily chose the latter course and"
33111 "being in the majority they ruined the whole Attic state. Not democracy"
33111 "caused the downfall of Athens, as the European glorifiers of princes and"
33111 "lickspittle schoolmasters would have us believe, but slavery"
33111 "ostracizing the labor of the free citizen."
33111 ""
33111 "The origin of the state among the Athenians presents a very typical form"
33111 "of state organization. For it took place without any marring external"
33111 "interference or internal obstruction--the usurpation of Pisistratos left"
33111 "no trace of its short duration. It shows the direct rise of a highly"
33111 "developed form of a state, the democratic republic, out of gentile"
33111 "society. And finally, we are sufficiently acquainted with all the"
33111 "essential details of the process."
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "<CHAPTER VI."
33111 ""
33111 "GENS AND STATE IN ROME.>"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "The legend of the foundation of Rome sets forth that the first"
33111 "colonization was undertaken by a number of Latin gentes (one hundred, so"
33111 "the legend says) united into one tribe. A Sabellian tribe (also said to"
33111 "consist of one hundred gentes) soon followed, and finally a third tribe"
33111 "of various elements, but again numbering one hundred gentes, joined"
33111 "them. The whole tale reveals at the first glance that little more than"
33111 "the gens was borrowed from reality, and that the gens itself was in"
33111 "certain cases only an offshoot of an old mother gens still existing at"
33111 "home. The tribes bear the mark of artificial composition on their"
33111 "foreheads; still they were made up of kindred elements and after the"
33111 "model of the old spontaneous, not artificial tribe. At the same time it"
33111 "is not impossible that a genuine old tribe formed the nucleus of every"
33111 "one of these three tribes. The connecting link, the phratry, contained"
33111 "ten gentes and was called curia. Hence there were thirty curiae."
33111 ""
33111 "The Roman gens is recognized as an institution identical with the"
33111 "Grecian gens. The Grecian gens being a continuation of the same social"
33111 "unit, the primordial form of which we found among the American Indians,"
33111 "the same holds naturally good of the Roman gens, and we can be more"
33111 "concise in its treatment."
33111 ""
33111 "At least during the most ancient times of the city, the Roman gens had"
33111 "the following constitution:"
33111 ""
33111 "1. Mutual right of inheritance for gentiles; the wealth remained in the"
33111 "gens. Paternal law being already in force in the Roman the same as in"
33111 "the Grecian gens, the offspring of female lineage were excluded."
33111 "According to the law of the twelve tablets, the oldest written law of"
33111 "Rome known to us, the natural children had the first title to the"
33111 "estate; in case no natural children existed, the agnati (kin of male"
33111 "lineage) took their place; and last in line came the gentiles. In all"
33111 "cases the property remained in the gens. Here we observe the gradual"
33111 "introduction of new legal provisions, caused by increased wealth and"
33111 "monogamy, into the gentile practice. The originally equal right of"
33111 "inheritance of the gentiles was first limited in practice to the agnati,"
33111 "no doubt at a very remote date, and afterwards to the natural children"
33111 "and their offspring of male lineage. Of course this appears in the"
33111 "reverse order on the twelve tablets."
33111 ""
33111 "2. Possession of a common burial ground. The patrician gens Claudia, on"
33111 "immigrating into Rome from Regilli, was assigned to a separate lot of"
33111 "land and received its own burial ground in the city. As late as the time"
33111 "of Augustus, the head of Varus, who had been killed in the Teutoburger"
33111 "Wald, was brought to Rome and interred in the gentilitius tumulus; hence"
33111 "his gens (Quinctilia) still had its own tomb."
33111 ""
33111 "3. Common religious rites. These are well-known under the name of sacra"
33111 "gentilitia."
33111 ""
33111 "4. Obligation not to intermarry in the gens. It seems that this was"
33111 "never a written law in Rome, but the custom remained. Among the"
33111 "innumerable names of Roman couples preserved for us there is not a"
33111 "single case, where husband and wife had the same gentile name. The law"
33111 "of inheritance proves the same rule. By marrying, a woman loses her"
33111 "agnatic privileges, discards her gens, and neither she nor her children"
33111 "have any title to her father's estate nor to that of his brothers,"
33111 "because otherwise the gens of her father would lose his property. This"
33111 "rule has a meaning only then when the woman is not permitted to marry a"
33111 "gentile."
33111 ""
33111 "5. A common piece of land. In primeval days this was always obtained"
33111 "when the tribal territory was first divided. Among the Latin tribes we"
33111 "find the land partly in the possession of the tribe, partly of the gens,"
33111 "and partly of the households that could hardly represent single families"
33111 "at such an early date. Romulus is credited with being the first to"
33111 "assign land to single individuals, about 2.47 acres (two jugera) per"
33111 "head. But later on we still find some land in the hands of the gentes,"
33111 "not to mention the state land, around which turns the whole internal"
33111 "history of the republic."
33111 ""
33111 "6. Duty of the gentiles to mutually protect and assist one another."
33111 "Written history records only remnants of this law. The Roman state from"
33111 "the outset manifested such superior power, that the duty of protection"
33111 "against injury devolved upon it. When Appius Claudius was arrested, his"
33111 "whole gens, including his personal enemies, dressed in mourning. At the"
33111 "time of the second Punic war the gentes united for the purpose of"
33111 "ransoming their captured gentiles. The senate vetoed this."
33111 ""
33111 "7. Right to bear the gentile name. This was in force until the time of"
33111 "the emperors. Freed slaves were permitted to assume the gentile name of"
33111 "their former master, but this did not bestow any gentile rights on them."
33111 ""
33111 "8. Right of adopting strangers into the gens. This was done by adoption"
33111 "into the family (the same as among the Indians) which brought with it"
33111 "the adoption into the gens."
33111 ""
33111 "9. The right to elect and depose chiefs is not mentioned anywhere. But"
33111 "inasmuch as during the first years of Rome's existence all offices were"
33111 "filled by election or nomination, from the king downward, and as the"
33111 "curiae elected also their own priests, we are justified in assuming the"
33111 "same in regard to gentile chiefs (principes)--no matter how well"
33111 "established the rule of choosing the candidates from the same family"
33111 "have been."
33111 ""
33111 "Such were the constitutional rights of a Roman gens. With the exception"
33111 "of the completed transition to paternal law, they are the true image of"
33111 "the rights and duties of an Iroquois gens. Here, also, \"the Iroquois is"
33111 "still plainly visible.\""
33111 ""
33111 "How confused the ideas of our historians, even the most prominent of"
33111 "them, are when it comes to a discussion of the Roman gens, is shown by"
33111 "the following example: In Mommsen's treatise on the Roman family names"
33111 "of the Republican and Augustinian era (Roemische Forschungen, Berlin,"
33111 "1864, Vol. I.) he writes: \"The gentile name was not only borne by all"
33111 "male gentiles including all adopted and wards, except, of course, the"
33111 "slaves, but also by the women.... The tribe (so Mommsen translates gens)"
33111 "is a common organization resulting from a common--actual, assumed or"
33111 "even invented--ancestor and united by common rites, burial grounds and"
33111 "customs of inheritance. All free individuals, hence women also, may and"
33111 "must claim membership in them. But the definition of the gentile name of"
33111 "the married women offers some difficulty. This is indeed obviated, as"
33111 "long as women were not permitted to marry any one but their gentiles."
33111 "And we have proofs that for a long time the women found it much more"
33111 "difficult to marry outside than inside of the gens. This right of"
33111 "marrying outside, the gentis enuptio, was still bestowed as a personal"
33111 "privilege and reward during the sixth century.... But wherever such"
33111 "outside marriages occurred in primeval times, the woman must have been"
33111 "transferred to the tribe of her husband. Nothing is more certain than"
33111 "that by the old religious marriage woman was completely adopted into"
33111 "the legal and sacramental group of her husband and divorced from her"
33111 "own. Who does not know that the married woman releases her active and"
33111 "passive right of inheritance in favor of her gentiles, but enters the"
33111 "legal group of her husband, her children and his gentiles? And if her"
33111 "husband adopts her as his child into his family, how can she remain"
33111 "separated from his gens?\" (Pages 9-11.)"
33111 ""
33111 "Here Mommsen asserts that the Roman women belonging to a certain gens"
33111 "were originally free to marry only within their gens; the Roman gens,"
33111 "according to him, was therefore endogamous, not exogamous. This opinion"
33111 "which contradicts the evidence of all other nations, is principally, if"
33111 "not exclusively, founded on a single much disputed passage of Livy (Book"
33111 "xxxix, c. 19). According to this passage, the senate decreed in the year"
33111 "568 of the city, i. e., 186 B. C., (uti Feceniae Hispallae datio,"
33111 "deminutio, gentis enuptio, tutoris optio idem esset quasi ei vir"
33111 "testamento dedisset; utique ei ingenuo nubere liceret, neu quid ei qui"
33111 "eam duxisset, ob id fraudi ignominiaeve esset)--that Fecenia Hispalla"
33111 "shall have the right to dispose of her property, to diminish it, to"
33111 "marry outside of the gens, to choose a guardian, just as if her (late)"
33111 "husband had conferred this right on her by testament; that she shall be"
33111 "permitted to marry a freeman and that for the man who marries her this"
33111 "shall not constitute a misdemeanor or a shame."
33111 ""
33111 "Without a doubt Fecenia, a freed slave, here obtains permission to marry"
33111 "outside of the gens. And equally doubtless the husband here has the"
33111 "right to confer on his wife by testament the right to marry outside of"
33111 "the gens after his death. But outside of which gens?"
33111 ""
33111 "If a woman had to intermarry in the gens, as Mommsen assumes, then she"
33111 "remained in this gens after her marriage. But in the first place, this"
33111 "assertion of an endogamous gens must be proven. And in the second"
33111 "place, if the women had to intermarry in the gens, then the men had to"
33111 "do the same, otherwise there could be no marriage. Then we arrive at the"
33111 "conclusion that the man could bequeath a right to his wife, which he did"
33111 "not have for himself. This is a legal impossibility. Mommsen feels this"
33111 "very well, and hence he supposes: \"The marriage outside of the gens most"
33111 "probably required not only the consent of the testator, but of all"
33111 "gentiles.\" (Page 10, footnote.) This is not only a very daring"
33111 "assertion, but contradicts also the clear wording of the passage. The"
33111 "senate gives her this right as a proxy of her husband; they expressly"
33111 "give her no more and no less than her husband could have given her, but"
33111 "what they do give is an absolute right, independent of all limitations,"
33111 "so that, if she should make use of it, her new husband shall not suffer"
33111 "in consequence. The senate even instructs the present and future consuls"
33111 "and praetors to see that no inconvenience arise to her from the use of"
33111 "this right. Mommsen's supposition is therefore absolutely inadmissible."
33111 ""
33111 "Then again: suppose a woman married a man from another gens, but"
33111 "remained in her own gens. According to the passage quoted above, her"
33111 "husband would then have had the right to permit his wife to marry"
33111 "outside of her own gens. That is, he would have had the right to make"
33111 "provisions in regard to the affairs of a gens to which he did not belong"
33111 "at all. The thing is so utterly unreasonable that we need not lose any"
33111 "words about it."
33111 ""
33111 "Nothing remains but to assume that the woman in her first marriage"
33111 "wedded a man from another gens and thereby became a member of her"
33111 "husband's gens. Mommsen admits this for such cases. Then the whole"
33111 "matter at once explains itself. The woman, torn away from her old gens"
33111 "by her marriage and adopted into the gentile group of her husband,"
33111 "occupies a peculiar position in the new gens. She is now a gentile, but"
33111 "not a kin by blood. The manner of her entrance from the outset excludes"
33111 "all prohibition of intermarrying in the gens, into which she has come by"
33111 "marriage. She is adopted into the family relations of the gens and"
33111 "inherits some of the property of her husband when he dies, the property"
33111 "of a gentile. What is more natural than that this property should remain"
33111 "in the gens and that she should be obliged to marry a gentile of her"
33111 "husband and no other? If, however, an exception is to be made, who is so"
33111 "well entitled to authorize her as her first husband who bequeathed his"
33111 "property to her? At the moment when he bequeathes on her a part of his"
33111 "property and simultaneously gives her permission to transfer this"
33111 "property by marriage or as a result of marriage to a strange gens, he"
33111 "still is the owner of this property, hence he literally disposes of his"
33111 "personal property. As for the woman and her relation to the gens of her"
33111 "husband, it is he who by an act of his own free will--the"
33111 "marriage--introduced her into his gens. Therefore it seems quite natural"
33111 "that he should be the proper person to authorize her to leave this gens"
33111 "by another marriage. In short, the matter appears simple and obvious, as"
33111 "soon as we discard the absurd conception of an endogamous Roman gens and"
33111 "accept Morgan's originally exogamous gens."
33111 ""
33111 "There is still another view which has probably found the greatest number"
33111 "of advocates. According to them the passage in Livy only means \"that"
33111 "freed slave girls (libertae) cannot without special permission, e gente"
33111 "enubere (marry outside of the gens) or undertake any of the steps which,"
33111 "together with capitis deminutio minima[25] (the loss of family rights)"
33111 "would lead to a transfer of the liberta to another gens.\" (Lange,"
33111 "Roemische Alterthuemer, Berlin, 1856, I, p. 185, where our passage from"
33111 "Livy is explained by a reference to Huschke.) If this view is correct,"
33111 "then the passage proves still less for the relations of free Roman"
33111 "women, and there is so much less ground for speaking of their obligation"
33111 "to intermarry in the gens."
33111 ""
33111 "The expression enuptio gentis (marriage outside of the gens) occurs only"
33111 "in this single passage and is not found anywhere else in the entire"
33111 "Roman literature. The word enubere (to marry outside) is found only"
33111 "three times likewise in Livy, and not in reference to the gens. The"
33111 "phantastic idea that Roman women had to intermarry in the gens owes its"
33111 "existence only to this single passage. But it cannot be maintained. For"
33111 "either the passage refers to special restrictions for freed slave women,"
33111 "in which case it proves nothing for free women (ingenuae). Or it applies"
33111 "also to free women, in which case it rather proves that the women as a"
33111 "rule married outside of the gens and were transferred by their marriage"
33111 "to their husbands' gens. This would be a point for Morgan against"
33111 "Mommsen."
33111 ""
33111 "Almost three hundred years after the foundation of Rome the gentile"
33111 "bonds were still so strong that a patrician gens, the Fabians, could"
33111 "obtain permission from the senate to undertake all by itself a war"
33111 "expedition against the neighboring town of Veii. Three hundred and six"
33111 "Fabians are said to have marched and to have been killed from ambush."
33111 "Only one boy was left behind to propagate the gens."
33111 ""
33111 "Ten gentes, we said, formed a phratry, named curia. It was endowed with"
33111 "more important functions than the Grecian phratry. Every curia had its"
33111 "own religious rites, sacred possessions and priests. The priests of one"
33111 "curia in a body formed one of the Roman clerical collegiums. Ten curiae"
33111 "formed a tribe which probably had originally its own elected"
33111 "chief--leader in war and high priest--like the rest of the Latin tribes."
33111 "The three tribes together formed the populus Romanus, the Roman people."
33111 ""
33111 "Hence nobody could belong to the Roman people, unless he was a member of"
33111 "a Roman gens, and thus a member of a curia and tribe. The first"
33111 "constitution of the Roman people was as follows. Public affairs were"
33111 "conducted by the Senate composed, as Niebuhr was the first to state"
33111 "correctly, of the chiefs of the three hundred gentes. Because they were"
33111 "the elders of the gentes they were called patres, fathers, and as a body"
33111 "senatus, council of elders, from senex, old. Here also the customary"
33111 "choice of men from the same family of the gens brought to life the first"
33111 "hereditary nobility. These families were called patricians and claimed"
33111 "the exclusive right to the seats in the senate and to all other offices."
33111 "The fact that in the course of time the people admitted this claim so"
33111 "that it became an actual privilege is confirmed by the legendary report"
33111 "that Romulus bestowed the rank of patrician and its privileges on the"
33111 "first senators. The senate, like the Athenian boule, had to make the"
33111 "final decision in many affairs and to undertake the preliminary"
33111 "discussion of more important matters, especially of new laws. These were"
33111 "settled by the public meeting, the so-called comitia curiata (assembly"
33111 "of curiae.) The people met in curiae, probably grouped by gentes, and"
33111 "every one of the thirty curiae had one vote. The assembly of curiae"
33111 "adopted or rejected all laws, elected all higher officials including the"
33111 "rex (so-called king), declared war (but the senate concluded peace), and"
33111 "decided as a supreme court, on appeal, all cases involving capital"
33111 "punishment of Roman citizens. By the side of the senate and the public"
33111 "meeting stood the rex, corresponding to the Grecian basileus, and by no"
33111 "means, such an almost absolute king as Mommsen would have it.[26] The"
33111 "rex was also a military leader, a high priest and a chairman of certain"
33111 "courts. He had no other functions, nor any power over life, liberty and"
33111 "property of the citizens, except such as resulted from his disciplinary"
33111 "power as military leader or from his executive power as president of a"
33111 "court. The office of rex was not hereditary. On the contrary, he was"
33111 "elected, probably on the suggestion of his predecessor, by the assembly"
33111 "of curiae and then solemnly invested by a second assembly. That he could"
33111 "also be deposed is proved by the fate of Tarquinius Superbus."
33111 ""
33111 "As the Greeks at the time of the heroes, so the Romans at the time of"
33111 "the so-called kings lived in a military democracy based on and developed"
33111 "from a constitution of gentes, phratries and tribes. What though the"
33111 "curiae and tribes were partly artificial formations, they were moulded"
33111 "after the genuine and spontaneous models of a society from which they"
33111 "originated and that still surrounded them on all sides. And though the"
33111 "sturdy patrician nobility had already gained ground, though the reges"
33111 "attempted gradually to enlarge the scope of their functions--all this"
33111 "does not change the elementary and fundamental character of the"
33111 "constitution, and this alone is essential."
33111 ""
33111 "Meantime the population of the city of Rome and of the Roman territory,"
33111 "enlarged by conquest, increased partly by immigration, partly through"
33111 "the inhabitants of the annexed districts, Latins most of them. All these"
33111 "new members of the state (we disregard here the clients) stood outside"
33111 "of the old gentes, curiae and tribes and so did not form a part of the"
33111 "populus Romanus, the Roman people proper. They were personally free,"
33111 "could own land, had to pay taxes and were subject to military service."
33111 "But they were not eligible to office and could neither take part in the"
33111 "assembly of curiae nor in the distribution of conquered state lands."
33111 "They made up the mass of people excluded from all public rights, the"
33111 "plebs. By their continually growing numbers, their military training and"
33111 "armament they became a threat for the old populus who now closed their"
33111 "ranks hermetically against all new elements. The land seems to have been"
33111 "about evenly divided between populus and plebs, while the mercantile and"
33111 "industrial wealth, though as yet not very considerable, may have been"
33111 "mainly in the hands of the plebs."
33111 ""
33111 "In view of the utter darkness that enwraps the whole legendary origin of"
33111 "Rome's historical beginning--a darkness that was rendered still more"
33111 "intense by the rationalistic and overofficious interpretations and"
33111 "reports of the juristically trained authors that wrote on the"
33111 "subject--it is impossible to make any definite statements about the"
33111 "time, the course and the motive of the revolution that put an end to the"
33111 "old gentile constitution. We are certain only that the causes arose out"
33111 "of the fights between the plebs and the populus."
33111 ""
33111 "The new constitution, attributed to rex Servius Tullius and following"
33111 "the Grecian model, more especially that of Solon, created a new public"
33111 "assembly including or excluding all the members of populus and plebs"
33111 "according to whether they rendered military service or not. The whole"
33111 "population, subject to enlistment, was divided into six classes"
33111 "according to wealth. The lowest limitis in the five highest classes"
33111 "were: I., 100,000 ass; II., 75,000; III., 50,000; IV., 25,000; V.,"
33111 "11,000; which according to Dureau de la Malle is equal to about $3,155,"
33111 "$2,333, $1,555, $800, and $388. The sixth class, the proletarians,"
33111 "consisted of those who possessed less and were exempt from military"
33111 "service and taxes. In this new assembly of centuriae (comitia"
33111 "centuriata) the citizens formed ranks after the manner of soldiers, in"
33111 "companies of one hundred (centuria), and every centuria had one vote."
33111 "Now the first class placed 80 centuriae in the field; the second 22, the"
33111 "third 20, the fourth 22, the fifth 30 and the sixth, for propriety's"
33111 "sake, one. To this were added 18 centuriae of horsemen composed of the"
33111 "most wealthy. Hence, there were 193 centuriae, giving a lowest majority"
33111 "vote of 97. Now the horsemen and the first class alone had together 98"
33111 "votes. Being in the majority, they had only to agree, and they could"
33111 "pass any resolution without asking the consent of the other classes."
33111 ""
33111 "This new assembly of centuriae assumed all the political rights of the"
33111 "former assembly of curiae, a few nominal privileges excepted. The curiae"
33111 "and the gentes composing them now were degraded to mere private and"
33111 "religious congregations, analogous to their Attic prototypes, and as"
33111 "such they vegetated on for a long time. But the assembly of curiae soon"
33111 "became obsolete. In order to drive also the three old tribes out of"
33111 "existence, a system of four local tribes was introduced. Every tribe was"
33111 "assigned to one quarter of the city and received certain political"
33111 "rights."
33111 ""
33111 "Thus the old social order of blood kinship was destroyed also in Rome"
33111 "even before the abolition of the so-called royalty. A new constitution,"
33111 "founded on territorial division and difference of wealth took its place"
33111 "and virtually created the state. The public power of coercion consisted"
33111 "here of citizens liable to military duty, to be used against the slaves"
33111 "and the so-called proletarians who were excluded from military service"
33111 "and general armament."
33111 ""
33111 "After the expulsion of the last rex, Tarquinius Superbus, who had really"
33111 "usurped royal power, the new constitution was further improved by the"
33111 "institution of two military leaders (consuls) with equal powers,"
33111 "analogous to the custom of the Iroquois. The whole history of the Roman"
33111 "republic moves inside of this constitution: the struggles between"
33111 "patricians and plebs for admission to office and participation in the"
33111 "allotment of state lands, the merging of the patrician nobility in the"
33111 "new class of large property and money owners; the gradual absorption by"
33111 "the latter of all the land of the small holders who had been ruined by"
33111 "military service; the cultivation of these enormous new tracts by"
33111 "slaves; the resulting depopulation of Italy which not only opened the"
33111 "doors to the imperial tyrants, but also to their successors, the German"
33111 "barbarians."
33111 ""
33111 "FOOTNOTES:"
33111 ""
33111 "[25] Translator's note."
33111 ""
33111 "The term caput received the meaning of legal right of a person from the"
33111 "legal status of the head of a family.... Legal science extended the"
33111 "meaning of the term so that it related not alone to slaves, but also to"
33111 "minors and women. This legal right, so conceived, could be curtailed in"
33111 "three ways: Capitis deminutio maxima was the loss of the status"
33111 "libertatis (personal liberty), which included the loss of the status"
33111 "civitatis and familiae (civil and family rights); the capitis deminutio"
33111 "minor or media was the loss of the status civitatis (civil rights),"
33111 "including the loss of the status familiae (family rights); the capitis"
33111 "deminutio minima was the loss of the status familiae (family rights)."
33111 "Lange, Roemische Alterthuemer, Berlin, 1876, Vol. I., p. 204."
33111 ""
33111 "[26] Author's note."
33111 ""
33111 "The Latin rex is equivalent to the Celtic-Irish righ (tribal chief) and"
33111 "the Gothic reiks. That this, like the German Fuerst, English first and"
33111 "Danish forste, originally signified gentile or tribal chief is evident"
33111 "from the fact that the Goths in the fourth century already had a special"
33111 "term for the king of later times, the military chief of a whole nation,"
33111 "viz., thiudans. In Ulfila's translation of the Bible Artaxerxes and"
33111 "Herod are never called reiks, but thiudans, and the empire of the"
33111 "emperor Tiberius not reiki, but thiudinassus. In the name of the Gothic"
33111 "thiudans, or king as we inaccurately translate, Thiudareiks (Theodoric,"
33111 "German Dietrich), both names flow together."
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "<CHAPTER VII."
33111 ""
33111 "THE GENS AMONG CELTS AND GERMANS.>"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "Space forbids a consideration of the gentile institutions found in a"
33111 "more or less pure form among the savage and barbarian races of the"
33111 "present day; or of the traces of such institutions, discovered in the"
33111 "ancient history of civilized nations in Asia. One or the other are met"
33111 "everywhere. A few illustrations may suffice: Even before the gens had"
33111 "been recognized, it was pointed out and accurately described in its main"
33111 "outlines by the man who took the greatest pains to misunderstand it,"
33111 "McLennan, who wrote of this institution among the Kalmucks, the"
33111 "Circassians, the Samoyeds and three Indian nations: the Warals, the"
33111 "Magars and the Munnipurs. Recently it was described by M. Kovalevsky,"
33111 "who discovered it among the Pshavs, Shevsurs, Svanets and other"
33111 "Caucasian tribes. A few short notes about the existence of the gens"
33111 "among Celts and Germans may find a place here."
33111 ""
33111 "The oldest Celtic laws preserved for us still show the gens in full"
33111 "bloom. In Ireland, it is alive in the popular instinct to this day,"
33111 "after it has been forced out of actual existence by the English. It was"
33111 "in full force in Scotland until the middle of the eighteenth century,"
33111 "and here it also succumbed only to the weapons, laws and courts of the"
33111 "English."
33111 ""
33111 "The old Welsh laws, written several centuries before the English"
33111 "invasion, not later than the 11th century, still show collective"
33111 "agriculture of whole villages, although only exceptionally and as the"
33111 "survival of a former universal custom. Every family had five acres for"
33111 "its special use; another lot was at the same time cultivated"
33111 "collectively and its yield divided among the different families. In view"
33111 "of Irish and Scotch analogies it cannot be doubted that these village"
33111 "communities represent gentes or subdivisions of gentes, even though a"
33111 "repeated investigation of the Welsh laws, which I cannot undertake from"
33111 "lack of time (my notes are from 1869), should not directly corroborate"
33111 "this. One thing, however, is plainly proven by the Welsh and Irish laws,"
33111 "namely that the pairing family had not yet given way to monogamy among"
33111 "the Celts of the 11th century. In Wales, marriage did not become"
33111 "indissoluble by divorce, or rather by notification, until after seven"
33111 "years. Even if no more than three nights were lacking to make up the"
33111 "seven years, a married couple could still separate. Their property was"
33111 "divided among them: the woman made the division, the man selected his"
33111 "share. The furniture was divided according to certain very funny rules."
33111 "If the marriage was dissolved by the man, he had to return the woman's"
33111 "dowry and a few other articles; if the woman wished a separation, then"
33111 "she received less. Of three children the man took two, the woman one,"
33111 "viz., the second child. If the woman married again after her divorce,"
33111 "and her first husband claimed her back, she was obliged to follow him,"
33111 "even if she had one foot in her new husband's bed. But if two had lived"
33111 "together for seven years, they were considered man and wife, even"
33111 "without the preliminaries of a formal marriage. Chasteness of the girls"
33111 "before marriage was by no means strictly observed, nor was it required."
33111 "The regulations regarding this subject are of an extremely frivolous"
33111 "nature and in contradiction with civilized morals. When a woman"
33111 "committed adultery, her husband had a right to beat her--this was one of"
33111 "three cases when he could do so without incurring a penalty--but after"
33111 "that he could not demand any other satisfaction, for \"the same crime"
33111 "shall either be atoned for or avenged, but not both.\" The reasons that"
33111 "entitled a woman to a divorce without curtailing her claims to a fair"
33111 "settlement were of a very diverse nature: bad breath of the man was"
33111 "sufficient. The ransom to be paid to the chief or king for the right of"
33111 "the first night (gobr merch, hence the medieval name marcheta, French"
33111 "marquette) plays a conspicuous part in the code of laws. The women had"
33111 "the right to vote in the public meetings. Add to this that similar"
33111 "conditions are vouched for in Ireland; that marriage on time was also"
33111 "quite the custom there, and that the women were assured of liberal and"
33111 "well defined privileges in case of divorce, even to the point of"
33111 "remuneration for domestic services; that a \"first wife\" existed by the"
33111 "side of others, and that legal and illegal children without distinction"
33111 "received a share of their deceased parent's property--and we have a"
33111 "picture of the pairing family among the Celts. The marriage laws of the"
33111 "American Indians seem strict in comparison to the Celtic, but this is"
33111 "not surprising when we remember that the Celts were still living in"
33111 "group marriage at Cesar's time."
33111 ""
33111 "The Irish gens (Sept; the tribe was called clainne, clan) is confirmed"
33111 "and described not alone by the ancient law codes, but also by the"
33111 "English jurists of the 17th century who were sent across for the purpose"
33111 "of transforming the clan lands into royal dominions. Up to this time,"
33111 "the soil had been the collective property of the gens or the clan,"
33111 "except where the chiefs had already claimed it as their private"
33111 "dominion. When a gentile died, and a household was thus dissolved, the"
33111 "gentile chief (called caput cognationis by the English jurists) made a"
33111 "new assignment of the whole gentile territory to the rest of the"
33111 "household. This division of land probably took place according to such"
33111 "rules as were observed in Germany. Until about fifty years ago, village"
33111 "marks were quite frequent, and some of these so-called rundales may be"
33111 "found to this day. The farmers of a rundale, individual tenants on the"
33111 "soil that once was the collective property of the gens, but had been"
33111 "confiscated by the English conquerors, each pay the rent for his"
33111 "respective parcel. But they all combine their lands and parcel it off"
33111 "according to situation and quality. These parcels, called \"Gewanne\" on"
33111 "the German river Mosel, are cultivated collectively and their yield is"
33111 "divided into shares. Marshland and pastures are used in common. Fifty"
33111 "years ago, new divisions were still made occasionally, sometimes"
33111 "annually. The field map of such a rundale village looks exactly like"
33111 "that of a German \"Gehoeferschaft\" (farming commune) on the Mosel or in"
33111 "the Hochwald. The gens also survives in the \"factions.\" The Irish"
33111 "farmers often form parties that seem to be founded on absolutely"
33111 "contradictory or senseless distinctions, quite incomprehensible to"
33111 "Englishmen. The only purpose of these factions is apparently to rally"
33111 "for the popular sport of hammering the life out of one another. They are"
33111 "artificial reincarnations, modern substitutes for the dispersed gentes"
33111 "that demonstrate the continuation of the old gentile instinct in their"
33111 "own peculiar manner. By the way, in some localities the gentiles are"
33111 "still living together on what is practically their old territory. During"
33111 "the thirties, for instance, the great majority of the inhabitants of the"
33111 "old county of Monaghan had only four family names, i. e., they were"
33111 "descended from four gentes or tribes (clans).[27]"
33111 ""
33111 "The downfall of the gentile order in Scotland dates from the"
33111 "suppression of the revolt in 1745. What link of this order the Scotch"
33111 "clan represented remains to be investigated; that it is a link, is"
33111 "beyond doubt. Walter Scott's novels bring this Scotch highland clan"
33111 "vividly before our eyes. It is, as Morgan says, \"an excellent type of"
33111 "the gens in organization and in spirit, and an extraordinary"
33111 "illustration of the power of the gentile life over its members.... We"
33111 "find in their feuds and blood revenge, in their localization by gentes,"
33111 "in their use of lands in common, in the fidelity of the clansman to his"
33111 "chief and of the members of the clan to each other, the usual and"
33111 "persistent features of gentile society.... Descent was in the male line,"
33111 "the children of the males remaining members of the clan, while the"
33111 "children of its female members belonged to the clans of their respective"
33111 "fathers.\" The fact that matriarchal law was formerly in force in"
33111 "Scotland is proved by the royal family of the Picts, who according to"
33111 "Beda observed female lineage. Even a survival of the Punaluan family had"
33111 "been preserved among the Scots, as among the Welsh. For until the middle"
33111 "ages, the chief of the clan or king, the last representatives of the"
33111 "former common husbands, had the right to claim the first night with"
33111 "every bride, unless a ransom was given."
33111 ""
33111 "It is an indisputable fact, that the Germans were organized in gentes"
33111 "up to the time of the great migrations. The territory between the"
33111 "Danube, the Rhine, the Vistula and the northern seas was evidently"
33111 "occupied by them only a few centuries before Christ. The Cimbri and"
33111 "Teutons were then still in full migration, and the Suebi did not settle"
33111 "down until Cesar's time. Cesar expressly states that they settled down"
33111 "in gentes and kins (gentibus cognatibusque), and in the mouth of a Roman"
33111 "of the gens Julia this term gentibus has a definite meaning, that no"
33111 "amount of disputation can obliterate. This holds good for all Germans."
33111 "It seems that even the provinces taken by them from the Romans were"
33111 "settled by distribution to gentes. The Alemanian code of laws affirms"
33111 "that the people settled in gentes (genealogiae) on the conquered land"
33111 "south of the Danube. Genealogia is used in exactly the same sense as was"
33111 "later on Mark--or Dorfgenossenschaft (mark or village community)."
33111 "Kovalevsky recently maintained that these genealogiae were the great"
33111 "household communities among which the land was divided, and from which"
33111 "the village communities developed later on. The same may be true of the"
33111 "fara, by which term the Burgundians and Langobards--a Gothic and a"
33111 "Herminonian or High German tribe--designated nearly, if not exactly, the"
33111 "same thing as the Alemanian genealogiae. Whether this is really the gens"
33111 "or the household community, must be settled by further investigation."
33111 ""
33111 "The language records leave us in doubt, whether all the Germans had a"
33111 "common expression for gens or not, and as to what this term was."
33111 "Etymologically, the Gothic, kuni, middle High German kuenne, corresponds"
33111 "to the Grecian genos and the Latin gens, and is used in the same sense."
33111 "We are led back to the time of matriarchy by the terms for \"woman\" which"
33111 "are derived from the same root: Greek gyne, Slav zena, Gothic qvino,"
33111 "Norse kona, kuna."
33111 ""
33111 "Among Langobards and Burgundians, I repeat, we find the term fara which"
33111 "Grimm derives from the hypothetical root fisan, to beget. I should"
33111 "prefer to trace it to the more obvious root faran, German fahren, to"
33111 "ride or to wander, in order to designate a certain well defined section"
33111 "of the wandering corps, composed quite naturally of relatives. As a"
33111 "result of centuries of wanderings from West to East and back again, this"
33111 "term was gradually applied to the sex group itself."
33111 ""
33111 "There is furthermore the Gothic sibja, Anglosaxon sib, old High German"
33111 "sippia, sippa, High German sippe. Old Norse has only the plural sifjar,"
33111 "the relatives; the singular occurs only as the name of a goddess, Sif."
33111 ""
33111 "Finally, another expression occurs in the Hildebrand Song, where"
33111 "Hildebrand asks Hadubrand \"who is your father among the men of the"
33111 "nation ... or what is your kin?\" (eddo huellihhes cnuosles du sis)."
33111 ""
33111 "If there was a common German term for gens, it was presumably the Gothic"
33111 "kuni. This is not only indicated by its identity with the corresponding"
33111 "term in related languages, but also by the fact that the word kuning,"
33111 "German Koenig, English king, is derived from it, all of which originally"
33111 "signified chief of gens or tribe. Sibja, German Sippe (relationship),"
33111 "does not appear worthy of consideration. In old Norse, at least, sifjar"
33111 "signifies not alone kin by blood, but also kin through marriage; hence"
33111 "it comprises the members of at least two gentes, and the term sif cannot"
33111 "have been applied to the gens itself."
33111 ""
33111 "In the order of battle, the Germans, like the Mexicans and Greeks,"
33111 "arranged the horsemen as well as the wedge-like columns of the troops on"
33111 "foot by gentes. Tacitus' indefinite expression, \"by families and"
33111 "kinships,\" is explained by the fact that at his time the gens had long"
33111 "ceased to be a living body in Rome."
33111 ""
33111 "Another passage of Tacitus is decisive. There he says: \"The mother's"
33111 "brother regards his nephew as his son; some even hold that the bond of"
33111 "blood between the maternal uncle and the nephew is more sacred and close"
33111 "than that between father and son, so that when persons are demanded as"
33111 "securities, the sister's son is considered a better security than the"
33111 "natural son of the man whom they desire to place under bonds.\" Here we"
33111 "have a living proof of the matriarchal, and hence natural, gens, and it"
33111 "is described as a characteristic mark of the Germans.[28] If a member of"
33111 "such a gens gave his own son as a security for the fulfillment of a vow"
33111 "and this son became the victim of his father's breach of faith, that was"
33111 "the concern of the father alone. But when the son of a sister was"
33111 "sacrificed, then the most sacred gentile law was violated. The next"
33111 "relative who was bound above all others to protect the boy or young man,"
33111 "was held responsible for his death; either he should not have given the"
33111 "boy in bail or he should have kept the contract. If we had no other"
33111 "trace of gentile law among the Germans, this one passage would be"
33111 "sufficient proof of its existence."
33111 ""
33111 "But there is another passage in the Old Norse song of the \"Dawn of the"
33111 "Gods\" and the \"End of the World,\" the Voeluspa, which is still stronger"
33111 "evidence, because it is 800 years younger. In this \"Vision of the"
33111 "Seeress,\" in which Bang and Bugge have now demonstrated the existence of"
33111 "Christian elements, also, the description of the time of general"
33111 "degeneration and corruption inaugurating the great catastrophe contains"
33111 "this passage:"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "     Broedbr munu berjask ok at boenum verdask"
33111 "     Munu systrungar sifjum spilla."
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "\"Brothers will wage war against one another and become each other's"
33111 "murderers, and sisters' children will break the bonds of blood.\""
33111 "Systrungr means the son of the mother's sister, and an abnegation of the"
33111 "blood kinship from that side surpasses in the eyes of the poet even the"
33111 "crime of fratricide. There is a deliberate climax in that systrungar,"
33111 "emphasizing the maternal kinship. If the term syskina-boern, brother's"
33111 "and sister's children, or syskina-synir, brother's and sister's sons,"
33111 "had been used, there would have been a weakening of the effect, instead"
33111 "of a climax. That shows that even at the time of the Vikings, when the"
33111 "Voeluspa was composed, the recollection of maternal law was not yet"
33111 "blotted out."
33111 ""
33111 "Among the Germans with whom Tacitus was familiar maternal law had"
33111 "already given way to paternal lineage. The children were the next heirs"
33111 "of the father; in the absence of children, the brothers and uncles on"
33111 "both sides were next in line. The admission of the mother's brother to"
33111 "the inheritance is a relic of maternal law and proves that paternal law"
33111 "had only recently been introduced by the Germans. Traces of maternal law"
33111 "were preserved until late in the middle ages. It seems that even at this"
33111 "late date people still felt certain misgivings about the reliability of"
33111 "fatherhood, especially among serfs. For when a feudal lord demanded the"
33111 "return of a fugitive serf from a city, it was first required, for"
33111 "instance in Augsburg, Basel and Kaiserslautern, that the fact of his"
33111 "serfdom should be established by the oaths of six of his next blood"
33111 "relations, all of whom had to belong to his mother's kin. (Maurer,"
33111 "Staedteverfassung, I, page 381.)"
33111 ""
33111 "Another relic of declining matriarchy was the (from the Roman"
33111 "standpoint) almost inexplicable respect of the Germans for the female"
33111 "sex. Young girls of noble family were considered the safest bonds to"
33111 "secure the keeping of contracts with Germans. In battle, nothing"
33111 "stimulated their courage so much as the horrible thought that their"
33111 "wives and daughters might be captured and carried into slavery. A woman"
33111 "was to them something holy and prophetical, and they listened to her"
33111 "advice in the most important matters. Veleda, the Bructerian priestess"
33111 "on the river Lippe, was the soul of the insurrection of the Batavians,"
33111 "in which Civilis at the head of German and Belgian tribes shook the"
33111 "foundations of Roman rule in Gaul. The women held undisputed sway in the"
33111 "house. If we may believe Tacitus, they, together with the old men and"
33111 "children, had to do all the work, for the men went hunting, drank and"
33111 "loafed. But as Tacitus does not say who cultivated the fields, and as"
33111 "according to his explicit statement the slaves paid only tithes, but did"
33111 "not work under compulsion, it seems that the adult men would have had to"
33111 "do what little agricultural work was required."
33111 ""
33111 "The form of marriage, as stated above, was the pairing family in gradual"
33111 "transition to monogamy. It was not yet strict monogamy, for polygamy was"
33111 "permitted for the wealthy. Chasteness of the girls was in general"
33111 "carefully maintained, different from the custom of the Celts. Tacitus"
33111 "speaks with special ardor of the sacredness of the matrimonial bond"
33111 "among the Germans. Adultery of the woman is alone quoted by him as a"
33111 "reason for a divorce. But his treatment of this subject leaves many a"
33111 "flaw and besides, it too openly holds up the mirror of virtue to the"
33111 "dissipated Romans. So much is certain: Granted that the Germans were"
33111 "such exceptional models of virtue in their forests, it required only a"
33111 "short contact with the outer world to bring them down to the level of"
33111 "the other average Europeans. In the whirl of Roman life the last trace"
33111 "of pure morals disappeared even faster than the German language. Just"
33111 "read Gregorius of Tours. It is obvious that in the primeval forests of"
33111 "Germany no such hyper-refined voluptuousness could exist as in Rome."
33111 "That implies fully enough superiority of the Germans over the Roman"
33111 "world, and there is no necessity for ascribing to them a moderation and"
33111 "chastity that have never been the qualities of any nation as a whole."
33111 ""
33111 "A result of gentile law is the obligation to inherit the enmities as"
33111 "well as the friendships of one's father and relatives; so is furthermore"
33111 "the displacement of blood revenge by the Wergeld, a fine to be paid in"
33111 "atonement of manslaughter and injuries. A generation ago this Wergeld"
33111 "was considered a specifically German institution, but it has since been"
33111 "found that hundreds of nations introduced this mitigation of gentile"
33111 "blood revenge. Like the obligatory hospitality, it is found, for"
33111 "instance, among the American Indians. Tacitus' description of the manner"
33111 "in which hospitality was observed (Germania, chapt. 21) is almost"
33111 "identical with Morgan's."
33111 ""
33111 "The hot and ceaseless controversy as to whether or not the Germans had"
33111 "already made a definite repartition of the cultivated land at Tacitus'"
33111 "time, and how the passages relating to this question should be"
33111 "interpreted, is now a thing of the past. After the following facts had"
33111 "been established: that the cultivated land of nearly all nations was"
33111 "tilled collectively by the gens and later on by communistic family"
33111 "groups, a practice which Cesar still found among the Suebi; that as a"
33111 "result of this practice the land was re-apportioned periodically; and"
33111 "that this periodical repartition of the cultivated land was preserved in"
33111 "Germany down to our days--after such evidence we need not waste any more"
33111 "breath on the subject. A transition within 150 years from collective"
33111 "cultivation, such as Cesar expressly attributes to the Suebi, to"
33111 "individual cultivation with annual repartition of the soil, such as"
33111 "Tacitus found among the Germans, is surely progress enough for any one."
33111 "The further transition from this stage to complete private ownership of"
33111 "land during such a short period and without any external intervention"
33111 "would involve an absolute impossibility. Hence I can only read in"
33111 "Tacitus what he states in so many words: They change (or re-divide) the"
33111 "cultivated land every year, and enough land is left for common use. It"
33111 "is the stage of agriculture and appropriation of the soil which exactly"
33111 "tallies with the contemporaneous gentile constitution of the Germans."
33111 ""
33111 "I leave the preceding paragraph unchanged, just as it stood in former"
33111 "editions. Meantime the question has assumed another aspect. Since"
33111 "Kovalevsky has demonstrated that the patriarchal household community"
33111 "existed nearly everywhere, perhaps even everywhere, as the connecting"
33111 "link between the matriarchal communistic and the modern isolated family,"
33111 "the question is no longer \"Collective property or private property?\" as"
33111 "discussed between Maurer and Waitz, but \"What was the form of that"
33111 "collective property?\" Not alone is there no doubt whatever, that the"
33111 "Suebi were the collective owners of their land at Cesar's time, but also"
33111 "that they tilled the soil collectively. The questions, whether their"
33111 "economic unit was the gens, or the household, or an intermediate"
33111 "communistic group, or whether all three of these groups existed at the"
33111 "same time as a result of different local conditions, may remain"
33111 "undecided for a long while yet. Kovalevsky maintains that the conditions"
33111 "described by Tacitus were not founded on the mark or village community,"
33111 "but on the household community, which developed much later into the"
33111 "village community by the growth of the population."
33111 ""
33111 "Hence the settlements of the Germans on the territory they occupied at"
33111 "the time of the Romans, and on territory later taken by them from the"
33111 "Romans, would not have consisted of villages, but of large co-operative"
33111 "families comprising several generations, who cultivated a sufficient"
33111 "piece of land and used the surrounding wild land in common with their"
33111 "neighbors. If this was the case, then the passage in Tacitus regarding"
33111 "the changing of the cultivated land would indeed have an agronomic"
33111 "meaning, viz., that the co-operative household cultivated a different"
33111 "piece of land every year, and the land cultivated during the previous"
33111 "year was left untilled or entirely abandoned. The scarcity of the"
33111 "population would have left enough spare wild lands to make all dispute"
33111 "about land unnecessary. Only after the lapse of centuries, when the"
33111 "members of the family had increased so that the collective cultivation"
33111 "became incompatible with the prevailing conditions of production, the"
33111 "household communities were dissolved. The former common fields and"
33111 "meadows were then divided in the well-known manner among the various"
33111 "individual families that had now formed. The division of farm lands was"
33111 "first periodical, but later final, while forest, pasture and"
33111 "watercourses remained common property."
33111 ""
33111 "It seems that this process of development has been fully established for"
33111 "Russia by historical investigation. As for Germany and, in the second"
33111 "place, for other German countries, it cannot be denied that this view"
33111 "affords in many instances a better interpretation of historical"
33111 "authorities and a readier solution of difficulties than the idea of"
33111 "tracing the village community to the time of Tacitus. The oldest"
33111 "documents, e. g. of the Codex Laureshamensis, are easier explained by"
33111 "the help of the household than of the village community. On the other"
33111 "hand, new difficulties now arise and new questions pose themselves. It"
33111 "will require further investigations to arrive at definite conclusions."
33111 "However, I cannot deny that the probability is very much in favor of the"
33111 "intermediate stage of the household community.[29]"
33111 ""
33111 "While the Germans of Cesar's time had either just taken up settled"
33111 "abodes, or were still looking for them, they had been settled for a full"
33111 "century at the time of Tacitus. As a result there is a manifest progress"
33111 "in the production of necessities. The Germans lived in block houses;"
33111 "their clothing was still as primitive as their forests, consisting of"
33111 "rough woolen cloaks, animal skins and linen underclothing for the women"
33111 "and the wealthy. They lived on milk, meat, wild fruit and, as Pliny"
33111 "adds, oatmeal porridge which is the Celtic national dish in Ireland and"
33111 "Scotland to-day. Their wealth consisted in cattle of an inferior race."
33111 "The kine were small, of unattractive appearance and without horns; the"
33111 "horses, little ponies, were not fast runners. Money, Roman coin only,"
33111 "was rarely used. They did not make ornaments of gold and silver, nor did"
33111 "they value these metals. Iron was scarce and, at least among the tribes"
33111 "on the Rhine and the Danube, was apparently only imported, not mined by"
33111 "themselves. The Runen script (imitations of Greek and Latin letters) was"
33111 "only used as a cipher and exclusively for religious sorcery. Human"
33111 "sacrifices were still in vogue. In short, they were a nation just"
33111 "emerged out of the middle stage of barbarism into the upper stage. But"
33111 "while the tribes whose immediate contact with the Romans facilitated the"
33111 "import of Roman products, were thereby prevented from acquiring a metal"
33111 "and textile industry of their own, there is not the least doubt that the"
33111 "tribes of the Northeast, on the Baltic, developed these industries. The"
33111 "pieces of armor found in the bogs of Sleswick--a long iron sword, a coat"
33111 "of mail, a silver helmet, etc., together with Roman coins from the close"
33111 "of the second century--, and the German metal ware spread by the"
33111 "migrations represent a peculiar type of a superior finish, even such as"
33111 "were modeled after Roman originals. With the exception of England, the"
33111 "emigration into the civilized Roman empire everywhere put an end to this"
33111 "home industry. How simultaneously this industry arose and developed, is"
33111 "shown e. g. by the bronze spangles. The specimens found in Burgundy, in"
33111 "Roumania and on the Sea of Asow, might have been manufactured in the"
33111 "same shop with those found in England or Sweden and are of undoubted"
33111 "German origin."
33111 ""
33111 "The German constitution was also in keeping with the upper stage of"
33111 "barbarism. According to Tacitus, the council of chiefs (principes)"
33111 "universally decided matters of minor importance and prepared important"
33111 "matters for the decision of the public meetings. So far as we know"
33111 "anything of the public meeting in the lower stage of barbarism, viz.,"
33111 "among the American Indians, it was only held by gentes, not by tribes or"
33111 "leagues of tribes. The chiefs of peace (principes) were still sharply"
33111 "distinguished from the chiefs of war (duces), just as among the"
33111 "Iroquois. The peace chiefs were already living in part on honorary"
33111 "donations of the gentiles, such as cattle, grain, etc. They were"
33111 "generally elected from the same family, analogous to America. The"
33111 "transition to paternal law favored, as in Greece and Rome, the gradual"
33111 "transformation of office by election into hereditary office. A \"noble\""
33111 "family was thus gradually raised in each gens. Most of this hereditary"
33111 "nobility came to grief during the migrations or shortly after. The"
33111 "military leaders were elected solely on their merits. They had little"
33111 "power and were obliged to rely on the force of their example. The actual"
33111 "disciplinary power in the army was held by the priests, as Tacitus"
33111 "implicitly states. The public meeting was the real executive. The king"
33111 "or chief of the tribe presided. The people decided. A murmur signified"
33111 "\"No,\" acclamation and clanging of weapons meant \"Yes.\" The public"
33111 "meeting was at the same time a court of justice. Complaints were here"
33111 "brought forth and decided, and death sentences pronounced. Only"
33111 "cowardice, treason and unnatural lust were capital crimes. The gentes"
33111 "and other subdivisions decided in a body under the chairmanship of the"
33111 "chief, who in all original German courts was only the manager of the"
33111 "transactions and questioner. Among Germans, the sentence has ever and"
33111 "everywhere been pronounced by the community."
33111 ""
33111 "Leagues of tribes came into existence since Cesar's time. Some of them"
33111 "already had kings. The first chief of war began to covet the usurper's"
33111 "place, as among Greeks and Romans, and sometimes succeeded in obtaining"
33111 "it. Such successful usurpers were by no means absolute rulers. But still"
33111 "they began to break through the bonds of the gens. While freed slaves"
33111 "generally occupied an inferior position, because they could not be"
33111 "members of any gens, they often gained rank, wealth and honors as"
33111 "favorites of the new kings. The same thing took place after the conquest"
33111 "of the Roman empire by those military leaders who had now become kings"
33111 "of great countries. Among the Frankons, slaves and freed slaves of the"
33111 "king played a leading role first at the court, then in the state. A"
33111 "large part of the new nobility were descended from them."
33111 ""
33111 "There was one institution that especially favored the rise of royalty:"
33111 "the military following. We have already seen, how among the American"
33111 "redskins private war groups were formed independently of the gens. Among"
33111 "the Germans, these private groups had developed into standing bodies."
33111 "The military leader who had acquired fame, gathered around his person a"
33111 "host of booty loving young warriors. They were pledged to personal"
33111 "faithfulness by their leader who in return pledged himself to them. He"
33111 "fed them, gave them presents and organized them on hierarchic"
33111 "principles: a body guard and a troop for immediate emergencies and short"
33111 "expeditions, a trained corps of officers for larger enterprises. These"
33111 "followings must have been rather insignificant, in fact we find them so"
33111 "later under Odoaker in Italy, still they portended the decay of the old"
33111 "gentile liberty, and the events during and after the migrations proved"
33111 "that military retainers were heralds of evil. For in the first place,"
33111 "they fostered the growth of royalty. In the second place, Tacitus"
33111 "affirms that they could only be held together by continual warfare and"
33111 "plundering expeditions. Robbery became their life purpose. If the leader"
33111 "found nothing to do in his neighborhood, he marched his troops to other"
33111 "countries, where a prospect of war and booty allured him. The German"
33111 "auxiliaries, many of whom fought under the Roman standard even against"
33111 "Germans, had been largely recruited among such followings. They"
33111 "represent the first germs of the \"Landsknecht\" profession, the shame and"
33111 "curse of the Germans. After the conquest of the Roman empire, these"
33111 "retainers of kings together with the unfree Roman courtiers formed the"
33111 "other half of the nobility of later days."
33111 ""
33111 "In general, then, the German tribes combined into nations had the same"
33111 "constitution that had developed among the Greeks of the heroic era and"
33111 "the Romans at the time of the so-called kings: public meetings, councils"
33111 "of gentile chiefs and military leaders who coveted actual royal power."
33111 "It was the highest constitution which the gentile order could produce;"
33111 "it was the standard constitution of the higher stage of barbarism. If"
33111 "society passed the limits for which this constitution sufficed, then the"
33111 "end of the gentile order had come. It collapsed and the state took its"
33111 "place."
33111 ""
33111 "FOOTNOTES:"
33111 ""
33111 "[27] Author's note to the fourth edition."
33111 ""
33111 "During a few days passed in Ireland, I once more became conscious to"
33111 "what extent the rural population is still living in the conceptions of"
33111 "the gentile period. The great landholder, whose tenant the farmer is,"
33111 "still enjoys a position similar to that of a clan chief, who has to"
33111 "supervise the cultivation of the soil in the interest of all, who is"
33111 "entitled to a tribute from the farmer in the form of rent, but who also"
33111 "has to assist the farmer in cases of need. Likewise everyone in"
33111 "comfortable circumstances is considered under obligation to help his"
33111 "poorer neighbors whenever they are in need. Such assistance is not"
33111 "charity, it is simply the prerogative of the poor gentile, which the"
33111 "rich gentile or the chief of the clan must respect. This explains why"
33111 "the professors of political economy and the jurists complain of the"
33111 "impossibility of imparting the idea of the modern private property to"
33111 "the Irish farmers. Property that has only rights, but no duties, is"
33111 "absolutely beyond the ken of the Irishman. No wonder that so many"
33111 "Irishmen who are suddenly cast into one of the modern great cities of"
33111 "England and America, among a population with entirely different moral"
33111 "and legal standards, despair of all morals and justice, lose all hold"
33111 "and become an easy prey to demoralization."
33111 ""
33111 "[28] Author's note."
33111 ""
33111 "The Greeks know this special sacredness of the bond between the mother's"
33111 "brother and his nephew, a relic of maternal law found among many"
33111 "nations, only in the mythology of heroic times. According to Diodorus"
33111 "IV., 34, Meleagros kills the sons of Thestius, the brother of his mother"
33111 "Althaia. The latter regards this deed as such a heinous crime that she"
33111 "curses the murderer, her own son, and prays for his death. \"It is said"
33111 "that the gods fulfilled her wish and ended the life of Meleagros.\""
33111 "According to the same Diordorus, IV., 44, the Argonauts under Herakles"
33111 "land in Thracia and there find that Phineus, at the instigation of his"
33111 "second wife, shamefully maltreats his two sons, the offspring of his"
33111 "first deserted wife, the Boread Kleopatra. But among the Argonauts there"
33111 "are also some Boreads, the brothers of Kleopatra, the uncles of the"
33111 "maltreated boys. They at once champion their nephews, set them free and"
33111 "kill their guards."
33111 ""
33111 "[29] Translator's note."
33111 ""
33111 "The household community is still a distinct stage of production in"
33111 "Georgia (South Russia). The northern boundary of Georgia is the"
33111 "Caucasus. The Georgians, a people of high intelligence, have for"
33111 "centuries maintained their independence against Persians, Arabs, Turcs"
33111 "and Tartars. Dr. Philipp Gogitshayshvili gives the following interesting"
33111 "description of their condition in an article, entitled \"Das Gewerbe in"
33111 "Georgien\" (Zeitschrift fuer die gesammte Staatswissenschaft,"
33111 "Ergaenzungsheft I., Tuebingen, 1901). \"The Swanians (a district of Georgia"
33111 "is called Swania) have all the necessities of life. They weave their own"
33111 "clothing, make their own weapons, powder and even silver, and gold"
33111 "ornaments. There is no modern trading.... They are acquainted with"
33111 "exchange, but only of products for products. Money does not circulate"
33111 "and there are neither shops nor markets.... There is not a single"
33111 "beggar, not a single man who asks for charity. With the exception of"
33111 "iron, salt and chintz, the Swanians produce all they need themselves."
33111 "They prepare their linen from hemp, their clothing from skins of wild"
33111 "animals and wool, their footwear from hides and leather. They make"
33111 "feltcaps, household goods, weapons, saddles, bridles and agricultural"
33111 "implements.\""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "<CHAPTER VIII."
33111 ""
33111 "THE RISE OF THE STATE AMONG GERMANS.>"
33111 ""
33111 ""
33111 "According to Tacitus the German nation was very strong in numbers. An"
33111 "approximate idea of the strength of individual German nations is given"
33111 "by Caesar. He states that the number of Usipetans and Tencterans who"
33111 "crossed over to the left bank of the Rhine amounted to 180,000,"
33111 "including women and children. About 100,000[30] members to a single"
33111 "nation is considerably more than e. g. the Iroquois numbered in their"
33111 "prime, when 20,000 of them became the terror of the whole country, from"
33111 "the Great Lakes to the Ohio and Potomac. If we attempt to place the"
33111 "better known nations of the Rhine country by the help of historical"
33111 "reports, we find that a single nation occupies on the map the average"
33111 "area of a Prussian government district, about 10,000 square"
33111 "kilometers[31] or 182 German geographical square miles.[32] The Germania"
33111 "Magna of the Romans, reaching to the Vistula, comprised about 500,000"
33111 "square kilometers. Counting an average of 100,000 for any single"
33111 "nation, the total population of Germania Magna would have amounted to"
33111 "five millions. This is a rather high figure for a barbarian group of"
33111 "nations, although 10 inhabitants to the square kilometer or 550 to the"
33111 "geographical square mile is very little when compared to present"
33111 "conditions. But this does not include the whole number of Germans then"
33111 "living. We know that German nations of the Gothic race, Bastarnians,"
33111 "Peukinians and others, lived all along the Carpathian mountains away"
33111 "down to the mouth of the Danube. They were so numerous that Pliny"
33111 "designated them as the fifth main division of the Germans. As much as"
33111 "180 years B. C. they were mercenaries of the Macedonian King Perseus,"
33111 "and during the first years of Augustus they were still pushing their way"
33111 "as far as the vicinity of Adrianople. Assuming them to have been one"
33111 "million strong we find that at least six millions was the probable"
33111 "population of Germany at the beginning of the Christian era."
33111 ""
33111 "After the final settlement in Germany, the population must have grown"
33111 "with increasing rapidity. The industrial progress mentioned above would"
33111 "be sufficient to prove it. The objects found in the bogs of Sleswick, to"
33111 "judge by the Roman coins found with them, are from the third century."
33111 "Hence at that time the metal and textile industry was already well"
33111 "developed on the Baltic, a lively traffic with the Roman empire was"
33111 "carried on, and the wealthier class enjoyed a certain luxury--all of"
33111 "which indicates that the population had increased. But at the same time"
33111 "the general war of aggression against the Romans commenced along the"
33111 "whole line of the Rhine, of the Roman wall and of the Danube, a line"
33111 "stretching from the North Sea to the Black Sea. This is another proof of"
33111 "the ever growing outward pressure of the population. During the struggle"
33111 "which lasted three centuries, the whole main body of the Gothic"
33111 "nations, with the exception of the Scandinavian Goths and the"
33111 "Burgundians, marched to the Southeast and formed the left wing of the"
33111 "long line of attack. The High Germans (Herminonians) on the Upper Danube"
33111 "fought in the center, and the Iskaevonians on the Rhine, now called"
33111 "Franks, advanced on the right wing. The conquest of Brittany fell to the"
33111 "lot of the Ingaevonians.[33] At the end of the fifth century, the"
33111 "exhausted, bloodless, and helpless Roman empire lay open to the Germans."
33111 ""
33111 "In former chapters we stood at the cradle of antique Greek and Roman"
33111 "civilization. Now we are standing at its grave. The equalizing plane of"
33111 "Roman world power had been gliding for centuries over all the"
33111 "Mediterranean countries. Where the Greek language did not offer any"
33111 "resistance, all national idioms had been crushed by a corrupted Latin."
33111 "There were no longer any distinctions of nationality, no more Gauls,"
33111 "Iberians, Ligurians, Noricans; they had all become Romans. Roman"
33111 "administration and Roman law had everywhere dissolved the old gentile"
33111 "bodies and thus crushed the last remnant of local and national"
33111 "independence. The new type of Romans offered no compensation for this"
33111 "loss, for it did not express any nationality, but only the lack of a"
33111 "nationality. The elements for the formation of new nations were present"
33111 "everywhere. The Latin dialects of the different provinces differentiated"
33111 "more and more. But the natural boundaries that had once made Italy,"
33111 "Gaul, Spain, Africa independent territories, were still present and made"
33111 "themselves felt. Yet there was no strength anywhere for combining these"
33111 "elements into new nations. Nowhere was there the least trace of any"
33111 "capacity for development, nor any power of resistance, much less any"
33111 "creative power. The immense human throng of that enormous territory was"
33111 "held together by one bond alone: the Roman state. But this state had in"
33111 "time become the worst enemy and oppressor of its subjects. The provinces"
33111 "had ruined Rome. It had become a provincial town like all others,"
33111 "privileged, but no longer ruling, no longer the center of the world"
33111 "empire, no longer even the seat of the emperors and subregents who lived"
33111 "in Constantinople, Treves and Milan. The Roman state had become an"
33111 "immense complicated machine, designed exclusively for the exploitation"
33111 "of its subjects. Taxes, state imposts and tithes of all sorts drove the"
33111 "mass of the people deeper and deeper into poverty. By the blackmailing"
33111 "practices of the regents, tax collectors and soldiers, the pressure was"
33111 "increased to such a point that it became insupportable. This was the"
33111 "outcome of Rome's world power. The right of the state to existence was"
33111 "founded on the preservation of order in the interior and the protection"
33111 "against the barbarians outside. But this order was worse than the most"
33111 "disgusting disorder, and the barbarians against whom the state pretended"
33111 "to protect its citizens, were hailed by them as saviors."
33111 ""
33111 "The condition of society was no less desperate. During the last years of"
33111 "the republic, the Roman rulers had already contrived the pitiless"
33111 "exploitation of the conquered provinces. The emperors had not abolished,"
33111 "but organized this exploitation. The more the empire fell to pieces, the"
33111 "higher rose the taxes and tithes, and the more shamelessly did the"
33111 "officials rob and blackmail. Commerce and industry had never been a"
33111 "strong point of the domineering Romans. Only in usury they had excelled"
33111 "all other nations before and after them. What commerce had managed to"
33111 "exist, had been ruined by official extortion. Only in the East, in the"
33111 "Grecian part of the empire, some commerce still vegetated, but this is"
33111 "outside of the scope of our study. Universal reduction to poverty,"
33111 "decrease of traffic, of handicrafts, of art, of population, decay of the"
33111 "towns, return of agriculture to a lower stage--that had been the final"
33111 "result of Roman world supremacy."
33111 ""
33111 "But now agriculture, the most prominent branch of production in the"
33111 "whole Old World, was again supreme, and more than ever. In Italy, the"
33111 "immense estates (latifundiae) that comprised nearly the whole country"
33111 "since the end of the republic, had been utilized in two ways: either as"
33111 "pastures on which the population had been replaced by sheep and oxen,"
33111 "the care of which required only a few slaves; or as country seats, on"
33111 "which masses of slaves carried on horticulture on a large scale, partly"
33111 "for the luxury of the owner, partly for sale on the markets of the"
33111 "towns. The great pastures had been preserved and even extended in"
33111 "certain parts. But the country seats and their horticulture had gone to"
33111 "ruin through the impoverishment of their owners and the decay of the"
33111 "towns. Latifundian economy based on slave labor was no longer"
33111 "profitable; but in its time it had been the only possible form of"
33111 "agriculture on a large scale. Now, however, small production had again"
33111 "become the only lucrative form. One country seat after the other was"
33111 "parceled and leased in small lots to hereditary tenants who paid a fixed"
33111 "rent, or to partiarii, more administrators than tenants who received"
33111 "one-sixth or even only one-ninth of a year's product in remuneration for"
33111 "their work. But these little lots were principally disposed of to"
33111 "colonists who paid a fixed sum annually and could be transferred by sale"
33111 "together with their lots. Although no slaves, still these colonists"
